Reason Needs Revelation for Preponderance
The author argues that reason alone cannot judge preponderance without a separate transmitted proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unaided reason cannot decide between equally possible alternatives and why transmitted proof is necessary for such judgments.

Core Idea
Reason sees equality; only revelation can tip the scale.

In this passage, the author confronts a key question: can the human intellect determine which of two equally possible outcomes must occur? He argues that when reason perceives both sides as equal, it cannot prefer one without external evidence. This introduces the crucial role of transmitted proof (al-dalīl al-sam‘ī) in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow, then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: العقل, الرجحان, الدليل السمعي.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: premise of equality, impossibility of combining equality and preponderance, need for a separate proof.
3
Consider the counter-example
Notice the author's observation that both atheists and monotheists share certain rational certainties, which seems to contradict the dependence on revelation.
The final sentence is difficult; it suggests some rational knowledge is independent.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Ask yourself: why is transmitted proof necessary in this context? How does this argument support the role of revelation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محال . فالعقل وحده لا يمكنه في هذا الموضع أن يجزم بأن أحد الطرفين ، لا محال : واقع . لأن حكم العقل ههنا : استواء الطرفين . والحكم بوقوع أحد الطرفين لا محال : جزم بحصول الرجحان ، والجمع بين الاستواء وبين الرجحان : محال . فيثبت : أن العقل لما تقرر عنده هذا الاستواء : امتنع أن يحكم بالرجحان إلا بدليل منفصل ، وهو الدليل السمعي . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون هذا العلم بحصول ذلك الرجحان مستنداً إلى الدليل السمعي . فوجب أن يكون الجاهل بذلك الدليل السمعي : جاهلا بذلك الرجحان . ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك ، لأن الملحد والموحد كلهم قاطعون ، بامتناع حصول القصر ، إلا عن المادة السابقة
Impossible. So the intellect alone cannot in this situation be certain that one of the two sides is necessarily occurring. Because the judgment of the intellect here is the equality of the two sides. And the judgment that one of the two sides is necessarily occurring is a decisive affirmation of preponderance (rujḥān), and combining equality with preponderance is impossible. Thus it is established that when the intellect has determined this equality, it is impossible for it to judge preponderance except by a separate proof, and that is the transmitted proof (al-dalīl al-sam‘ī). On this basis, this knowledge of the occurrence of that preponderance is dependent upon the transmitted proof. So it must be that the one ignorant of that transmitted proof is ignorant of that preponderance. But it is known that this is not the case, because both the atheist and the monotheist are all certain of the impossibility of the occurrence of the [qasr?] except from the prior matter.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about the limitations of reason. It begins by stating that it is impossible for reason alone to judge that one of two equally possible alternatives must occur. The reason is that when the intellect sees both sides as equal, any judgment about one side having preponderance (rujḥān) would require a factor that tips the balance. Since reason perceives only equality, it cannot determine which side preponderates unless there is an external proof. That proof is identified as transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-sam‘ī) from revelation. Consequently, knowledge of the preponderance must come from revelation. If that were true, then whoever is ignorant of revelation would be ignorant of the preponderance. However, the author points out that this conclusion does not hold, because both atheists and monotheists are certain about the impossibility of certain things (e.g., a thing occurring without prior matter). This suggests that some rational certainties do not require revelation. The final sentence is ambiguous but seems to assert that both parties agree on the impossibility of some occurrence except from a prior material cause, implying that reason can indeed judge in some cases.

Key Terms
العقل
الرجحان
الدليل السمعي
استواء الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author claims that reason is completely incapable of any knowledge without revelation.
Correction: The author only argues that reason cannot determine preponderance when both sides are equal; reason retains other capacities, as shown by shared certainties between atheists and believers.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can reason alone not judge that one of two sides necessarily occurs?
A) Because reason always errs.
B) Because reason perceives equality of the two sides.
C) Because revelation is always required.
D) Because atheists and monotheists disagree.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot decide between equally possible alternatives; revelation provides the needed preponderance, but reason still has independent certainties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision between two seemingly equal options—whether in belief or daily life—do you honestly seek guidance from revelation, or do you rely solely on your own reason? What would change if you truly submitted to the transmitted proof?
?
This passage shows that without revelation, reason cannot tip the scale. Is there an area of your faith where you have been relying on your own intellectual comfort rather than the clear texts? What would it cost you to surrender that comfort and accept the revealed preponderance?
?
The author notes that both atheist and monotheist share rational certainties, yet you claim to know the truth about God. Is your certainty anchored in the transmitted proof, or have you mixed it with your own inclinations? How will you test your faith against the revelation today?

The True Criterion for Self-Evident Truths
The author refutes the claim that mutakallimun's opinions determine what is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct criterion for distinguishing self-evident truths from theoretical matters, and why the opinions of mutakallimūn in the heat of debate are not authoritative.

Core Idea
Sound rational nature, not polemical habit, is the true judge of self-evident truths.

In this passage, the author tackles a methodological objection that appeals to the authority of mutakallimūn in identifying self-evident premises. He argues that their judgments are unreliable due to their practice of accepting absurdities in debate. Instead, he proposes a criterion based on the innate human disposition (fiṭra) that has not been corrupted by sophistic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the objection and the author's outright rejection of the authority of mutakallimūn.
Pay attention to the strong language: 'لا عبرة' (no weight).
2
Examine the Mu'tazila example
See how the author illustrates the unreliability of mutakallimūn through the case of the Mu'tazila abandoning a self-evident premise under debate pressure.
This example shows that even acknowledged self-evident truths can be denied in polemical contexts.
3
Identify the positive criterion
Find the sentence where the author states the true criterion: rational people who remain on the original fiṭra, untainted by debate habits.
The phrase 'العقلاء الذين بقوا على الفطرة الأصلية' is key.
4
Analyze the two examples
Read the examples of the sudden old man and the rivers. Notice how the author argues that all sound rational people would judge them impossible, proving the premises are self-evident.
These examples are meant to be universally compelling.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that even mutakallimūn themselves, when not debating, would agree. This reinforces that the criterion is sound innate reason, not polemical opinion.
The author aims to show that the objection is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله سابعاً : ( إن المتكلمين حكموا بافتقار البناء إلى الباني ، وحكموا بإستغناء البناء عن المادة والمدة ) فنقول : إنه لا عبرة في تمييز البديهيات عن النظريات بقول المتكلمين . وذلك لأنهم عند الإلزامات القوية ، قد اعتادوا التزام المحالات ، وارتكاب المنكرات . ألا ترى أن الجبرية لما ألزموا على المعتزلة : أن القادر لما كان قادراً على الضدين، امتنع رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، وحينئذ يلزم الجبر فالمعتزلة : خوفاً من هذا الإلزام : جوزوا رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا المرجح . مع أن العقلاء المحققين اتفقوا على أن هذه المقدمة بديهية . بل نقول : العبرة في تمييز البديهيات عن النظريات : بالعقلاء الذين بقوا على الفطرة الأصلية ، والسلامة الخلقية ، وما مارسوا المحالات ، ولم يألفوا التزام المنكرات والمحالات ، في مواقف المناظرات . ومعلوم أن جميع عقلاء الدنيا إذا كانوا بالصفة المذكورة ، ثم عرضت عليهم : أنه هل يجوز حدوث إنسان شيخ دفعة واحدة، من غير أن كان مسبوقاً بالأبوين ، ومن غير أن كان مسبوقاً بالطفولية والشباب ؟ فإنهم يقطعون بامتناعه . وإذا عرضت عليهم قول من يقول : إن هذه الأنهار جرت في هذه المفازة من غير أن يقال : إنها سالت إليها من مواضع أخر وإنما جرت في هذه المفازة على سبيل أن هذه المياه حدثت في هذه المواضع ابتداء . فإن العقلاء بأسرهم يقطعون يكون هذا القول كذباً باطلاً ، أو بأن هذا القائل صار مجنوناً . وهذا يدل على أن هذه المقدمة من أقوى البديهيات . وأما أن المتكلمين يجوزونه ، فليس الأمر كذلك ، لأن المتكلم لو سألته عن هذه الوقائع ، لا في وقت المناظرة ، بل في وقت سلامة عقله ، لأقر بذلك ، ولقضى على من ينكروه بالجنون والعته . فثبت : أن الأمر كما ذكرناه .
As for his seventh statement: 'The mutakallimūn ruled that the building requires a builder, and ruled that the building is independent of matter and time.' We say: There is no weight in distinguishing self-evident matters (badīhiyyāt) from theoretical matters (naẓariyyāt) by the opinions of the mutakallimūn. Because when faced with strong logical constraints, they have become accustomed to affirming impossibilities and committing objectionable things. Do you not see that when the Jabriyya argued against the Mu‘tazila: 'Since the capable agent is capable of two opposites, the preponderance of one side over the other without a preponderator is impossible, and then coercion (jabr) follows,' the Mu‘tazila, fearing this constraint, allowed the preponderance of one possible side over the other without a preponderator, even though the sound rationals have agreed that this premise is self-evident. Rather we say: The criterion for distinguishing self-evident matters from theoretical matters is the rational people who remain on the original innate disposition (fiṭra), sound natural constitution, who have not practiced impossibilities, and have not become accustomed to affirming objectionable things and impossibilities in positions of debate. It is known that all rational people of the world, if they are described as above, and then are asked: 'Is it possible for a human to come into existence as an old man all at once, without being preceded by parents, and without being preceded by childhood and youth?' They would certainly judge it impossible. And if presented with someone saying: 'These rivers flowed in this desert without it being said that they flowed to it from other places, but rather they flowed in this desert because these waters originated in these places from the beginning,' then all rational people would judge that statement to be false and invalid, or that its speaker has become insane. This indicates that this premise is among the strongest self-evident truths. As for the fact that the mutakallimūn might allow it, that is not the case. Because if you asked the mutakallim about these cases, not at the time of debate, but at the time of his sound reason, he would acknowledge that, and would judge one who denies it as insane and imbecilic. Thus it is established that the matter is as we have stated.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of a seventh objection which apparently claims that the mutakallimūn's judgments about what is self-evident should be authoritative. The author rejects this outright. He argues that the mutakallimūn, due to their habit of accepting absurdities in the heat of debate, cannot be trusted to distinguish self-evident truths from theoretical ones. He gives the example of the Jabriyya-Mu'tazila debate where the Mu'tazila, to avoid an undesirable implication, abandoned a self-evident premise. The author then lays down the correct criterion: the judgment of rational people who remain on the original fiṭra (sound innate disposition) and have not been corrupted by the practices of debate. He provides two examples: (1) a human appearing suddenly as an old man without parents or childhood, and (2) rivers appearing in a desert without prior source. All sound rational people would immediately reject these as impossible, proving that these are self-evident truths. Even the mutakallimūn themselves, when not engaged in debate, would agree. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
بديهيات
نظريات
فطرة
المتكلمون
الرجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is dismissing all theological reasoning as invalid.
Correction: He is only rejecting the authority of mutakallimūn in defining self-evident truths when their reasoning is compromised by polemics, not rejecting theology itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct criterion for distinguishing self-evident truths?
A) The consensus of mutakallimūn.
B) The judgment of rational people on innate disposition.
C) The ruling of religious texts.
D) The majority opinion.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident truths are recognized by sound innate reason (fiṭra), not by the conditioned reasoning of mutakallimūn in debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a heated argument about religion, have you ever defended a position that, in your quiet moments alone, you know does not sit right with your innate sense of truth? What would it take for you to abandon that defense and return to your fiṭra?
?
This lesson shows that even the mutakallimun themselves, when not in debate, would judge as insane those who deny self-evident truths like the impossibility of a human appearing suddenly as an old man. Is there any belief you hold only because of your group's stance, but which your own uncorrupted mind would reject? Will you examine that belief today?
?
The author says the criterion is not the opinion of theologians but 'rational people who remain on the original innate disposition.' Are you willing to let your own innate disposition judge matters, even if it contradicts what you have learned from your teachers or community? What is one area where you need to do that right now?

The Second Proof: Needs of Originated vs Persistent
The author presents the second proof comparing the needs of the originated and the persistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for istishab, comparing the factors required for the originated and the persistent to show the latter's preponderance.

Core Idea
The originated needs three factors; the persistent needs two—so the persistent is stronger.

The author continues his arguments for istishab by comparing the originated and the persistent. He shows that the originated requires more conditions for its existence than the persistent, making the latter more likely to remain. However, he also anticipates an objection about infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the three factors for the originated and the two for the persistent.
2
Identify the factors
List the three factors for the originated (coming-to-be of itself, time, occurrence in time) and the two for the persistent (time, occurrence in time). Compare them.
The persistent does not need the coming-to-be of its essence because it already exists.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if origination depends on three things, each of those is also originated and would depend on three more, leading to infinite regress. Consider why this poses a challenge.
The passage ends without a response, but the proof is not automatically invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الحادث يفتقر حدوثه إلى أمور ثلاثة : إلى حدوث ذلك الشيء ، وإلى ذلك الزمان ، وإلى حدوث حصوله في ذلك الزمان وأما الباقي : فإنه لا يفتقر إلى حدوث ذاته ، وإنما يفتقر إلى حدوث أمرين : إلى حدوث ذلك الزمان ، وإلى حدوث حصوله في ذلك الزمان . والمفتقر إلى أمور ثلاثة : مرجوح بالنسبة إلى ما يكون مكتفياً بأحد ذينك الأمرين ولقائل أن يقول : لو كان الحدوث مفتقراً إلى هذه الأمور الثلاثة ، لافتقر كل واحد منها إلى ثلاثمة أخرى . کلام
The second: that the originated, its origination depends on three things: on the coming-to-be of that thing, on that time, and on the coming-to-be of its occurrence in that time. As for the persistent, it does not depend on the coming-to-be of its essence, but rather depends on the coming-to-be of two things: on the coming-to-be of that time, and on the coming-to-be of its occurrence in that time. And that which depends on three things is outweighed relative to that which is content with two of those things. And an objector might say: If origination depended on these three things, each one of them would depend on yet another three terms. Speech.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the principle of istishab. It compares the needs of the originated (al-hadith) and the persistent (al-baqi). The originated requires three factors for its coming-to-be: (1) the coming-to-be of its own essence, (2) the coming-to-be of the time, and (3) the coming-to-be of its occurrence in that time. The persistent, however, does not require the coming-to-be of its essence—it already exists—but only requires two factors: (1) the coming-to-be of the time, and (2) the coming-to-be of its occurrence in that time. Since the originated depends on three things, it is outweighed (marjuh) relative to that which is content with two. Therefore, the persistent is preponderant. The objection raises a potential infinite regress: if origination depends on three things, each of those things are originated and would themselves depend on three things, ad infinitum. The text ends with 'كلام' (speech/argument), possibly introducing a response, but it is not given here.

Key Terms
الحادث
الباقي
الحدوث
المفتقر
المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection disproves the second proof entirely.
Correction: The objection suggests an infinite regress, but the proof is not negated because the comparison is about the status of the original object, not the factors. The principle of istishab remains intact.

Checkpoint
How many factors does the originated depend on according to the second proof?
A) Two
B) Three
C) One
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that the persistent is stronger than the originated because it depends on fewer factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that what persists needs fewer conditions than what begins. In your own life, are you more focused on starting new things or on maintaining what you already have? Which does this proof suggest is stronger, and how should that change your priorities?
?
The objector raised an infinite regress to challenge the proof. When you hear a truth that challenges your comfort, do you instinctively raise objections? Ask yourself: are your objections sincere or are they a shield?
?
You depend on time and occurrence for every action you perform. How does realizing your constant dependence humble you before the One who is not subject to time? What will you change about your daily attitude because of this?

Three Proofs for the Principle of Permanence
Three proofs that the original state is continuance, applied to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three independent proofs establishing that the default assumption for any matter is its continuance, and that change requires a separate proof, especially as applied to bodies.

Core Idea
The default is permanence; change needs a proof.

In this passage, the author presents three compelling arguments from everyday experience and reason to show that our natural assumption is that things continue as they are. These proofs establish a foundational principle in theology and philosophy—the principle of permanence—which will then be applied to the question of the original state of physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation to get the flow of the three proofs.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage contains three distinct arguments: the understanding argument, the traveler argument, and the letter-writing argument. Note each one's structure.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the proofs, the author concludes the general principle and then applies it to bodies. Observe the logical leap.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is this principle important? How does it shift the burden of proof?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام الثالث : لو كان ظن التغير معلولاً لظن البقاء ، لما فهم أحد شيئاً من أحد . لأنه لما كان احتمال بقاء تلك الألفاظ ، دالة على الموضوعات السابقة ، معادلة لزوال تلك الدلالة ، لم يسارع الفهم إلى جانب البقاء ، بـل بقي متردداً وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يحصل الفهم. وحيث حصل ، علمنا أن ذلك ، إنما كان لأجل أن اعتقاد البقاء على ما كان : راجح على اعتقاد الزوال عما كان • الرابع : أن من خرج عن بلد ، وكان قد شاهد بعض جوانب تلك البلدة معموراً ، والجانب الآخر منه خراباً . فإنه بعد خروجه عن ذلك البلد ، وغيبته عنه بمدة مديدة ، فإنه يبقى ظنه في الجانب الذي كان معموراً أنه بقي معموراً وفي الجانب الذي رآه خراباً أنه بقي خراباً ولا يرجع عن هذا الحكم إلا لدليل منفصل . وذلك يدل على أن البديهة حاكمة : بأن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان • الخامس : أنه إذا خرج من بلد ، فإنه يكتب إلى أقاربه ، وأصدقائه وما ذاك إلا لأن ظن البقاء ، راجح على ظن الزوال . فيثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن الأصل في كل أمر ، بقاؤه على ما كان . ويثبت : أن التغير لا يجوز إثباته إلا بدليل منفصل · إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الأصل في هذه الأجسام ، الموصوفة بهذه التركيبات المخصوصة ، والصفات المخصوصة، أنها كانت معدومة ثم صارت موجودة هكذا، فإن قام دليل قاهر من الخارج على أنها كانت معدومة ثم صارت موجودة ، قضينا بذلك . وإلا وجب القضاء بأنها كانت على هذه الحالة والصفة ، أبداً من غير تغير فالحاصل أن القول بالدوام متأيد ومتأكد بحكم الأصل . ولا حاجة فيه إلى دليل منفصل . وإنما المحتاج إلى دليل المنفصل هو القول بالتغير والحدوث
The third discourse: If the presumption of change were an effect of the presumption of permanence, then no one would understand anything from anyone. Because when the probability of the continuity of those expressions indicating previous subjects is equal to the probability of the disappearance of that indication, understanding does not rush to the side of continuity, but remains hesitant, and then it follows that no understanding occurs. But when understanding does occur, we know that it is only because the belief that things continue as they were is more preponderant than the belief that they have changed from what they were. The fourth: That whoever leaves a town and had seen some of its quarters inhabited and the other quarter ruined, then after leaving that town and being absent from it for a long time, he continues to assume that the quarter that was inhabited remains inhabited, and that the quarter he saw ruined remains ruined, and he does not turn away from this judgment except with an external proof. That indicates that innate reason judges that the original state of every matter is its continuance as it was. The fifth: That if he leaves a town, he writes to his relatives and friends, and that is only because the presumption of permanence is more preponderant than the presumption of change. Thus by these proofs it is established that the original state of every matter is its continuance as it was, and it is established that change may not be affirmed except by an external proof. If this is established, we say: The original state of these bodies, described with these specific compositions and specific attributes, is that they were nonexistent and then became existent in this manner. If a compelling external proof arises that they were nonexistent and then became existent, we judge accordingly. Otherwise, it is necessary to judge that they were in this state and attribute forever without change. The conclusion is that the affirmation of perpetuity is supported and strengthened by the rule of the original state. There is no need for an external proof for it. Only the affirmation of change and origination needs an external proof.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents three independent proofs (the third, fourth, and fifth) that the default assumption (al-asl) for any matter is that it remains as it is (baqa'). The third proof argues from the fact that understanding occurs despite equal probabilities: if understanding were dependent on the presumption of change, it would never occur; but it does occur, so the presumption of permanence must be stronger. The fourth proof uses the example of a traveler who left a town: he naturally assumes the inhabited part remains inhabited and the ruined part remains ruined, showing that innate reason (badiha) judges permanence as the default. The fifth proof notes that when someone leaves a town, he writes to his relatives—an action based on the presumption that his relatives still exist, which is stronger than the presumption that they have changed. From these, the author concludes that the original state is permanence and that change requires a separate proof. He then applies this principle to physical bodies: the default is that bodies continue in their state unless a proof shows they were originated (hadith). The final conclusion is that the claim of perpetuity (dawam) is supported by the principle of the original state and needs no proof, while the claim of change and origination requires external proof.

Key Terms
ظن التغير
ظن البقاء
الأصل
البقاء
التغير
البديهة
دليل منفصل

Universal Rational Repugnance and Necessity
Second station: universal repugnance as proof of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal repugnance of the intellect serves as evidence that certain events are necessary.

Core Idea
Universal repugnance points to necessity, not mere impossibility.

This passage presents the second stage of a larger argument. The author first reminds us that innate rationality instinctively rejects certain scenarios—like a human born without parents. Then he argues that the universal agreement of rational minds on this repugnance is evidence that such events must be necessary, not merely contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the repetition of 'We have shown' indicating continuity from an earlier discussion.
2
Identify the two examples of repugnance
The author gives two examples: a human born without parents, and a day not preceded by night or day. Note how these are intuitively impossible scenarios.
These examples are meant to be universally repugnant to the rational mind.
3
Understand the inference to necessity
The author argues that universal consensus on this repugnance indicates the events are necessary (wajibat) in themselves.
The key logical conditional: if not necessary, certainty about occurrence or non-occurrence would be impossible.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider how the passage builds on previously established points, hinting at a larger systematic theology.
The author ends by saying 'no benefit in repetition,' showing this is a continuation.

This passage presents the second station (al-maqam al-thani) of an argument. The author argues that if all rational people (jami' al-'uqala') agree on this repulsion, it indicates that the occurrence of these states in these specific ways must be necessary (wajibat). The reasoning is that if these events were not necessary in themselves, then it would be impossible to achieve certainty (j)0!زم) in designating one side for occurrence and the other for non-occurrence. The author notes that this point has been established previously and thus does not need repetition.

The Default of Permanence and Rational Consensus
Razi establishes permanence as default and proves it by rational consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the default judgment in theology is permanence unless there is a clear proof of change, and how rational consensus serves as a proof for this principle.

Core Idea
Permanence is the default; change requires proof.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a foundational methodological principle: when examining specific compositions and attributes, we assume they have always existed as they are until a decisive external proof shows they originated. He then defends this principle by appealing to rational intuition and universal consensus of rational people. This lesson unpacks both the rule and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Razi divides his discussion into two parts: first stating the rule, then providing evidence for it.
The passage is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Identify the default rule
Find the sentence that states the default judgment (الأصل). Under what condition does he allow for change?
He says: 'إذا قام دليل...وإلا وجب القضاء بأنها كانت على هذه الحالة'.
3
Understand the proof from rational consensus
Razi says that rational intuition (بديهة العقل) deems certain events impossible (e.g., human without parents). The consensus of all rational people on this shows that these events must be necessary. Try to trace the logic: why does consensus indicate necessity?
He links consensus to certainty about which alternative occurs.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this principle is important for theology. How does it relate to arguments about the eternity of the world or divine attributes?
This is a key move in kalam debates about change and permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التركيبات المخصوصة ، والصفات المخصوصة، أنها كانت [ معدومة ثم صارت (1) ] موجودة هكذا، فإن قام دليل قاهر من الخارج على أنها كانت معدومة ثم صارت موجودة ، قضينا بذلك . وإلا وجب القضاء بأنها كانت على هذه الحالة والصفة ، أبداً من غير تغير. فالحاصل أن القول [ بالدوام (3) ] متأيد ومتأكد بحكم الأصل . ولا حاجة فيه إلى دليل منفصل . وإنما المحتاج إلى دليل المنفصل هو القول بالتغير والحدوث.
المقام الثاني في تقرير هذه الحجة : أن نقول : قد بينا أن بديهة العقل تستبعد حدوث الإنسان ، لا عن الوالدين، وتستبعد حدوث نهار لا يسبقه ليل ، ولا نهار وبينا : أن إطباق كل العقلاء على هذا الاستبعاد ، يدل على أن وقوع هذه الأحوال على هذه الوجوه المخصوصة ، لا بد وأن يكون من الواجبات لان لو لم تكن في أنفسها من الواجبات ، لامتنع أن يحصل الجزم بتعيين أحد الطرفين للوقوع ، وتعيين الطرف الثاني لعدم الوقوع . وذلك مما سبق تقريره فلا فائدة في الإعادة . والله أعلم
The specific compositions and specific attributes, that they were non-existent then became existent in this manner: if a conclusive proof from outside establishes that they were non-existent then became existent, we judge accordingly. Otherwise, it is necessary to judge that they were in this state and attribute, perpetually without change. So the conclusion is that the statement of permanence is supported and confirmed by the principle. There is no need for a separate proof for it. Rather, the one in need of a separate proof is the statement of change and origination.
The second station in establishing this proof: We say: We have shown that the intuitive intellect deems unlikely the origination of a human without parents, and deems unlikely the occurrence of a day not preceded by night or day. And we have shown that the consensus of all rational people upon this deeming unlikely indicates that the occurrence of these states in these specific ways must be necessary. For if they were not necessary in themselves, it would be impossible to have certainty in specifying one of the two alternatives for occurrence and specifying the second alternative for non-occurrence. This has already been established, so there is no benefit in repetition. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments. First, it establishes a methodological principle: when considering specific compositions and attributes, if there is a decisive external proof that they were non-existent and then came into existence, we accept that. Otherwise, the default judgment is that they have always been in that state without change. The author asserts that the position of permanence (al-dawām) is strengthened by the principle (al-aṣl), meaning the default assumption is continuity. Change and origination (al-taġayyur wa-l-ḥudūth) are what require a separate proof.

Second, the author moves to what he calls the second station, which is to establish a proof for this principle itself. He argues that rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql) deems it impossible for a human to arise without parents, or for a day to occur without being preceded by night or day—meaning without a temporal sequence. The universal consensus of rational people on this impossibility indicates that such specific occurrences must be necessary in themselves. If they were not necessary, there would be no basis for certainty in specifying which alternative occurs and which does not. Since that argument has already been presented earlier, the author concludes that repeating it is unnecessary, and ends with 'God knows best'.

حدوث
أصل
استبعاد
وجوب
بديهة العقل
إطباق العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author argues for absolute eternalism without allowing for any change, or that he claims permanence is proven by consensus alone.
Correction: Razi explicitly allows for change when a strong external proof exists. The default of permanence is a methodological starting point, not a dogmatic claim about everything being eternal. The consensus argument is about specific necessary connections, not about all things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default judgment (حكم الأصل) regarding specific compositions and attributes when no external proof of change exists?
A) They are necessarily originated (حادث).
B) They are in a state of permanence (دوام) without change.
C) Their status is unknown and requires investigation.
D) They should be considered non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The default theological presumption is permanence, and any claim of change must be proven by external evidence; rational intuition and consensus confirm this principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You believe in Allah's attributes as eternal. But do you live as though they are permanent in your heart, or do you let doubts shake your certainty? What one attribute do you need to reaffirm as changeless today?
?
The rational consensus says certain things are necessary. Yet your actions sometimes deny that necessity—like choosing sin over obedience. What is the one change in your behavior that you have accepted without proof, and what would happen if you subjected it to Razi's standard?

The First Argument for the Eternity of Duration
Enumerates three possibilities, refutes two, and concludes the eternity of duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities regarding the relationship between the Creator and the world, why two are impossible, and how the remaining possibility establishes the eternity of duration.

Core Idea
The Creator's priority to the world must be infinite, proving duration is eternal.

This lesson presents a foundational argument from Islamic theology regarding the concept of time and duration. The author, using rational analysis, lays out three possible relationships between the Creator and the world. By eliminating the first two through logical reasoning, he arrives at a conclusion that affirms the eternity of duration (al-midda al-qadima). This argument is crucial for understanding how theologians reconcile the Creator's pre-existence with the world's origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument. Note the three possibilities introduced at the beginning.
The argument is presented twice, first in a general form and then applied to today's events.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The author lists three logical possibilities: (1) the Creator and the world existed together, (2) the world is posterior by a finite duration, or (3) by an infinite duration.
3
Understand why the first two are false
The first possibility (together) would imply both are eternal or both originated—impossible. The second (finite delay) would make the Creator originated—also impossible.
Focus on the logical refutations given.
4
Grasp the conclusion and its implication
The third possibility (infinite delay) remains. The duration of priority must be eternal, thus establishing the eternity of duration.
The alternative formulation reinforces the same conclusion without reference to the world's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجوه المستنبطة في هذا الباب من الزمان. فالحجة الأولى : أن يقال : البارىء والعالم إما أن يقال : إنها وجدا معاً ، أو يقال : العالم متأخر عن البارى بمدة غير متناهية ، أو متأخر عنه بمدة متناهية . والقسم الأول والثاني باطل فتعين الثالث. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل ، إذ لو كان البارى والعالم معاً ، لزم أن يكونا معاً قديمين أو يكونا معاً محدثين. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الثاني باطل ، لأن المتقدم على المحدث بمدة متناهية يكون محدثاً فيلزم حدوث البارى. فبقي الثالث وهو أن يقال : البارى متقدم على العالم بمدة غير متناهية ، فهذا يوجب القول بأن تلك المدة التي بسببها تقدم البارى على العالم تقدماً لا نهاية له تكون قديمة ، فهذا يوجب قدم المدة. ويمكن ذكر هذا الكلام في تقرير قدم المدة بحيث لا يحتاج فيه إلى ذكر قدم العالم. فيقال : لا شك أن البارى متقدم على الحوادث الحادثة في هذا اليوم ، فإما أن يكون تقدمه عليها بمدة متناهية أو بمدة غير متناهية . والأول يوجب حدوث البارى ، والثاني يوجب قدم المدة.
The arguments derived in this chapter regarding time. The first argument: It is said: the Creator and the world: either they are said to have existed together, or the world is posterior to the Creator by an infinite duration, or posterior to Him by a finite duration. The first and second divisions are false, so the third is determined. We say that the first division is false because if the Creator and the world were together, it would necessitate that they are both eternal or both originated. We say that the second division is false because that which is prior to an originated thing by a finite duration would be originated, thus necessitating the origination of the Creator. So the third remains, which is to say: the Creator is prior to the world by an infinite duration. This necessitates the statement that that duration, due to which the Creator is prior to the world with a priority that has no end, would be eternal, and this necessitates the eternity of duration. And this statement can be mentioned in establishing the eternity of duration such that it does not need to mention the eternity of the world. It is said: There is no doubt that the Creator is prior to the events occurring today. Either His priority to them is by a finite duration or by an infinite duration. The first necessitates the origination of the Creator, and the second necessitates the eternity of duration.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument regarding the eternity of duration (qidam al-midda). It is a classification of possibilities followed by a refutation of the false ones. The author begins by stating that there are three possibilities concerning the Creator (al-Bari') and the world (al-'alam): (1) they existed together, (2) the world is posterior to the Creator by a finite duration, or (3) the world is posterior by an infinite duration. The first possibility is false because if they were together, either both would be eternal (qadimayn) or both would be originated (muhdathayn). Both alternatives are impossible: the Creator is eternal, the world is originated, so coexistence is ruled out. The second possibility is false because if the Creator were prior to the world by a finite duration, the Creator Himself would be originated (since that which precedes an originated thing by a finite time must itself be originated), leading to the absurdity of the Creator being originated. Therefore, the third possibility remains: the Creator is prior to the world by an infinite duration. This entails that the duration itself is eternal, thus proving the eternity of duration. The author then offers an alternative formulation of the same argument without referencing the world's eternity: focusing on the Creator's priority to today's events, the same two options of finite or infinite duration lead to the same conclusion—finite duration implies the Creator's origination, infinite implies the eternity of duration. Thus the argument establishes the concept of an eternal duration (al-midda al-qadima) that precedes the world.

Key Terms
البارىء (al-Bari')
العالم (al-'alam)
قدم المدة (qidam al-midda)
مدة متناهية (mudda mutanahiya)
مدة غير متناهية (mudda ghayr mutanahiya)
حادث (muhdath)
قديم (qadim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'infinite duration' means the world is eternal.
Correction: The argument only proves that the duration of the Creator's priority is eternal, not the world itself. The world is still originated (muhdath).

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is the second possibility (the world is posterior to the Creator by a finite duration) false?
A) It would make both the Creator and the world eternal.
B) It would make the Creator originated (muhdath).
C) It would make the world eternal.
D) It would imply that the duration is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The only rational possibility is that the Creator is prior to the world by an infinite duration, which proves that duration itself is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Creator is prior to the world by an infinite duration, what does that mean for your personal sense of urgency in turning to Him? Are you living as if today is just another finite moment in an infinite timeline, or are you aware that your finite existence is contained within an eternal duration that God has ordained?
?
The argument shows that a finite duration between Creator and world would imply the Creator's origination. How does that challenge any subtle assumptions you might have that God is somehow bound by time or that He 'began' at some point? Does your heart truly believe God is beyond time, or do you unintentionally imagine Him as a being within time?
?
If the infinite duration before the world is eternal, then God's priority is absolute. How does this affect your understanding of His sovereignty over your life? Do you live with the awareness that every moment of your existence is preceded by an infinite duration of God's existence, and that He has always been there, even before you were born?

The Dilemma of Temporal Precedence
Introduction to six proofs against temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's precedence over creation cannot be temporal, based on the initial dilemma and the six proofs introduced.

Core Idea
Allah's precedence is not by time; duration is finite or infinite, both impossible for God.

This lesson introduces a foundational refutation in Islamic theology: the claim that Allah's priority over the world is temporal. The author presents a logical dilemma regarding the supposed duration of precedence, showing it must be either finite or infinite, both impossible for the Eternal. He then announces six proofs that will be elaborated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the author's concise style and the logical structure.
The passage is very compact; each phrase carries weight.
2
Understand the Dilemma
The author states that the supposed duration of Allah's precedence is either finite or infinite. Both are impossible: finite implies a beginning for God's existence, infinite implies an unending temporal regression. Realize that both contradict divine eternity.
3
Prepare for the Proofs
The author concludes that it is false to say Allah is prior by a duration and announces six proofs. This lesson only covers the introduction; subsequent lessons will detail the proofs.
Focus on the logical structure here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك : إن تلك المدة، إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية . وذلك باطل فإنه لا يجوز أن يقال : البارى تعالى متقدم على العالم بالمدة . والدليل على بطلان هذا القول وجوه :
That: Indeed that duration is either finite or infinite. And that is false, for it is not permissible to say: The Creator, exalted, is prior to the world by a duration. And the proof of the falsity of this statement are aspects:
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage introduces a refutation of the claim that Allah's precedence over the world is temporal. The author first presents the dilemma: the supposed duration (muddah) of precedence must be either finite or infinite. Both are impossible for the Eternal. Then he states that it is impermissible to say the Creator is prior to the world by a duration. The 'proof of falsity' consists of several aspects (wujuh), which he will enumerate. This sets up the following six proofs.

Key Terms
المدة
التقدم
الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah existed before the world in time, just as one thing precedes another in time.
Correction: Allah's precedence is not temporal; time itself is created. The supposed duration of precedence is either finite or infinite, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the initial dilemma presented by the author regarding the duration of Allah's precedence?
A) It must be either eternal or created.
B) It must be either finite or infinite.
C) It must be either spatial or temporal.
D) It must be either necessary or contingent.

Key Takeaway
Allah's precedence is not measured by any duration, because duration is finite or infinite, both contradictions with divine eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

First Proof: Precedence Without Time
Analogical argument against temporal precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the analogy of yesterday's precedence over today to argue that Allah's precedence over creation is not temporal.

Core Idea
Precedence does not require time—even for yesterday over today.

This passage presents the first proof against temporal precedence. Sanusi begins with a simple analogy: the precedence of yesterday over today is not measured by time or duration, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. If such non-temporal precedence is conceivable, then by greater reason the Creator's precedence over the world can be understood without time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the compact argumentative style.
The passage is a single short proof, so read carefully.
2
Analyze the conditional argument
Identify the 'if-then' structure: if precedence required time, then infinite regress. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The key is the phrase 'وإلا لزم' (otherwise it would necessitate).
3
Apply the analogy to divine precedence
If yesterday's precedence can be non-temporal, then certainly the Creator's precedence can be. This is an argument from the lesser to the greater.
Sanusi uses the question 'فلم لا يعقل' (why is it not understood?) to drive the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن تقدم الأمس على اليوم ليس بالزمان والمدة . وإلا لزم وقوع المدة في مدة أخرى ، إلى ما لا نهاية له . وإذا عقل أن يكون تقدم الأمس على اليوم لا بالمدة فلم لا يعقل أن يكون تقدم وجود البارىء على وجوه العالم ، لا بالمدة ؟
The first: Indeed the precedence of yesterday over today is not by time and duration. Otherwise it would necessitate the occurrence of a duration within another duration, ad infinitum. And if it is understood that the precedence of yesterday over today is not by duration, then why is it not understood that the precedence of the existence of the Creator over the existence of the world is not by duration?
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against temporal precedence. The author begins with an analogy: the precedence of yesterday over today is not measured by time or duration, because if it were, we would need a duration for that precedence, leading to an infinite regress. Since we can conceive of such precedence without invoking time, by analogy we can conceive of Allah's precedence without time. The logic is that if a lower order of precedence can be non-temporal, then the divine precedence certainly can be.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All precedence necessarily involves time or duration.
Correction: This passage shows that conceptual precedence, like yesterday over today, can be understood without invoking time. Thus, divine precedence can also be non-temporal.

Key Takeaway
This first proof establishes that precedence can be understood without time, using a simple analogy to reject the necessity of temporal duration in divine precedence.

Second Proof: The Contingency of Time
Second proof: the sum of time is contingent and requires a free agent prior to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time is composed of contingent instants, and therefore the Creator must be prior to time in a non-temporal way.

Core Idea
Every moment of time points to its Creator, who is beyond time.

This second proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah builds on the contingent nature of time itself. By analysing the flow of instants, the author demonstrates that time cannot be self-sufficient. Let us follow the logical chain that leads from passing moments to the necessity of a free, creative Agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure of the proof.
2
Identify the definition of time
The passage begins by defining duration (al-mudda) as a series of flowing instants. Understand that each instant is separate and passes away.
3
Trace the contingency argument
Each instant was non-existent then existed – that is the mark of contingency. Every contingent needs a cause (muhdith). Therefore the entire sum of time has a cause.
This cause is described as a free agent (fa'il mukhtar).
4
Understand the conclusion about the Creator
The free agent must be prior to his act. So the Creator is prior to time, and that priority is not itself temporal – otherwise time would have to exist before it existed, a contradiction.
This establishes a non-temporal precedence of the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن المدة عبارة عن آنات منقضية سيالة ، فكل واحد من تلك الآنات ، قد كان معدوماً ، ثم صار موجوداً . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ممكن وكل ممكن فله محدث. فلمجموع الزمان محدث ، هو فاعل مختار . والفاعل المختار لا بد وأن يكون متقدماً على مفعوله ، فيكون البارىء متقدماً في الوجود على وجود المدة والزمان. وتقدمه على المدة يمتنع أن يكون بالمدة ، وإلا لزم كون المدة : موجودة ، حال كونها معدومة . وذلك محال
The second: Indeed duration is composed of passing, flowing instants. Each of those instants was non-existent, then became existent. And everything that is so is contingent. And every contingent has an originator (muhdith). So the sum of time has an originator, who is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). And the free agent must be prior to his act. So the Creator is prior in existence to the existence of duration and time. And His precedence over duration cannot be by duration, otherwise it would necessitate duration being existent while it is non-existent. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The author defines time as a series of flowing, successive instants. Each instant was once non-existent and then came into existence, making it contingent. Every contingent requires a cause (muhdith). Therefore, the entire sum of time has a cause, which is a free agent. A free agent must be prior to his effect. Thus, the Creator exists before time and duration. His precedence cannot itself be temporal because that would mean time existed before it existed, a contradiction.

Key Terms
المدة
الآنات
الممكن
المحدث
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is eternal and does not require a creator.
Correction: The reasoning shows that every moment is contingent, so the whole of time is contingent and needs a Creator who is outside time.

Checkpoint

A) The Creator is within time.
B) The Creator is prior to time and not by time.
C) Time is eternal.
D) The Creator is simultaneous with time.

Key Takeaway
Time is contingent and points to a timeless Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Time and Eternity Are Incompatible
Third proof: time's essence requires change and being preceded, incompatible with eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time and divine eternity are logically incompatible.

Core Idea
Time requires change and precedence; eternity excludes precedence — they cannot coexist.

In this third proof, the author examines the very nature of time and shows that it necessarily involves change and being preceded by a prior state. Since divine eternity (al-azal) means having no beginning and not being preceded by anything, time and eternity are fundamentally opposed. This passage demonstrates the logical impossibility of applying temporal concepts to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how each statement builds on the previous.
The passage is concise; each clause is a step in the argument.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms for essence (mahiyya), change (taghayyur), being preceded (masbuqiyya), and eternity (azal). Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are repeated and carry the argument.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: time involves change → change implies being preceded → so time implies being preceded → eternity denies being preceded → therefore time and eternity cannot be combined.
Each 'therefore' is stated explicitly in the text.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the author says 'combining them is impossible'. How does this proof support the doctrine that Allah is not subject to time?
This conclusion is a key point in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن ماهية المدة متعلقة بالتغير من حال إلى حال . والتغير ماهيته تقتضي المسبوقية بالحال المتنقل عنه فماهية المدة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير ، وماهية الأزل تنافي المسبوقية بالغير ، والجمع بينهما محال
The third: Indeed the essence of duration is connected to change from state to state. And change, its essence necessitates being preceded by the state from which it transitions. So the essence of duration necessitates being preceded by another, and the essence of eternity (azal) negates being preceded by another, and combining them is impossible.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author analyzes the essence of time. Time is defined as the measure of change from one state to another. The very concept of change implies that the changing thing is preceded by the state it leaves. Therefore, time itself, as a concept, necessarily depends on something before it. In contrast, eternity (al-azal) by definition is without beginning and not preceded by anything. Thus, if Allah is eternal, His precedence cannot be measured by time, because time would introduce a prior state, contradicting eternity. The two are incompatible.

Key Terms
الماهية
التغير
المسبوقية
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time and eternity can both describe Allah's existence.
Correction: The proof shows that time requires change and being preceded, which directly contradicts the definition of eternity (no beginning, no prior state). Therefore, they cannot be combined.

Checkpoint

B) That it is finite.
C) That it is preceded by something else.
D) That it is simultaneous with God.

Key Takeaway
Time and divine eternity are mutually exclusive because time entails change and precedence, while eternity entails absolute beginninglessness without any predecessor.

The Present Moment and Non-Temporal Precedence
Sanusi presents a proof that contingency implies possible perishment, leading to the idea of non-temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the present moment demonstrates that precedence and posteriority can exist without time, proving that Allah's precedence is non-temporal.

Core Idea
Time can cease, but non-temporal precedence remains conceivable.

Sanusi now presents a fourth proof demonstrating that Allah's precedence is not temporal. He focuses on the present moment—a contingent entity that was not before and will cease to be. By imagining its perishment without a successor, we see that time itself can end. Yet in that scenario, we still speak of 'after' time, proving that precedence can be non-temporal. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author builds the argument from the nature of the present moment.
The passage is concise; track each logical step.
2
Identify the key premise
The present moment is contingent: it was not before it occurs, and it will cease after. Anything contingent can perish and be followed by a similar thing.
Contingency is the foundation of the proof.
3
Follow the supposition
Imagine the present moment perishes with no successor. What happens? Duration becomes discontinuous and ends. Time itself would cease.
This thought experiment is crucial.
4
Extract the conclusion
In that hypothetical, the non-existence of time is 'after' its existence, but that 'after' cannot be temporal (since time is gone). Thus we conceive non-temporal posteriority, proving that precedence and subsequence can be non-temporal.
This directly supports that Allah's precedence is not in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن الآن الحاضر ما كان موجوداً قبل حضوره ، وسيعدم بعد دخوله في الوجود . وكل ما كان كذلك، فإنه ممكن لذاته ، وكل ممكن لذاته ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يفنى ويوجد مثله عقيبه . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن بتقدير أن يفنى هذا الآن الحاضر ، ولا يوجد بعده أن آخر ، فإنه تكون المدة منقطعة وفانية. ومع هذا التقدير ، فإنه يكون عدم الزمان متأخراً عن وجوده ، لا بالزمان، وإلا لزم عدمه عند وجوده . وذلك محال . فقد عقلنا حصول التقدم والتأخر ، لا بسبب الزمان والمدة .
The fourth: Indeed the present moment was not existent before its occurrence, and will cease to exist after its entry into existence. And everything that is so, it is contingent in itself. And everything contingent in itself, it is not impossible that it perishes and its like comes into existence after it. If this is established, we say: Suppose that this present moment perishes, and no other instant exists after it, then duration would be discontinuous and perishing. And with this supposition, the non-existence of time would be posterior to its existence, not by time, otherwise its non-existence would occur at its existence, and that is impossible. So we have conceived the occurrence of precedence and posteriority, not by means of time and duration.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof. The author begins by noting that the present moment is contingent—it was not before and will cease. As a contingent, it is possible for it to perish without a replacement. If that happens, time would be cut off and cease. In that scenario, the non-existence of time is 'after' its existence, but that 'after' cannot be temporal because time no longer exists. Thus we conceive of posteriority without time. This demonstrates that precedence and subsequence can be non-temporal, so Allah's precedence need not be temporal.

الممكن

التقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Precedence and subsequence always require time.
Correction: This proof shows that we can conceive of one thing being after another even when time does not exist, demonstrating non-temporal precedence.

Checkpoint

B) Precedence and subsequence can exist without time.
C) The present moment is necessary.
D) Time cannot perish.

Key Takeaway
The present moment's perishment shows that precedence and posteriority can be understood without time, so Allah's precedence is non-temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fifth Proof: Time's Nature and Non-Temporal Attributes
Fifth proof: whether time is permanent or changing, its attributes can be understood without time, avoiding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that shows how permanence and priority can be conceived without time, avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Time's own properties reveal that time itself is not needed to conceive permanence or priority.

This proof presents a dilemma: whether time is permanent or changing, its own attributes (permanence or sequence) are understood without invoking another time. Thus, we can conceive of non-temporal attributes, which supports the possibility of Allah's eternal qualities without temporal dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the clear structure of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities: time is either permanent (eternal) or originated (changing). Find where each is introduced.
3
Examine the reasoning for each alternative
For each alternative, see how the author avoids infinite regress: permanence of time is not due to another time; priority of parts is not due to another time.
The key is that each alternative's own attribute is understood without additional time.
4
Understand the conclusion
In both cases, we grasp permanence or priority without time. Therefore, these concepts can be non-temporal, relevant for attributes of Allah.
This proof undermines the necessity of time for conceiving such attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس : إن المدة والزمان. إما أن تكون في نفسه وذاته من الأمور الدائمة الباقية ، أو تكون من الأمور الحادثة المتبدلة. فإن كان الأول ، فنقول : هذه المدة أمر دائم . ودوامها ليس بسبب مدة أخرى . وإلا لزم التسلسل . فقد عقلناه دواماً ، لا بسبب المدة، وإذا عقل ذلك ، فلم لا يعقل الدوام والاستمرار في سائر الأشياء من غير حاجة إلى فرض زمان ومدة ؟ وإن كان الثاني ، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان ، سابقاً على غيره ، أو متأخراً عنه . وذلك التقدم والتأخر ، ليس بسبب المدة وإلا لزم التسلسل وإذا عقل ذلك ، فلم لا يعقل حصول التقدم والتأخر في سائر الأشياء ، لأجل المدة والزمان ؟
And the fifth: Indeed duration and time are either in themselves and their essence among permanent enduring things, or among originated changing things. If the first, we say: This duration is permanent. And its permanence is not due to another duration, otherwise regress would follow. So we have conceived it as permanence, not due to duration. And if that is conceived, then why is not permanence and continuity conceived in other things without needing to suppose time and duration? If the second, then each of the parts of time is prior to another or posterior to it. And that precedence and posteriority are not due to duration, otherwise regress would follow. And if that is conceived, then why is not the occurrence of precedence and posteriority conceived in other things, without time and duration?
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which is a dilemma. The author considers two possibilities: time is either eternal (permanent) or originated (changing). If time is permanent, its permanence cannot be due to another time (infinite regress). So we conceive of permanence without invoking time. By analogy, other things can also have non-temporal permanence. If time is originated and changing, then the sequence of its parts (priority and posteriority) is not due to another time (again to avoid regress). Thus we conceive of non-temporal priority. So in either case, we can conceive of attributes like permanence and priority without time, making it possible for Allah's attributes to be non-temporal.

Key Terms
المدة
الزمان
الدوام
التسلسل
السابق
المتأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is necessary for conceiving permanence or priority.
Correction: This proof shows that both permanence and priority can be conceived without invoking another time, as demonstrated by the properties of time itself.

Checkpoint

A) Time must be eternal.
B) Infinite regress is avoided only by positing time.
C) Attributes like permanence and priority can be conceived without time.
D) Time is necessary for any conception of priority.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that permanence and priority are intelligible without time, undermining the notion that time is a necessary condition for these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If time is not necessary for permanence, then your soul's permanence in the afterlife is not dependent on time. How does that affect your urgency for repentance and good deeds? Are you living as if eternity has already begun?

Sixth Proof: Time Entails Change, Allah Is Beyond Time
Sixth proof: time entails change, so God's precedence is not temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof that God's precedence is not temporal because time necessarily involves change, which is incompatible with divine immutability.

Core Idea
God is beyond time because time equals change, and God does not change.

The sixth and final proof in this series examines the concept of time itself. The author argues that time (duration) is only conceivable where there is a flow from future to present to past—a process of constant change. Since Allah is absolutely free from change, His existence cannot be bounded by time, and His priority over the world is not a temporal priority. This passage also brings together all six proofs into a final conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth proof
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author defines duration and then draws implications.
The proof is short but densely logical.
2
Identify the logical sequence
Break down the argument: Duration requires past/present/future → Future becomes present, present becomes past → This is change → Allah is free from change → So His precedence is not by time.
Each step follows necessarily.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The final sentence states that the six proofs together establish that Allah's precedence need not be temporal. Reflect on how this proof fits with the previous five.
The author ends with 'Allah knows best' as a humble reminder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن المدة لا يعقل حصولها ، إلا حيث حصل فيه الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . فالذي يكون مستقبلاً ، يصير بعده حالاً ، ثم يصير الحال ماضياً . وهذا لا يحصل إلا حيث حصل فيه التغير والتبدل . والبارى تعالى منزه عن التبدل والتغير ، فامتنع أن يكون تقدمه على العالم بالمدة والزمان . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة : أنه لا يجب أن يكون تقدم الباري على العالم بالمدة والزمان . والله أعلم
The sixth: Indeed duration is not conceived except where past, present, and future occur. So what is future becomes afterward present, then the present becomes past. And this only occurs where change and alteration occur. And the Creator, exalted, is free from alteration and change. So it is impossible that His precedence over the world be by duration and time. So it is established by these six aspects that it is not necessary that the precedence of the Creator over the world be by duration and time. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof and the overall conclusion. The author argues that time is only conceivable in a context where there is past, present, and future, implying constant change. Since Allah is beyond change, He cannot be subject to time, nor can His precedence be temporal. Therefore, the necessity of temporal precedence is refuted. The conclusion reaffirms that the six proofs collectively demonstrate that Allah's precedence need not be by time. The phrase 'Wa Allahu a'lam' is a typical seal, acknowledging that true knowledge rests with God.

The Fifth Argument: Priority Without Time

By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that God's precedence is not temporal, based on the nature of time as either permanent or originated.

Core Idea
Priority can exist without time; we can conceive permanence or priority without duration.

The fifth argument examines whether time itself is permanent or originated. In either case, the argument shows that priority and permanence can be conceived without invoking another time, breaking the assumption that all priority is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن بتقدير أن يفنى هذا الآن الحاضر ، ولا يوجد بعده أن آخر ، فإنه تكون المدة منقطعة وفانية. ومع هذا التقدير ، فإنه يكون عدم الزمان متأخراً عن وجوده ، لا بالزمان، وإلا لزم عدمه عند وجوده . وذلك محال . فقد عقلنا حصول التقدم والتأخر ، لا بسبب الزمان والمدة . والخامس : إن المدة والزمان. إما أن تكون في نفسه وذاته من الأمور الدائمة الباقية ، أو تكون من الأمور الحادثة المتبدلة. فإن كان الأول ، فنقول : هذه المدة أمر دائم . ودوامها ليس بسبب مدة أخرى . وإلا لزم التسلسل . فقد عقلناه دواماً ، لا بسبب المدة، وإذا عقل ذلك ، فلم لا يعقل الدوام والاستمرار في سائر الأشياء من غير حاجة إلى فرض زمان ومدة ؟ وإن كان الثاني ، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان ، سابقاً على غيره ، أو متأخراً عنه . وذلك التقدم والتأخر ، ليس بسبب المدة وإلا لزم التسلسل وإذا عقل ذلك ، فلم لا يعقل حصول التقدم والتأخر في سائر الأشياء ، لأجل المدة والزمان ؟ السادس : إن المدة لا يعقل حصولها ، إلا حيث حصل فيه الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . فالذي يكون مستقبلاً ، يصير بعده حالاً ، ثم يصير الحال ماضياً . وهذا لا يحصل إلا حيث حصل فيه التغير والتبدل . والبارى تعالى منزه عن التبدل والتغير ، فامتنع أن يدخل تحت الزمان ، فامتنع أن يكون تقدمه على العالم بالمدة والزمان . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة : أنه لا يجب أن يكون تقدم الباري على العالم بالمدة والزمان . والله أعلم
If this is established, we say: If we suppose that this present 'now' perishes and no other 'now' exists after it, then duration would be discontinuous and perishable. With this supposition, the non-existence of time would be posterior to its existence, not by time—otherwise its non-existence would be simultaneous with its existence, and that is impossible. Thus we have conceived the occurrence of priority and posteriority without time and duration. Fifth: Duration and time are either in themselves and their essence among the permanent, enduring things, or among the originated, changing things. If the first, we say: This duration is a permanent thing. Its permanence is not due to another duration—otherwise there would be infinite regress. We have conceived it as permanent without duration. If that is conceivable, why should permanence and continuity not be conceivable in other things without needing to posit time and duration? If the second, then each part of time is prior to another or posterior to it. That priority and posteriority is not due to duration—otherwise infinite regress would follow. If that is conceivable, why should the occurrence of priority and posteriority in other things not be conceivable without duration and time? Sixth: Duration is not conceivable except where past, present, and future occur. What is future becomes present after it, then the present becomes past. This does not occur except where change and alteration occur. The Most High Creator is transcendent above alteration and change, so it is impossible for Him to fall under time, so it is impossible that His precedence over the world be through duration and time. Thus it is established by these six proofs that it is not necessary that the precedence of the Creator over the world be through duration and time. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents two of the six arguments (al-wujuh al-sitta) that God's precedence over the world is not necessarily temporal. The fifth argument considers whether time itself is permanent or originated. If permanent, its permanence is not due to another time, so we can conceive of permanence without time—therefore God's permanence need not be temporal. If time is originated, each part of time is prior or posterior to another, but that priority is not due to another time, so priority without time is conceivable. The sixth argument observes that time involves past, present, and future, which entail change, but God is changeless, so He cannot be in time; thus His precedence cannot be temporal. The conclusion is that the six proofs (only two and the conclusion are given here) establish that God's precedence is not through time.

Key Terms
مدة (duration)
زمان (time)
تقدم (priority)
تأخر (posteriority)
دوام (permanence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is inherently temporal.
Correction: The argument shows that priority can be conceived without time in both cases of time's nature.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fifth argument?
A) Time is always permanent.
B) Time is always originated.
C) Both possibilities show that priority can be conceived without time.
D) Priority requires time.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument demonstrates that God's precedence does not require temporal priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pressured by time—deadlines, aging, missed opportunities—how does it affect your relationship with Allah? Does the knowledge that His priority over you is not bounded by time change the way you seek His forgiveness or ask for patience?
?
You often measure your spiritual progress by time: how long you've prayed, how many years you've been Muslim. But if precedence can be non-temporal, what would it mean to measure your closeness to Allah by something other than time? What would that measure be, and how would you pursue it?
?
Think of a time you wished you could 'go back' or 'start over.' If Allah's priority is not temporal, does that shift your understanding of His mercy? Does it make repentance feel more accessible—or more urgent? Why?

The Sixth Argument: Time Implies Change, So God is Transcendent
The sixth argument linking time to change and God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument that God's precedence cannot be temporal because time involves change, which is incompatible with God's immutability.

Core Idea
Time entails change, but God is changeless, so He cannot be in time.

The sixth argument builds on the nature of time as involving past, present, and future. This sequence implies change, and since God is absolutely unchanging, He cannot be subject to time. Therefore, His precedence over the world cannot be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the definition of time as involving past, present, future.
Notice the logical sequence: time requires change, God is changeless.
2
Identify the key inference
Time implies change; God is changeless. Therefore, God cannot be in time.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion follows: God's precedence over the world is not temporal.
This is the final proof among the six.
