The Tenth Argument: A Fragment on Prior Worlds
Rāzi begins a proof using the possibility of another world before this one, but the argument is cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial setup of Rāzi's tenth argument for the eternity of time, and why the argument is considered incomplete as preserved.

Core Idea
Even a fragmented argument reveals key methods of kalām: hypothetical reasoning and dichotomy.

Rāzi's tenth argument is preserved only in fragment, but its opening shows a classic kalām move: suppose the world is originated, then imagine another world before it separated by cycles. The argument then sets up a dichotomy. Though the text breaks off, we can reconstruct the intended logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic fragment and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that it breaks off mid-sentence.
This fragment is only the beginning of a longer argument.
2
Understand the premise
The premise is: if the world is originated (ḥādith), then we can conceive another world before this one, separated by ten cycles (adwār).
This is a hypothetical scenario, not a claim of actual existence.
3
Recognize the dichotomy structure
The argument then presents a dichotomy: 'either it is possible...' (imā an yakūna mumkinan...). The text cuts off here.
In kalām, such dichotomies often lead to an absurdity or a contradiction.
4
Reflect on the incomplete nature
Consider that the argument is incomplete. From context, Rāzi likely intended to show that if the prior world is possible, an infinite regress of worlds follows, which is absurd; if not, the original world cannot be originated.
The full argument would need to be completed to assess its force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو كان العالم محدثاً ، فوجد عالم آخر قبل هذا العالم آخره إلى أول هذا العالم بعشرة أدوار ، إما أن يكون ممكناً ، بحيث ينتهي
The tenth proof: If the world were originated, and there existed another world before this world, its end being ten cycles before the beginning of this world, then either it is possible such that it ends...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the tenth proof (al-ḥujja al-‘āshira) for the eternity of time. The premise is that if the world is originated (ḥādith), one can suppose another world existing before it, separated by ten cycles (adwār). The argument then presents a dichotomy (imā...), but the text cuts off. From context, the intended argument likely is that either this prior world is possible (which would imply an infinite regress of worlds or an eternal time) or it is not possible, leading to a contradiction. However, since the text is incomplete, we can only note the initial setup.

أدوار (cycles)
إما (either)

Correction: The text is incomplete; it breaks off mid-sentence. The conclusion is missing, so we cannot treat it as a complete proof.

The Objection to the Time-Based Definition of Ḥudūth
Opponents present alternative definition and proof from infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' alternative definition of ḥudūth and their proof based on infinite regress.

Core Idea
Some argue ḥudūth needs no time, but their proof leads to an infinite regress.

In this passage, an objection is raised against the standard kalam definition of ḥudūth that requires a prior time. The opponents propose a simpler definition: just existence after non-existence, without temporal assumptions. They then argue that if time were needed, every part of time would itself be originated, requiring a prior time ad infinitum, which is absurd. This sets up the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Note the opponents' definition of ḥudūth and their key claim: no need for time.
The phrase 'من غير حاجة إلى تقرير مدة، وفرض زمان' is central.
2
Identify the opponents' proof
The opponents provide a proof by contradiction: if ḥudūth required time, each part of time would need a prior time, leading to infinite regress. Identify this argument.
Look for 'لزم أن يفتقر حدوث كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان، إلى زمان آخر'.
3
Reflect on the force of the argument
Consider why infinite regress is considered impossible (muḥāl) in kalam. This is a standard argument against temporal regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لا نسلم أن المعقول من الحدوث : هو أنه ما كان موجوداً في الوقت المتقدم ، ثم صار موجوداً . بل نقول : المعقول من الحدوث : أنه ما كان موجوداً ، ثم صار موجوداً من غير حاجة إلى تقرير مدة ، وفرض زمان . والدليل على صحة ما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان الأمر كما ذكرتم ، لزم أن يفتقر حدوث كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان، إلى زمان آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال . والجواب : إن قول القائل : إنه ما كان موجوداً : ثم صار موجوداً صريح في إثبات الزمان . لأن قولنا : ( كان ) : لفظ يدل على الماضي ولا يتصور العقل من الماضي ، إلا أمر من الأمور ، كان حاضراً ثم انقضى . وأيضاً لفظ ( ثم ) : يدل على حصول شيء ، بعد حصول شيء آخر . وكل هذه الألفاظ : تدل على أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يتصور معنى الحدوث البتة ، إلا بعد فرض مدة مستمرة ، و زمان دائم. فإن قالوا : فهذا تمسك بمجرد الألفاظ . فنقول : ليس الأمر هذا تنبيه على أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يتلفظ بلفظ، ولا أن يشير إلى معنى معقول ، إلا ويقرن حدوثه بزمان ، ويقرن عدمه السابق بزمان . وذلك يدل على أن الإقرار بدوام المدة ووجودها من الأزل إلى الأبد : مركوز في بدائه العقول وأما قوله : ( كان يلزم افتقار حدوث كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان ، إلى زمان آخر ، إلى غير النهاية : كنقول : وهكذا نقول ، لأنه لولا أن الجزء السابق كان موجوداً ، وإلا لامتنع أن يحكم على الجزء المتأخر : بكونه حادثاً متأخراً والله أعلم
For if they say: We do not accept that what is rationally understood from origination (al-ḥudūth) is that it is what was not existent in the preceding time, then became existent. Rather we say: What is rationally understood from origination is that it is what was not existent, then became existent, without needing to posit a duration (mudda) or assume a time (zamān). The proof for the correctness of what we have mentioned is: If the matter were as you have stated, it would necessitate that the origination of every single part of time (azminā) would depend on another time, ad infinitum, which is impossible (muḥāl). The answer: Indeed the statement of the one who says: 'It was not existent, then became existent' is explicit in affirming time (al-zamān), because our saying 'was' (kāna) is a word indicating the past, and the intellect cannot conceive of the past except as something that was present then elapsed. Also the word 'then' (thumma) indicates the occurrence of something after the occurrence of another. All these words indicate that the intellect cannot conceive the meaning of origination at all except after assuming a continuous duration and a perpetual time. If they say: This is merely clinging to words. We say: The matter is not so; it is an indication that the intellect cannot utter a word or refer to a rational meaning without associating its origination with a time, and associating its prior non-existence with a time. That indicates that the acknowledgment of the perpetuity of duration and its existence from pre-eternity to post-eternity is ingrained in the primary intuitions of intellects. As for his saying: 'It would necessitate that the origination of every part of time depends on another time ad infinitum.' We say: Thus we say, because if the prior part were not existent, it would be impossible to judge the later part as being originated and later. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the author's definition of ḥudūth (origination) and the author's response. The objectors claim that the rational meaning of ḥudūth does not require a preceding time; rather, something can be originated simply by transitioning from non-existence to existence without any temporal interval. They argue that if ḥudūth required time, then each part of time would itself be originated and require a prior time, leading to an infinite regress. The author refutes this by analyzing the language used: the words 'was' and 'then' inherently imply temporal prior and posterior. The intellect cannot conceive of past or sequence without assuming a continuous duration. He argues that even the objector's own definition uses these temporal terms, so it is impossible to express the concept of origination without implicitly affirming time. The objection that this is merely verbal is dismissed; it is about the necessary conceptual association. The author concludes that the recognition of eternal duration is ingrained in primary intuitions. Finally, he acknowledges that the infinite regress objection is indeed correct: without a prior part of time, no part can be originated, so each requires a prior, but this does not refute the necessity of time; it shows the necessity of an eternal time.

Key Terms
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth)
الزمان (al-zamān)
المدة (al-mudda)
غير النهاية (ghayr al-nihāya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ḥudūth can be defined without any notion of time.
Correction: The opponents themselves use temporal terms ('was', 'then') in their definition, showing that time is implicitly present even in their account, as the author will argue.

Checkpoint
What is the opponents' main argument against the author's definition of ḥudūth?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of times.
C) It is not based on rational intuition.
D) It requires belief in an eternal time.

Key Takeaway
The opponents define ḥudūth without time and argue that the author's definition leads to an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a debate about whether ḥudūth requires time. In your own understanding of Allah's creation of the universe, do you unconsciously assume that time is necessary for that act? How might that assumption limit your conception of Allah's absolute power, which is not bound by time? What step can you take today to train your mind to think of Allah's creative act as beyond temporal constraints?
?
The opponents argue that requiring time for origination leads to infinite regress. When you think about your own daily actions—your repentance, your prayers, your intentions—do you sometimes feel trapped by time, as if you need 'more time' to become a better Muslim? How does the concept of ḥudūth without time challenge that feeling, and what immediate change in your reliance on Allah should follow?

The Author's Refutation: Language Necessitates Time
Author refutes by showing language implies time and that infinite regress confirms eternal duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation: how language and reason inherently involve time, and why the infinite regress actually confirms the need for eternal duration.

Core Idea
Even the opponent's definition uses temporal words, proving time is inescapable in conceiving origination.

The author responds to the objection by analyzing the very words used by the opponents. He points out that the words 'was' and 'then' in their own definition imply past and sequence, which presuppose time. He then addresses the infinite regress argument, turning it into evidence for an eternal, uncreated time. This lesson unpacks that sophisticated response.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the answer. Notice how the author starts by analyzing the opponent's own statement.
Focus on the terms 'كان' and 'ثم'.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
The author argues that the words 'was' (كان) and 'then' (ثم) inherently refer to past and sequence, which are temporal concepts. Without assuming time, these words are meaningless.
The author claims this is not just verbal; it reflects a deep conceptual necessity.
3
Follow the response to the infinite regress objection
The author agrees that each part of time requires a prior part, but this simply shows that time must be eternal (without beginning) rather than created. The regress is not vicious; it indicates the necessity of perpetual duration.
The phrase 'وهكذا نقول' shows acceptance of the regress as evidence for eternal time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لا نسلم أن المعقول من الحدوث : هو أنه ما كان موجوداً في الوقت المتقدم ، ثم صار موجوداً . بل نقول : المعقول من الحدوث : أنه ما كان موجوداً ، ثم صار موجوداً من غير حاجة إلى تقرير مدة ، وفرض زمان . والدليل على صحة ما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان الأمر كما ذكرتم ، لزم أن يفتقر حدوث كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان، إلى زمان آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال . والجواب : إن قول القائل : إنه ما كان موجوداً : ثم صار موجوداً صريح في إثبات الزمان . لأن قولنا : ( كان ) : لفظ يدل على الماضي ولا يتصور العقل من الماضي ، إلا أمر من الأمور ، كان حاضراً ثم انقضى . وأيضاً لفظ ( ثم ) : يدل على حصول شيء ، بعد حصول شيء آخر . وكل هذه الألفاظ : تدل على أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يتصور معنى الحدوث البتة ، إلا بعد فرض مدة مستمرة ، و زمان دائم. فإن قالوا : فهذا تمسك بمجرد الألفاظ . فنقول : ليس الأمر هذا تنبيه على أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يتلفظ بلفظ، ولا أن يشير إلى معنى معقول ، إلا ويقرن حدوثه بزمان ، ويقرن عدمه السابق بزمان . وذلك يدل على أن الإقرار بدوام المدة ووجودها من الأزل إلى الأبد : مركوز في بدائه العقول وأما قوله : ( كان يلزم افتقار حدوث كل واحد من أجزاء الزمان ، إلى زمان آخر ، إلى غير النهاية : كنقول : وهكذا نقول ، لأنه لولا أن الجزء السابق كان موجوداً ، وإلا لامتنع أن يحكم على الجزء المتأخر : بكونه حادثاً متأخراً والله أعلم
For if they say: We do not accept that what is rationally understood from origination (al-ḥudūth) is that it is what was not existent in the preceding time, then became existent. Rather we say: What is rationally understood from origination is that it is what was not existent, then became existent, without needing to posit a duration (mudda) or assume a time (zamān). The proof for the correctness of what we have mentioned is: If the matter were as you have stated, it would necessitate that the origination of every single part of time (azminā) would depend on another time, ad infinitum, which is impossible (muḥāl). The answer: Indeed the statement of the one who says: 'It was not existent, then became existent' is explicit in affirming time (al-zamān), because our saying 'was' (kāna) is a word indicating the past, and the intellect cannot conceive of the past except as something that was present then elapsed. Also the word 'then' (thumma) indicates the occurrence of something after the occurrence of another. All these words indicate that the intellect cannot conceive the meaning of origination at all except after assuming a continuous duration and a perpetual time. If they say: This is merely clinging to words. We say: The matter is not so; it is an indication that the intellect cannot utter a word or refer to a rational meaning without associating its origination with a time, and associating its prior non-existence with a time. That indicates that the acknowledgment of the perpetuity of duration and its existence from pre-eternity to post-eternity is ingrained in the primary intuitions of intellects. As for his saying: 'It would necessitate that the origination of every part of time depends on another time ad infinitum.' We say: Thus we say, because if the prior part were not existent, it would be impossible to judge the later part as being originated and later. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the author's definition of ḥudūth (origination) and the author's response. The objectors claim that the rational meaning of ḥudūth does not require a preceding time; rather, something can be originated simply by transitioning from non-existence to existence without any temporal interval. They argue that if ḥudūth required time, then each part of time would itself be originated and require a prior time, leading to an infinite regress. The author refutes this by analyzing the language used: the words 'was' and 'then' inherently imply temporal prior and posterior. The intellect cannot conceive of past or sequence without assuming a continuous duration. He argues that even the objector's own definition uses these temporal terms, so it is impossible to express the concept of origination without implicitly affirming time. The objection that this is merely verbal is dismissed; it is about the necessary conceptual association. The author concludes that the recognition of eternal duration is ingrained in primary intuitions. Finally, he acknowledges that the infinite regress objection is indeed correct: without a prior part of time, no part can be originated, so each requires a prior, but this does not refute the necessity of time; it shows the necessity of an eternal time.

الزمان (al-zamān)
المدة (al-mudda)
بدائه العقول (badāʾih al-ʿuqūl)
العدم السابق (al-ʿadam al-sābiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author's refutation is merely semantic wordplay.
Correction: The author insists that the necessity of time is a conceptual, not just verbal, requirement; it is ingrained in primary intuitions.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that his argument is merely clinging to words?
A) He concedes and offers a different argument.
B) He argues it is about necessary conceptual association, not just words.
C) He says words are irrelevant to rational understanding.
D) He accuses the opponents of linguistic ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that time is inescapable in defining ḥudūth, and the infinite regress actually proves the need for eternal duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'was' in your daily speech, do you realize that you are affirming a belief in time and change? How does it change your awareness of the Eternal when you catch yourself thinking in temporal terms about Allah? This week, each time you say 'was', pause and remind yourself that Allah is beyond time — then ask for forgiveness for any fleeting assumption of temporality in His essence.
?
The author says the acknowledgment of perpetual duration is 'ingrained in the primary intuitions of intellects'. Look inside yourself: do you find it impossible to think of origination without presupposing time? If so, does this strengthen your certainty that there must be an eternal Creator who is not subject to time? Commit to using this intuitive awareness as a silent proof whenever doubt creeps in.
?
The opponent feared infinite regress, but the author embraced it. What fear or discomfort do you have about accepting infinite sequences in matters of faith? Are you willing to let go of the need for a finite starting point for time and instead submit to the necessity of an eternal duration that points to Allah's eternity? Resolve today to trust the scholars who accept such rational conclusions, and ask Allah to increase your intellectual humility.

Argument from Increase and Decrease for the Existence of Time
Argument that increase and decrease necessitate time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concepts of increase and decrease logically necessitate the existence of time as a distinct entity.

Core Idea
Increase and decrease cannot belong to God or non-existence — they point to time.

This lesson presents a concise rational argument for the existence of time. The author considers three possibilities for where increase and decrease can inhere: God's essence, pure non-existence, or a third thing. By eliminating the first two, the existence of time becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Follow the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the three possibilities listed at the beginning.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The argument starts with a disjunction: increase and decrease must belong to either God's essence, pure non-existence, or a third thing. List them mentally.
These are the key alternatives.
3
Understand why God is excluded
The first possibility is rejected because God's essence is free from accepting increase and decrease. This is a basic theological principle.
God is transcendent and unchanging.
4
Understand why non-existence is excluded
Pure non-existence cannot have attributes like increase and decrease, as it is not a thing that can receive descriptions.
Non-existence is not a subject for qualities.
5
Conclude that the third is necessary
With both alternatives eliminated, the third must be accepted. That third thing is time (al-mudda) or something that occurs in time, which suffices to prove the existence of time.
The argument is complete: time exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنقصان، إما أن يكون ذات الله تعالى أو لعدم، أو شيء ثالث والأول باطل. لأن ذات الله منزه عن قبول الزيادة والنقصان. وأما العدم المحض، فقد ذكرنا: أنه لا يقبل الوصف بالزيادة والنقصان. فلا بد من الاعتراف بوجود أمر آخر مغاير لذات الله تعالى وللعدم المحض. وذلك الأمر الذي يقبل هذه الزيادة والنقصان. وذلك هو المدة، أو شيء يقع في المدة. وعلى التقديرين، فالمطلوب حاصل والله أعلم.
And decrease and increase: either they belong to the essence of God, or to non-existence, or a third thing. The first is false because God's essence is free from accepting increase and decrease. As for pure non-existence, we have mentioned that it does not accept the description of increase and decrease. So it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of another thing different from God's essence and from pure non-existence. That thing which accepts this increase and decrease is al-mudda (time) or something that occurs in time. On both estimates, the desired conclusion is established, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents an argument (likely the eleventh of twelve) for the existence of time (al-mudda). The author considers the attribute of increase and decrease (al-ziyada wa al-nuqsan). He states that increase and decrease cannot be attributed to God's essence because God is transcendent and does not accept change. They also cannot be attributed to pure non-existence (al-‘adam al-mahd) because non-existence is not a thing that can have attributes. Therefore, they must belong to a third entity that is distinct from both God and pure non-existence. That entity is either time (al-mudda) itself or something that occurs within time. In either case, the conclusion (al-matlab) is established: time exists. The author ends with 'wa-Allahu a‘lam' (and God knows best). The argument is a simple disjunction (either God, nothing, or a third) eliminating the first two possibilities, leaving the third.

الزيادة والنقصان (increase and decrease)
الذات (essence)
العدم المحض (pure non-existence)
المدة (time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think increase and decrease are attributes of God.
Correction: God is transcendent and does not accept increase or decrease, as the text explicitly states: 'God's essence is free from accepting increase and decrease.'

Checkpoint

C) Because increase and decrease are accidents
D) Because God is the creator of time

Key Takeaway
The existence of time is rationally necessary because only it can receive increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Inconceivability of Beginning and Duration Without Time
The twelfth argument: time must be presupposed for conceiving change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concepts of beginning (huduth) and duration (dawam) necessarily presuppose time, and how this leads to the conclusion that time itself cannot be originated.

Core Idea
Time must be presupposed for any notion of beginning or duration.

This final argument in the series tackles a foundational epistemological point: our very concepts of 'beginning' (huduth) and 'duration' (dawam) are impossible without assuming the existence of time. The author argues that whenever we think of something coming into existence or enduring, we inevitably frame it within a temporal context. Therefore, claiming that time itself has a beginning leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا نعقل' (we do not conceive) – it sets the epistemological tone.
2
Identify the definitions of originated and eternal
Find where the author defines what we mean by an originated thing (al-hadith) and an eternal thing (al-da'im al-qadim). Note how both definitions involve time.
3
Understand the reductio argument
The author argues that if time itself were originated, then its origination would require a prior time, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. This shows that time cannot be originated.
Think of it as: if time began, what time did it begin in?
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that the assumption of time's non-existence is impossible, because that very assumption would require a concept of time to make sense of 'non-existence' in a temporal sense.
5
Reflect on the twelve arguments
The author mentions that these are the twelve arguments for establishing time (al-mudda). Consider how this argument fits with the previous ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر: إنا لا نعقل حدوث شيء، ولا دوام شيء، إلا مع فرض الوقت والزمان. فإنا البتة لا نعقل من الحادث، إلا أنه الذي حدث في وقت بعد أن كان معدوماً في الوقت السابق عليه. ولا نعقل البتة من الدائم القديم، إلا أنه الذي يفرض وجوده حاصلا في وقت، إلا وقد كان موجوداً قبل ذلك. فإذا كنا لا نعقل معنى الحدوث، ومعنى البقاء إلا مع فرض الأوقات، إما بهذه العبارة المذكورة أو بعبارات أخرى، تفيد أيضاً: معاني الأوقات. فحينئذ ظهر أنه لا يمكننا أن نعقل معنى الحدوث ومعنى الدوام، إلا مع تقدير وجود الأوقات. وإذا ثبت هذا، ظهر أن القول بحدوث المدة والوقت: محال. إلا أنا إذا حكمنا بحدوثهما، وثبت أن الحدوث لا يمكن تعقله إلا مع فرض الوقت، لزم أنا متى فرضنا عدم الوقت، فإنه يلزم من فرض عدمه، فرض وجوده. وذلك يقتضي أن يكون فرض عدمه محالاً. فهذه الوجوه الاثني عشر في هذا الباب مذكورة لإثبات هذا المطلوب. ومن أراد الزيادة عليها، فلينظر فيما كتبناه في تحقيق الكلام في المدة والزمان والله أعلم بالصواب.
The twelfth argument: We do not conceive of the beginning of anything nor the duration of anything except with the assumption of time and temporality. For we absolutely do not conceive of the originated (al-hadith) except as that which occurs in a time after having been non-existent in the previous time. And we absolutely do not conceive of the eternal perpetual (al-da’im al-qadim) except as that whose existence is assumed to be obtained in a time, but it was existent before that. Since we do not conceive of the meaning of origination (huduth) and the meaning of continuance (baqa’) except with the assumption of times, either by this mentioned expression or other expressions that also indicate the meanings of times. Then it becomes clear that we cannot conceive the meaning of huduth and the meaning of dawam except with the estimation of the existence of times. And when this is established, it becomes clear that the saying that al-mudda (time) and al-waqt (moment) are originated is impossible. However, if we judge that they are originated, and it is established that origination cannot be conceived except with the assumption of time, then whenever we assume the non-existence of time, it follows from the assumption of its non-existence the assumption of its existence. And that necessitates that the assumption of its non-existence is impossible. So these are the twelve arguments mentioned in this chapter to establish this desired conclusion. And whoever wants more than them, let him look at what we have written in the verification of the discussion of al-mudda and al-zaman. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth and final argument for the existence of time. The argument is epistemological: we cannot conceive (na‘qil) of anything coming into existence (huduth) or of something eternal (da’im qadim) without assuming time (waqt and zaman). The author explains that what we understand by 'originated' (hadith) is that it occurs in a time after being non-existent in the previous time. Similarly, the eternal is that which is conceived as existing in any given time but also existed before. Thus, the very notion of beginning and enduring is tied to time. Therefore, to claim that time itself is originated (hadith) leads to a contradiction: if time originated, then its origination would require a prior time, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. Moreover, if we try to assume the non-existence of time, then the concept of origination becomes meaningless. The argument is a reductio: the assumption that time is originated implies that its own origination requires time, which is impossible. Hence, time must be eternal or necessary for the conception of change. The author concludes by stating that these are the twelve arguments for establishing the existence of time (al-mudda) and notes that further discussion can be found in his other writings on the topic.

Key Terms
الحدوث
الدوام
الوقت
الزمان
المدة
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the reality of time independent of God, or about time being a created entity.
Correction: The argument is epistemological: it shows that time is a necessary conceptual precondition for understanding change and duration. It does not directly address whether time is created or eternal in a theological sense, but rather that it cannot be originated in the same way as contingent things.

Checkpoint
According to the twelfth argument, what is the meaning of an originated thing (al-hadith)?
A) Something that comes from nothing
B) Something that occurs after being non-existent in the previous time
C) Something that has a cause
D) Something that is created directly by God

Key Takeaway
Time is a necessary condition for understanding beginning and duration; thus time itself cannot have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time must be presupposed for any change, then your every action, thought, and intention is framed by time. Does this realisation make you more mindful that each moment is a gift from the Eternal Creator? How will you change your use of time today in light of this truth?

The argument shows that you cannot conceive of your own existence without time. Yet time itself cannot have a beginning. What does this say about the nature of the One who is beyond time? How does this affect your worship and reliance on Him as the Timeless?
?
You have just understood that assuming the non-existence of time is impossible. Yet you often live as if time is your own possession to waste or fill. Will you now see time as a divine sign pointing to its Creator? What specific step will you take to treat time as sacred?

Self-evident Sciences and the Necessity of Dimensions
Argument for self-evident sciences and necessity of dimensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-evident sciences are necessary to avoid circularity in proofs and how the author argues that dimensions and body are necessary in themselves.

Core Idea
Without self-evident sciences, all reasoning would be trapped in circularity.

Before we can prove anything about God, we need foundations that themselves require no proof. In this passage, the author establishes that self-evident sciences (بديهيات) are the bedrock of all argumentation. He then makes a bold claim: the existence of spatial dimensions is necessary in itself, leading to the necessity of body. However, the argument is incomplete as the passage cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once through, noticing the logical flow. Don't worry about the corrupted opening; focus on the main argument.
The opening phrase 'الوجوه المستنبطة في حفه الباب' may have textual issues; treat it as a heading.
2
Understand the circularity argument
Identify why the author says self-evident sciences must exist. They are the foundation for all proofs; otherwise, we get circular reasoning (دور).
The key phrase: 'فلو وقفنا البديهيات على الأمور التي يحاول إثباتها بالدلائل والبينات، لزم الدور'.
3
Define necessary knowledge
Find the definition of 'العلم الضروري' – it is what the innate disposition of the intellect and soul's intuition attest to.
This bridges the self-evident sciences to the next step about dimensions.
4
Examine the claim about dimensions
Notice that the author declares dimensions (أبعاد) as 'واجب الوجود لذاته' – necessary in themselves. This is a metaphysical claim that body is also necessary.
The argument is cut off, but the claim is clear: if dimensions are necessary, body (composed of them) is also necessary.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The deduced aspects concerning the proof of the door relating to the [؟]. Thus, arguments and evidences depend for their validity on self-evident sciences. If we made the self-evident sciences depend on the matters that one tries to prove by proofs and evidences, circularity would result—and it is impossible. Commentary
This passage is an argument about the necessity of self-evident sciences (بديهية) and their implications for the existence of dimensions and body. The author begins by stating that they have already proven the necessity of admitting primary self-evident sciences that require no proof. The reason is that any proof is built from already accepted sciences to reach an unknown judgment; thus proofs depend on self-evident sciences. If self-evident sciences themselves depended on what they are trying to prove, circularity (دور) would occur, which is impossible. Therefore, self-evident sciences are self-sustaining. Next, necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري) is defined as what the innate disposition of the intellect (فطرة العقل) and the soul's intuition (بديهة النفس) testify to. After settling that, the author makes a metaphysical claim: the existence of dimensions (أبعاد) in the three spatial directions—length, width, and depth—is necessary in itself (واجب الوجود لذاته). From this, it follows that body (الجسم), which is defined by these dimensions, is also necessary in itself. The author then begins to explain the first point (المقام الأول): for anything possible, assuming it occurs does not entail impossibility. So if the removal of dimensions were possible, their removal could be assumed without contradiction. The passage breaks off here, leaving the argument incomplete on this page.

بديهة النفس
أبعاد ممتدة

جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'necessary in itself' (واجب الوجود لذاته) always refers to God.
Correction: In this passage, the author uses 'واجب الوجود لذاته' for dimensions and body as part of a metaphysical argument that later may lead to God. Here it means they are self-subsistent, not contingent on something else.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must there be self-evident sciences?
A) Because all sciences are innate.
B) Because without them, proofs would lead to circularity.
C) Because the intellect cannot doubt them.
D) Because they are necessary for the existence of dimensions.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident sciences are the necessary foundation of all proofs, and their existence is established by the impossibility of circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on self-evident truths in your daily life, or do you demand proof for everything, even at the risk of falling into circular reasoning? Where in your faith have you unknowingly demanded proof for what is foundational?
?
When you reflect on the 'innate disposition of your intellect', do you find that it testifies to truths about God and existence, or have you silenced it with skepticism? What would it mean to trust that intuition again?
?
If dimensions and body are necessary in themselves, what does that say about the trustworthiness of the physical world as a sign of the Creator? Are you using the material world as a means to know God, or are you distracted by it?

The Necessity of Primary Intuitive Sciences
Author argues that intuitive sciences must be self-evident to avoid circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam must begin with self-evident intuitive sciences that require no proof, and how circular reasoning is avoided.

Core Idea
All proofs rest on premises that themselves need no proof — the intuitive sciences.

Before we can build any theological argument, we need a foundation that is certain without proof. In this passage, the author establishes that such foundational sciences exist, and he explains why they cannot require proof without leading to an impossible circularity. This argument is essential for the entire method of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intuitive sciences
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the claim that there must be primary intuitive sciences that need no proof.
The author uses the term 'بديهية أولية' — primary intuitive sciences.
2
Understand the circularity argument
Notice the reasoning: if every premise needed proof, then proofs would depend on premises and premises on proof, leading to a vicious circle (دور). The author says this is impossible.
This is a classic argument for foundationalism.
3
Define necessary knowledge
The author then defines 'necessary knowledge' (العلم الضروري) as what the innate constitution of the intellect and the intuition of the soul testify to. This is the criterion for intuitive sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجوه المستنبطة في حفه الباب مما يتعلق بالمكـ قالوا : قد دللنا على أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود علوم بديهية أولية ، لا يحتاج في إثباتها أو في تقريرها إلى حجة وبينة. وذلك لأنه لا معنى للحجة والبيئة : إلا تركيب علوم مسلّمة ليتوسل بها إلى الحكم بأمر مجهول الثبوت فعلى هذا : الحجج والبينات : موقوفة الصحة على البديهيات . فلو وقفنا البديهيات على الأمور التي يحاول إثباتها بالدلائل والبينات ، لزم الدور . وإنه محال فيثبت : أن العلوم البديهية : مسلمة بأنفسها ، متقررة بذواتها ، من غير أن يحتاج إثباتها إلى الدليل والبيئة. إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه لا معنى للعلم الضروري، إلا ما تشهد بصحته فطرة العقل ، وبديهة النفس . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وجود الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً : أمر واجب الوجود لذاته . ومتى ثبت هذا ، كان الجسم موجوداً واجب الوجود لذاته. أما المقام الأول : فهو أن كل ما كان ممكناً ، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال فلو كان ارتفاع الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً : ممكناً لذاته، لكنا إذا فرضنا ارتفاع هذه الابعاد ، فعند هذا الفرض وجب أن لا يلزم المحال . إلا أن المحال لازم لا محالة ، لأن عند ذلك الفرض ، إذا فرضنا حيواناً واقفاً على طرف العالم فإما أن يتميز الجانب الذي يلي وجهه عن الجانب الذي يلي قفاه ، وإما أن لا يتميز . والقسم الثاني مدفوع في بديهة العقل . لأن فطرة النفس شاهدة بأن على جميع التقديرات ، فإنه لابد وأن يتميز الجانب الأيمن عن الجانب الأيسر ، والجانب الذي يحاذي الوجه ، عن الجانب الذي يحاذي القفا . والقول بأنه تحصل حالة لا يحصل معها هذا الامتياز ، مما لا يقبله العقل ، وإذا ثبت حصول هذا الامتياز ، فقد حصلت الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً . فيثبت : أن هذه الأبعاد موجودة ، وأنها غير قابلة للعدم البتة ، فكانت واجبة الوجود لذواتها .
Derived expressions in its gate [al-bāb] regarding what relates to the... They said: we have demonstrated that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of primary intuitive sciences (ʿulūm badīhiyya awwaliyya), which do not need for their proof or establishment a proof (ḥujja) or evidence (bayyina). That is because proof and evidence mean nothing other than combining accepted sciences to reach a judgment about something whose establishment is unknown. So on this basis, proofs and evidences are valid only through the intuitive sciences. So if we made the intuitive sciences depend on the matters that one tries to prove by proofs and evidences, a vicious circle (dawr) would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that the intuitive sciences are accepted by themselves, established by themselves, without needing proof or evidence for their proof. When this is established, we say: necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) is obtained that 'necessary knowledge' means nothing other than what the natural constitution of the intellect (fiṭrat al-ʿaql) and the intuition of the soul (badīhat al-nafs) testify to its soundness. When this is established, we say: The existence of extended dimensions (al-abʿād al-mumtadda) — length, width, and depth — is a matter necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) in itself. And when this is established, then body (al-jism) exists as necessary of existence in itself. As for the first position (al-maqām al-awwal): it is that everything that is possible (mumkin), no contradiction (muḥāl) follows from the assumption of its occurrence. So if the removal of extended dimensions — length, width, and depth — were possible in itself, then if we assumed the removal of these dimensions, upon that assumption it would be necessary that no contradiction follows. But a contradiction necessarily follows, because upon that assumption, if we assume an animal standing at the edge of the world, then either the side facing its face is distinguished from the side facing its back, or it is not distinguished. The second division is rejected by the intuition of the intellect. For the natural constitution of the soul witnesses that in all assessments, it is necessary that the right side be distinguished from the left side, and the side facing the face from the side facing the back. Saying that a state occurs in which this distinction does not occur is something the intellect does not accept. And if this distinction is established, then the extended dimensions — length, width, and depth — are established. So it is established that these dimensions exist, and that they are absolutely not subject to non-existence, so they are necessary of existence in themselves.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of intuitive sciences and an argument for the necessary existence of extended dimensions. The author begins by stating that there must be primary intuitive sciences that need no proof, because all proofs are composed of accepted premises; if those premises themselves required proof, there would be an infinite regress or circularity. Thus intuitive sciences are self-established. Then he defines necessary knowledge as that which is attested by the innate constitution of the intellect and intuition of the soul. Using this, he argues that extended dimensions (length, width, depth) are necessary existents. The proof is a reductio: if dimensions were possible (contingent), then assuming their non-existence should imply no contradiction, but it does. Because if you imagine an animal standing at the edge of the world (assuming dimensions are absent), then either its front and back sides are distinguished or not. The intellect necessarily affirms distinction, which implies extension. Therefore dimensions cannot be absent; they exist necessarily. The conclusion is that dimensions are necessary existents, and consequently body is also necessary existent.

Key Terms
علوم بديهية أولية
فطرة العقل
بديهة النفس
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all knowledge, including foundational principles, requires proof.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to a vicious circle; foundational sciences must be self-evident and need no proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would follow if intuitive sciences themselves required proof?
A) A vicious circle (dawr)
B) Infinite regress
C) Contradiction
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The foundation of all knowledge is self-evident intuitive sciences that require no proof; otherwise, reasoning would be circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on logical reasoning every day. But have you ever paused to ask: on what ultimate premise does your trust in reason rest? This lesson says that some sciences are self-evident. What would it mean for you if you discovered that a cherished belief of yours actually does not rest on such a foundation? Will you commit to identifying one such belief this week and tracing it back to its roots?
?
The author argues that if every premise needed proof, we would never know anything. Are there ways you demand proof for certain matters of faith while accepting other things (like your own existence, or the reality of your senses) without proof? Is that inconsistency a sign of intellectual honesty or fear? Ask yourself: what are you afraid to admit requires no proof?
?
This passage defines necessary knowledge as what the natural constitution of the intellect and the intuition of the soul testify to. When was the last time you truly listened to that inner testimony, without the noise of opinions or doubts? Perhaps today, you can set aside all argument and ask: what do I know without needing to prove? Write it down, and let that be your starting point.

Proof of the Necessary Existence of Extended Dimensions
Author proves that extended dimensions are necessary existents via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that extended dimensions (length, width, depth) exist necessarily and cannot be removed without contradiction.

Core Idea
If dimensions could be absent, a contradiction follows; therefore they are necessary existents.

Having established the foundation of intuitive sciences, the author now applies it to prove that the physical dimensions of length, width, and depth are necessary existents. This is a crucial step in proving the existence of body as necessary. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the removal of dimensions leads to an intellectual contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dimensions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. The author presents a proof by contradiction.
The passage begins with 'إذا ثبت هذا' referring back to the previous lesson.
2
Understand the reductio structure
The author assumes for reductio that dimensions could be removed (i.e., they are possible). Then he shows that this assumption leads to a contradiction involving the distinction of sides of an animal at the edge of the world.
The contradiction is that the sides would not be distinguished, which the intellect rejects.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to an impossibility, dimensions cannot be possible; they must be necessary existents.
This proof is used to establish the necessary existence of body.

Refuting Skepticism of Innate Judgments
Author distinguishes intellect and imagination in innate judgments to refute skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the skeptical claim that innate judgments can be false by showing that distinguishing intellect from imagination leads to circularity.

Core Idea
Innate judgments are certain; doubting them leads to logical circularity.

The author addresses an objection: if innate disposition contains both intellect (true) and imagination (false), how can we trust innate judgments? He exposes the flaw by examining how we know which judge is truthful, showing that the opponent's position leads to a vicious circle. This lesson walks through that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice that the opponent concedes innate disposition testifies to the proposition but claims there are two rulers: intellect (true) and imagination (false).
3
Understand the author's dilemma
The author asks how we know one ruler is truthful and the other false. If that knowledge is immediate, then we already have certainty; if it is theoretical, then circularity occurs because theoretical premises depend on immediate truths.
4
Conclude the refutation
The opponent's view leads to sophistry and impugning all immediate sciences. Therefore, whatever the sound innate disposition initially accepts must be certainly true.
5
Note the reference to logic
The author mentions that the full discussion is in logic, at the distinction between immediate and primary premises. This shows the broader context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : إنكم سلمتم أن الفطرة الأصلية شاهدة بصحة هذه القضية . فنقول : قولهم : إن في الفطرة حاكمان : أحدهما : العقل وحكمه صحيح ، والثاني : الوهم ، وحكمه باطل . فنقول : علمنا بأن أحد الحاكمين صادق والآخر كاذب . إن كان علماً بديهياً فيلزم أن لا يحصل الجزم القاطع في حكم الحاكم الكاذب وإن كان فطرياً فحينئذ تتوقف صحة البديهيات على هذه المقدمة النظرية . ولا شك أنها موقوفة على القضايا البديهية. فيلزم وقوع الدور ، وإنه باطل . فيثبت : أن قول القائل : إن الأحكام الفطرية ، إن كانت من نتائج العقل فهي صحيحة. وإن كانت من نتائج الوهم ، فهي باطلة توجب السفسطة، وتوجب القدح في جميع العلوم البديهية وإنه باطل. فيثبت : أن كل ما اعترف به أول الفطرة السليمة ، وجب الحكم بصحته قطعاً . وتمام الكلام في هذا الباب : مذكور في المنطق ، عند الفرق بين المقدمات البديهية ، وبين المقدمات الأولية
And the answer to the first question is that we say: You have conceded that the original innate disposition (al-fiṭrah al-aṣliyyah) bears witness to the soundness of this proposition. So we say: Their statement that in the innate disposition there are two rulers: one of them is the intellect (‘aql) and its judgment is sound, and the second is imagination (wahm) and its judgment is false. Then we say: Our knowledge that one of the two rulers is truthful and the other is lying—if it is immediate knowledge (badīhī), then it follows that decisive certainty does not occur in the judgment of the false ruler; and if it is innate (fiṭrī), then the soundness of immediate truths depends on this theoretical premise. And there is no doubt that they depend on immediate propositions. Thus circularity (dawr) occurs, and it is false. So it is established that the statement of the one who says: 'Innate judgments (al-aḥkām al-fiṭriyyah), if they are results of the intellect, are sound; and if they are results of imagination, are false'—this leads to sophistry (safsatah) and leads to impugning all immediate sciences, and it is false. So it is established that everything that the sound innate disposition acknowledges at first must be judged as certainly sound. And the complete discussion of this topic is mentioned in logic (al-manṭiq), at the distinction between immediate premises and primary premises.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a first objection. The author begins by noting that the opponent concedes that the original innate disposition testifies to the truth of the proposition in question. The opponent then claims that within the innate disposition there are two judges: intellect (sound) and imagination (false). The author refutes this by examining how we know one is truthful and the other false. If that knowledge is immediate, then we already have certainty that the false judge's ruling is not certain — so no problem. If it is innate (i.e., theoretical), then the foundation of immediate knowledge depends on a theoretical premise, but that premise itself depends on immediate knowledge; this creates a vicious circle. Therefore, the opponent's position leads to accepting sophistry and undermining all sciences. The conclusion is that whatever the sound innate disposition initially accepts must be judged as certainly true. The author notes that the full treatment of this topic is in logic, regarding the distinction between immediate and primary premises.

وهم

فطري
دور
سفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the presence of false innate judgments (from imagination) invalidates all innate judgments.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to circularity; therefore, the sound innate disposition's initial judgments are certainly true.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the author identify if the distinction between intellect and imagination in innate judgments is itself innate (fiṭrī)?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It leads to circularity (dawr).
C) It leads to contradiction.
D) It leads to equivocation.

Key Takeaway
The sound innate disposition's initial judgments are certainly true; any attempt to question them leads to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt creeps into your heart about a belief you have always held as certain, do you immediately begin to deconstruct it with logic, or do you trust the innate certainty that first settled in your soul? How might this passage call you to examine which seeds of doubt you are watering?
?
Think of a time you used sophisticated reasoning to talk yourself out of something your inner disposition (fiṭrah) knew to be true. What was the cost of that? Will you now repent and realign with that original certainty?
?
This passage says that every initial judgment of the sound innate disposition is certainly true. Examine your life: is there an area where you have allowed imagination or false reasoning to override that innate certainty about Allah, the truth of Islam, or your own accountability? What step will you take today to restore that certainty and act on it?

The Opponents' Argument Against an Eternal World
Razi presents the opponents' argument with a rational proof and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that if the world were eternal, it would be independent of a cause, and the rational proof and examples they used to support this claim.

Core Idea
If the world were eternal, it would need no cause — which opponents use to deny the Creator.

This lesson presents the argument of those who affirm the temporal origination of the world (huduth) against the possibility of an eternal world. They claim that an eternal world would be independent of a cause, and they support this with a rational proof and several analogies. Understanding this argument is crucial for engaging with the debate on the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' claim
Read the passage once to get the main claim: that an eternal world would be independent of the efficient cause, leading to denial of the Maker.
Notice the distinction between proof and example.
2
Analyze the rational proof
The proof defines the eternal as that which has always existed. If its existence were caused, that would require bringing into existence what already exists, which is impossible.
This is a key logical principle in kalam.
3
Examine the analogies
The passage gives three examples: building, henna dye, and a thrown stone. Each illustrates that something does not need a cause for its continued existence, only for its origination.
Consider how these analogies support the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن كون العالم أزليا لا يقتضي استضافه عن المؤثر. اعلم أن القائلين بالحدوث قالوا: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان غنياً عن المؤثر، وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع بالكلية. واحتجوا على صحة قولهم بأنه لو كان قديماً، لكان غنياً عن المؤثر بحجة ومثال. أما الحجة: فهي أنهم قالوا: القديم هو الذي يكون موجوداً أبداً. فلو قلنا: إن وجوده حصل لسبب، لزم منه إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال. وأما المثال: فهو أنهم قالوا: البناء يحتاج إلى الباني حال حدوثه، وأما حال بقائه فلا يحتاج إليه. وأيضاً: إن من خضب يده بالحناء، فإنه يحتاج في أول الأمر إلى إلصاق الحناء باليد، ثم إن اللون يبقى بعد زوال ذلك السبب. وأيضاً: إن من رمى حجراً إلى فوق، فإن المذهب الصحيح أن ذلك القاسر يحدث في ذلك الجسم قوة قسرية، تحرك ذلك الجسم إلى فوق. فتلك القوة حال حدوثها تحتاج إلى المؤثر، وأما حال بقائها فإنها غنية عن المؤثر. فهذا جملة كلام القوم.
An explanation that the world's being eternal does not necessitate its independence from the effect. Know that the proponents of temporal origination said: If the world were eternal, it would be independent of the effect, and that necessitates the complete denial of the Maker. And they argued for the correctness of their statement that if it were eternal, it would be independent of the effect, with a proof and an example. As for the proof: it is that they said: The eternal is that which exists forever. So if we say that its existence came about due to a cause, that would necessitate bringing into existence what already exists and procuring what is already procured, which is impossible. And as for the example: it is that they said: The building needs the builder at the time of its origination, but at the time of its persistence it does not need him. Also, whoever dyes his hand with henna, he needs at the first instance to attach the henna to the hand, then the color remains after the removal of that cause. Also, whoever throws a stone upward, the correct doctrine is that that coercer produces in that body a coercive force that moves that body upward. So that force at the time of its origination needs the effect, but at the time of its persistence it is independent of the effect. This is the sum of the people's statement.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of those who affirm the temporal origination of the world (al-huduth) against the claim that an eternal world could be independent of a cause. It begins by stating their claim: if the world were eternal (qadim), it would be independent of the efficient cause (mu'aththir), which would negate the existence of the Creator entirely. They support this with two types of evidence: a rational proof (hujjah) and an example (mithal). The rational proof defines the eternal as that which has always existed. If its existence were caused, that would require bringing into existence what already exists (ijad al-mawjud) and procuring what is already procured (tahsil al-hasil), both of which are impossible. The example uses three analogies: a building, which only needs a builder during its construction, not during its continued existence; henna dye, which requires application only initially, then the color persists without the cause; and a thrown stone, where the coercive force (quwwa qasriyya) that moves it upward needs a cause at its origination but not during its persistence. These analogies illustrate that once something exists or a quality is produced, it does not require the cause for its continued existence. The passage concludes that this is the complete argument of the opponents.

Key Terms
قديم
حدوث
مؤثر
إيجاد الموجود
تحصيل الحاصل
قوة قسرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents believe that an eternal being cannot have a cause at all.
Correction: They actually argue that if the world were eternal, it would be independent of a cause, which they consider impossible for the world. The world, they claim, is clearly temporal and needs a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents, what would be the consequence if the world were eternal?
A) It would require a cause for its persistence.
B) It would be independent of the efficient cause, negating the Maker.
C) It would be impossible for it to have a cause.
D) It would be necessary by itself.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that if the world were eternal, it would require no cause, leading to atheism, and they support this with a proof and analogies showing that caused things only need a cause at their beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean to truly acknowledge your utter dependence on the Creator right now, not just at your birth but for your next breath? Will you pause and thank the One who sustains you?
?
The building needs a builder only at its construction, but henna's color persists after the cause is removed. Do you treat your relationship with God as something needed only at the start of your faith journey, expecting your good deeds to self-persist without ongoing connection to Him? Where in your life have you become complacent, assuming your spiritual state can remain without active maintenance from the Source? What step will you take today to renew your reliance on Him?

The Philosophers' Claim: Eternal Effect from Eternal Cause
Razi reports the philosophers' argument for an eternal effect caused by an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' reasoning that an effect eternal in time can be caused by an eternal cause without logical impossibility, based on the distinction between possible and necessary existence.

Core Idea
Co-eternity does not remove the need for a cause; the possible always needs a preponderating factor.

The philosophers (al-hukama') advance a key argument to defend the possibility of an eternal effect from an eternal cause. They grant that both effect and cause are eternal in duration, but they stress a fundamental difference: one is possible by itself, the other necessary. This distinction preserves the need for a cause while rejecting the idea that co-eternity implies self-sufficiency. Let's examine their reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'athar qadim', 'mu'aththir qadim', 'mumkin li-dhatihi', 'wajib li-dhatihi', and 'murajjih'.
The passage is a single, dense argument. Read it twice.
2
Identify the claim and the distinction
The philosophers first assert that an eternal effect causing an eternal cause is not rationally impossible. Then they explain why: the effect is possible in itself and needs a preponderating factor, while the cause is necessary and independent.
Focus on the phrase 'إلا أن أحدهما ممكن لذاته والآخر واجب لذاته'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that if the possibility is established, the opponents' claim of impossibility is refuted. Ask yourself why this refutation works.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: that eternal things are equally independent. How does the passage correct this?
This misconception is explicitly addressed in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الحكماء قالوا: لا يمتنع في العقل كون الأثر القديم معللاً بمؤثر قديم، وهما وإن كانا مشتركين في الدوام، إلا أن أحدهما ممكن لذاته والآخر واجب لذاته. فكان الممكن لذاته محتاجاً إلى المرجح، وكان الواجب لذاته غنياً عن المؤثر. فكان تعليل أحدهما بالآخر أولى من تعليل الآخر بالأول. وإذا ثبت أن هذا المعنى غير ممتنع في الجملة، فقد بطل قول من ادعى أن ذلك محال.
And know that the philosophers said: It is not rationally impossible for an eternal effect to be caused by an eternal cause, even though they share in perpetuity, except that one of them is possible by itself and the other is necessary by itself. So the possible by itself needs the preponderating factor, and the necessary by itself is independent of the effect. Thus, causing one by the other is more appropriate than causing the other by the first. And if it is established that this meaning is not impossible in general, then the claim of whoever asserts that it is impossible is invalidated.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the philosophers (al-hukama') to the opponents' argument. They argue that it is not rationally impossible (la yamtani'u fi al-'aql) for an eternal effect (al-athar al-qadim) to be caused by an eternal cause (mu'aththir qadim). Although both the cause and effect are co-eternal (mushtarikan fi al-dawam), they are fundamentally different: one is possible by itself (mumkin li-dhatihi) and the other is necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi). The possible entity is inherently in need of a preponderating factor (murajjih) to bring it into existence from possibility, whereas the necessary entity is self-sufficient and independent of any cause. Therefore, it is more fitting (awla) that the effect be caused by the necessary being rather than the other way around. Once it is established that this concept is not inherently impossible, the opponents' claim that it is impossible (muhal) is refuted (batala qawl man idda'a anna dhalika muhal).

Key Terms
حكماء
أثر قديم
مؤثر قديم
ممكن لذاته
واجب لذاته
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are both eternal, they must be equally independent and thus neither can be the cause of the other.
Correction: Eternality does not entail necessity; a thing can be eternal yet possible in itself, requiring an external cause. The philosophers distinguish between possible and necessary existence regardless of duration.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers say an eternal effect caused by an eternal cause is not impossible?
A) Because both are eternal and thus share the same nature.
B) Because the effect is necessary by itself and does not need a cause.
C) Because the effect is possible by itself and the cause is necessary, so the need for a preponderating factor remains.
D) Because the cause is possible and the effect is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defend the possibility of an eternal effect from an eternal cause by distinguishing essential possibility from essential necessity, preserving the need for a cause despite co-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are a being that is possible by itself (mumkin li-dhātihi), what does that imply about your need for the Necessary Being every moment? How does this truth reshape the way you start your day and end your night?
?
The philosophers claim that an eternal effect can be caused by an eternal cause without temporal priority. If your very existence is caused by God without any gap in time, does that make you feel more or less dependent on Him? Write down one concrete habit you will change to live in that constant awareness.
?
The passage says the necessary being is 'independent of the effect' (ghaniy ‘an al-mu’aththir). God does not need you at all. How does that uncomfortable truth confront your pride? What are you holding onto that you think makes you valuable to God, and what would it take to let that go and trust only in His sheer bounty?

Objection to Necessary Extension
Objection to necessary extension with hyle/form theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodies are not necessarily extended due to the hyle/form distinction, and why Razi concedes extension but challenges the concept of a specific space.

Core Idea
Extension is not self-evident; even if conceded, 'space' remains undefined.

This lesson presents an objection to the claim that every existing body must be extended (mutahayyiz). The objector invokes a theory distinguishing between a non-extended substrate (hyle) and volumetric extension (form). Razi then grants extension but immediately questions what 'space' really means, setting up a deeper analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the hyle/form theory
Carefully read the passage from 'فإن قيل' to 'وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم'. Notice how the objector uses the distinction between hyle (substrate) and form (extension) to argue that a body need not be extended.
The objector's conclusion is that without extension, the body is not necessarily in a space.
2
Observe Razi's concession and new challenge
Razi responds with 'سلمنا' (we grant) that a body, when existing, is extended. But then he challenges the notion of a 'specific space' (حيز معين) by asking what it means.
This concession is dialectical: he does not refute the objection but shifts the burden to define space.
3
Identify the key question about space
The passage ends with a question: 'فما المراد من هذا الحيز...' This sets up the next lesson's analysis. Reflect on why defining space is crucial for the argument.
The opponent must clarify whether space is a real entity or a logical construct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتحرك . فيثبت : أن الجسم متى كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون ، إما متحركاً ، وإما ساكناً .
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الجسم كلها (١) كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون متحيزاً . وذلك لأن من الناس [ من قال (۲) ] : الحجمية والتحيز صفة قائمة بمحل ، وذلك المحل في نفسه ، ليس بحجم ولا بمتحيز . ويسمون ذلك المحل : بالهيولى. وهذه الحجمية : بالصورة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ وبتقدير أن تكون تلك الذات خالية عن الحجمية ، لم يجب كونها حاصلة في حيز أصلا ؟ وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم .
سلمنا (۳) أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً ، كان متحيزاً . لكن قولكم : وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين ، كلام مبني على تصور أصل الحيز ، فما المراد من هذا الحيز ، الذي جعلتموه ظرفاً لهذا الجسم ؟ وتقريره أن نقول : هذا الشيء المسمى بالحيز ، إما أن يقال : إنه عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وإما أن يكون موجوداً .
فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، امتنع كون الجسم واجب الحصول فيه . لأن جعل المعدوم ظرفاً للموجود محال . وأما إن كان موجوداً ، فإما أن يكون مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس ، أو غير مشار إليه. والأول باطل . لأن المشار إليه ، بحسب الحس إما أن يكون [ كذلك (4) ] بحسب الاستقلال ، أو بحسب التبعية . فإن كان الأول فهو الجسم (۹) فالقول بأن الجسم حاصل في الحيز يكون معناه : أن الجسم حاصل في الجسم. فإن كان المراد من هذه الظرفية : الزمان فحينئذ يلزم تداخل الأجسام، وإن كان المراد منها المماسة ، لزم أن يكون كل جسم متصلا بجسم آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال .
وإن كان الثاني : وهو أن يكون [الحيز (۱) ] مشار إليه بحسب التبعية ، فهذا هو العرض والعرض حاصل في الجسم، فلو كان الجسم حاصلا في العرض ، لزم كون كل واحد منها ظرفاً للآخر ومظروفاً له ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان المسمى بالحيز موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، امتنع حصول الجسم فيه ، بمعنى كونه مظروفاً لذلك الشيء ، وكون ذلك الشيء ظرفاً للجسم ، لأن الجسم موجود مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، وإذا كان المسمى [ بالحيز (۲) ] موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه ، كان كل واحد منهما مبايناً عن الآخر وحينئذ يمتنع كونه ظرفاً له . فهذا هو الكلام في الحيز .
The moving. So it is established that whenever the body exists, it must be either moving or at rest.
If it is said: We do not grant that every body, whenever it exists, must be extended. That is because among people there are those who say: Volumetric extension (al-hajmiyya) and extension (al-tahayyuz) are an attribute subsisting in a substrate, and that substrate in itself is neither a volume nor extended. They call that substrate 'hyle' (al-hayula) and this volumetric extension 'form' (al-sura). So why is it not permissible that the matter be so? And if we suppose that that essence is devoid of volumetric extension, then its being located in a space (hayyiz) would not be necessary at all? And then their argument becomes invalid.
We grant that every body, whenever it exists, is extended. But your statement: 'And everything that is like that must be located in a specific space' is an argument based on conceiving the principle of space (al-hayyiz). So what is meant by this space, which you have made a container for this body? Its exposition is that we say: This thing called space is either said to be pure non-existence and absolute negation, or it is existent.
If the truth is the first, then it is impossible for the body to be necessarily obtaining in it, because making non-existence a container for the existent is absurd. But if it is existent, then it is either perceptible by sense or not. The first is false, because that which is perceptible by sense is either independently or dependently. If it is the first, then it is the body, so the statement that the body is in space would mean that the body is in the body. If by this containment are meant time, then interpenetration of bodies follows; and if contact is meant, then it would require every body to be in contact with another body ad infinitum, which is absurd.
And if the second [case]—that the space is perceptible dependently—then this is the accident, and the accident subsists in the body; so if the body were located in the accident, then each of them would be a container for the other and contained, which is absurd. And if the thing called space is existent and not perceptible by sense, then it is impossible for the body to obtain in it, meaning being contained by that thing and that thing being a container for the body, because the body is existent and perceptible by sense; and if that which is called space is existent and not perceptible, then each of them is distinct from the other, and then it is impossible for it to be a container for it. So this is the discussion on space.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument in which the author (Razi) first asserts that a body must be either moving or at rest, then immediately entertains an objection that not all bodies are necessarily extended (mutahayyiz). The objector cites a theory distinguishing between hyle (matter) and form: the substrate (hyle) is neither a volume nor extended, while volumetric extension (hajmiyya) is an attribute. Thus a body could exist without being in a space. Razi responds by granting ('sallamna') that body is extended when existing, but then attacks the notion of a specific space (hayyiz mu'ayyan). He analyzes space using a dichotomy: either space is pure non-existence or it is existent. If non-existence, a body cannot be 'in' non-existence. If existent, it must be either perceptible by sense or not. The perceptible case is subdivided: if space is independently perceptible, it is itself a body (jism), so saying a body is in space reduces to a body in a body, leading to absurdities of interpenetration or infinite regress of contact. If space is dependently perceptible, it is an accident, and a body cannot be contained by an accident because that would entail mutual containment. If space is existent but not perceptible, it is distinct from the body and cannot serve as a container. Thus the concept of a specific space as a container for body is refuted. The passage ends with 'This is the discussion on space.' The argument shows that the opponent cannot rely on a notion of space without clarifying its nature, and any coherent understanding leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
al-jism (body)
al-mutahayyiz (extended)
al-hayula (hyle)
al-sura (form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can exist without being extended because its substrate (hyle) is non-extended.
Correction: Razi does not directly refute this possibility; he concedes extension but then questions what 'space' means, showing that even if extension is an attribute, the problem of locating the body remains.

Checkpoint
According to the objector in this passage, what is the relation between the substrate (hyle) and volumetric extension?
A) The substrate itself is extended.
B) Volumetric extension is a separate attribute subsisting in the non-extended substrate.
C) The substrate and extension are identical.
D) Volumetric extension is the substrate itself.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses the hyle/form distinction to argue that bodies need not be extended, but Razi will shift the focus to the incoherence of 'space'.
