Analysis of Space: Dichotomy and Refutation
Dichotomous analysis of space and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s refutation of a specific container-space through a fourfold division: non-existence, independently perceptible, dependently perceptible, and non-perceptible.

Core Idea
Every possible definition of 'space' leads to an absurdity.

Razi now systematically examines the concept of 'space' using a dichotomy: is it non-existence or existence? Each branch is analyzed, leading to contradictions such as interpenetration, infinite regress, or mutual containment. This shows that the notion of a specific space as a container for body is incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Follow the initial dichotomy
Read from 'وتقريره أن نقول' to 'فإن كان الحق هو الأول'. Note the two options: space as pure non-existence or as existent.
Non-existence as a container is immediately ruled out.
2
Examine the existence branch
If space is existent, it must be either perceptible by sense or not. Each leads to subcases.
Perceptible space is further divided into independent and dependent.
3
Analyze the perceptible subcases
If space is independently perceptible, it is a body, leading to 'body in body' with interpenetration or infinite contact. If dependently perceptible, it is an accident, leading to mutual containment.
Both are absurd.
4
Consider the imperceptible case
If space is existent but imperceptible, it is distinct from the body and cannot serve as a container.
This also fails.
5
Conclude the refutation
All possible definitions of space lead to contradictions, so the concept of a specific, pre-existing space is incoherent.
The passage ends with 'فهذا هو الكلام في الحيز'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتحرك . فيثبت : أن الجسم متى كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون ، إما متحركاً ، وإما ساكناً .
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الجسم كلها (١) كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون متحيزاً . وذلك لأن من الناس [ من قال (۲) ] : الحجمية والتحيز صفة قائمة بمحل ، وذلك المحل في نفسه ، ليس بحجم ولا بمتحيز . ويسمون ذلك المحل : بالهيولى. وهذه الحجمية : بالصورة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ وبتقدير أن تكون تلك الذات خالية عن الحجمية ، لم يجب كونها حاصلة في حيز أصلا ؟ وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم .
سلمنا (۳) أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً ، كان متحيزاً . لكن قولكم : وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين ، كلام مبني على تصور أصل الحيز ، فما المراد من هذا الحيز ، الذي جعلتموه ظرفاً لهذا الجسم ؟ وتقريره أن نقول : هذا الشيء المسمى بالحيز ، إما أن يقال : إنه عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وإما أن يكون موجوداً .
فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، امتنع كون الجسم واجب الحصول فيه . لأن جعل المعدوم ظرفاً للموجود محال . وأما إن كان موجوداً ، فإما أن يكون مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس ، أو غير مشار إليه. والأول باطل . لأن المشار إليه ، بحسب الحس إما أن يكون [ كذلك (4) ] بحسب الاستقلال ، أو بحسب التبعية . فإن كان الأول فهو الجسم (۹) فالقول بأن الجسم حاصل في الحيز يكون معناه : أن الجسم حاصل في الجسم. فإن كان المراد من هذه الظرفية : الزمان فحينئذ يلزم تداخل الأجسام، وإن كان المراد منها المماسة ، لزم أن يكون كل جسم متصلا بجسم آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال .
وإن كان الثاني : وهو أن يكون [الحيز (۱) ] مشار إليه بحسب التبعية ، فهذا هو العرض والعرض حاصل في الجسم، فلو كان الجسم حاصلا في العرض ، لزم كون كل واحد منها ظرفاً للآخر ومظروفاً له ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان المسمى بالحيز موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، امتنع حصول الجسم فيه ، بمعنى كونه مظروفاً لذلك الشيء ، وكون ذلك الشيء ظرفاً للجسم ، لأن الجسم موجود مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، وإذا كان المسمى [ بالحيز (۲) ] موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه ، كان كل واحد منهما مبايناً عن الآخر وحينئذ يمتنع كونه ظرفاً له . فهذا هو الكلام في الحيز .
The moving. So it is established that whenever the body exists, it must be either moving or at rest.
If it is said: We do not grant that every body, whenever it exists, must be extended. That is because among people there are those who say: Volumetric extension (al-hajmiyya) and extension (al-tahayyuz) are an attribute subsisting in a substrate, and that substrate in itself is neither a volume nor extended. They call that substrate 'hyle' (al-hayula) and this volumetric extension 'form' (al-sura). So why is it not permissible that the matter be so? And if we suppose that that essence is devoid of volumetric extension, then its being located in a space (hayyiz) would not be necessary at all? And then their argument becomes invalid.
We grant that every body, whenever it exists, is extended. But your statement: 'And everything that is like that must be located in a specific space' is an argument based on conceiving the principle of space (al-hayyiz). So what is meant by this space, which you have made a container for this body? Its exposition is that we say: This thing called space is either said to be pure non-existence and absolute negation, or it is existent.
If the truth is the first, then it is impossible for the body to be necessarily obtaining in it, because making non-existence a container for the existent is absurd. But if it is existent, then it is either perceptible by sense or not. The first is false, because that which is perceptible by sense is either independently or dependently. If it is the first, then it is the body, so the statement that the body is in space would mean that the body is in the body. If by this containment are meant time, then interpenetration of bodies follows; and if contact is meant, then it would require every body to be in contact with another body ad infinitum, which is absurd.
And if the second [case]—that the space is perceptible dependently—then this is the accident, and the accident subsists in the body; so if the body were located in the accident, then each of them would be a container for the other and contained, which is absurd. And if the thing called space is existent and not perceptible by sense, then it is impossible for the body to obtain in it, meaning being contained by that thing and that thing being a container for the body, because the body is existent and perceptible by sense; and if that which is called space is existent and not perceptible, then each of them is distinct from the other, and then it is impossible for it to be a container for it. So this is the discussion on space.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument in which the author (Razi) first asserts that a body must be either moving or at rest, then immediately entertains an objection that not all bodies are necessarily extended (mutahayyiz). The objector cites a theory distinguishing between hyle (matter) and form: the substrate (hyle) is neither a volume nor extended, while volumetric extension (hajmiyya) is an attribute. Thus a body could exist without being in a space. Razi responds by granting ('sallamna') that body is extended when existing, but then attacks the notion of a specific space (hayyiz mu'ayyan). He analyzes space using a dichotomy: either space is pure non-existence or it is existent. If non-existence, a body cannot be 'in' non-existence. If existent, it must be either perceptible by sense or not. The perceptible case is subdivided: if space is independently perceptible, it is itself a body (jism), so saying a body is in space reduces to a body in a body, leading to absurdities of interpenetration or infinite regress of contact. If space is dependently perceptible, it is an accident, and a body cannot be contained by an accident because that would entail mutual containment. If space is existent but not perceptible, it is distinct from the body and cannot serve as a container. Thus the concept of a specific space as a container for body is refuted. The passage ends with 'This is the discussion on space.' The argument shows that the opponent cannot rely on a notion of space without clarifying its nature, and any coherent understanding leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
al-hayyiz (space)
al-`adam (non-existence)
al-wujud (existence)
al-mubayana (distinctness)
al-mumassa (contact)
al-tadakhul (interpenetration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage argues that bodies have no spatial location at all.
Correction: The passage refutes the idea of a pre-existing, specific container-space; it does not deny that bodies are located relative to each other (e.g., contact).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if space is considered an independently perceptible existent, what does it become, and what problem follows?
A) It becomes an accident, leading to mutual containment.
B) It becomes a body, leading to interpenetration or infinite regress.
C) It becomes non-existence, making occupancy impossible.
D) It becomes the hyle, making extension unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the notion of a specific container-space by showing that every possible definition leads to a logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument demolishes the opponent's undefined notion of 'space' by demanding a clear definition and showing every alternative fails. In your own understanding of Islamic creed, are there terms you use without precise meaning? What would it cost you to examine them as rigorously as Razi does here?
?
The passage forces you to confront that what seems obvious—'everything is in a space'—may be logically incoherent. Have you ever held a belief about God, the soul, or the afterlife that you assumed was self-evident but never actually tested? Will you write down that belief and honestly check if it holds up to scrutiny?
?
Razi’s method shows that a small logical mistake at the foundation (like assuming a container-space exists) leads to cascade of absurdities. Where in your spiritual life have you built on an assumption without questioning it? Commit today to identify one such assumption and trace its consequences as Razi did.

Refutation of the Necessity of Motion or Rest
Razi refutes the argument that a body must be either moving or at rest using a trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's trilemma argument refuting the claim that a body must always be in motion or at rest.

Core Idea
A body is not forced to be always moving or still; the trilemma shows the 'necessity' claim leads to absurdity.

Razi now engages with a key objection: if space is intelligible, why must a body always be either moving or at rest? He counters by exposing the logical options—a trilemma—each of which is impossible. This passage is a classic exercise in refutation by exhaustive division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become familiar.
Notice the trilemma is introduced with 'إما أن يستلزمهما معاً...'
2
Identify the three possibilities
The argument lists three ways a body could necessarily entail motion or rest: both together, one specifically, one non-specifically. Find each and note how Razi refutes them.
The third possibility is most subtle; it involves non-specific existence.
3
Understand the refutation of each
For each possibility, Razi shows it leads to an impossibility: both together makes a body moving and rest at once; one specifically prevents change; one non-specifically requires a non-specific existent, which cannot exist.
The third refutation relies on the principle that everything in external reality is specific.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since all three possibilities are false, the original claim that a body necessarily entails motion or rest is false. The body can be free from both.
This does not mean a body is never moving or at rest; it means there is no logical necessity for it to be always in one state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن الحيز معقول الماهية. فلم قلتم : إن الجسم لا ينفك عن الحركة والسكون ؟ قوله : ولأن الجسم إما أن يبقى مستقراً في حيزه المعين ، أو ينتقل منه إلى غيره ، قلنا : هذا الدليل معارض بدليل آخر ، وهو أن يقال : لو كان الجسم مستلزماً للحركة والسكون ، لكان إما أن يستلزمهما معاً ، أو يستلزم أحدهما بعينه ، أو يستلزم أحدهما لا بعينه. والكل باطل . فبطل القول بهذا الاستلزام . أما إنه لا يمكن أن يستلزمهما معاً ، فلانه يقتضي كون الجسم الواحد متحركاً ساكناً معاً. وهو محال . وأما إنه لا يمكن أن يستلزم أحدهما بعينه ، فلأنه يلزم أن لا يصير المتحرك ساكناً . وبالعكس . وذلك محال . وإما إنه لا يمكن أن يستلزم أحدهما لا بعينه ، فلأن كل ما كان موجوداً في الأعيان ، فهو في نفسه معين، فإنه من المحال أن يحصل في الوجود : موجود ، لا يكون هو في نفسه معيناً ، بل يكون هو في نفسه ، إما هذا ، وإما ذاك .
وإذا امتنع وجود موجود غير معين في نفسه ، امتنع كون غير المعين لازم للجسم ، لأن كون الشيء لازماً [ لغيره (۳) ] في الأعيان [ فرع على كونه موجوداً في الأعيان ، فيا لا وجود له في الأعيان ، امتنع كونه لازماً (٤)) للجسم الذي هو
We grant that space is intelligible in essence. But why do you say that the body is never free from motion and rest? His statement: 'Because the body either remains stable in its specific space or transfers from it to another'—we say: This proof is opposed by another proof, which is that it be said: If the body entailed motion and rest necessarily, then it would either entail both together, or entail one specifically, or entail one not specifically. And all are false. So the claim of this entailment is false. As for its being impossible that it entails both together, because it would require the single body to be both moving and at rest together, which is absurd. As for its being impossible that it entails one specifically, because it would require that the moving does not become at rest, and vice versa, which is absurd. As for its being impossible that it entails one not specifically, because everything that exists in external things (a'yan) is in itself specific; it is impossible for an existent to occur in existence that is not specific in itself, but rather is in itself either this or that. And if the existence of an existent that is non-specific in itself is impossible, then it is impossible for a non-specific thing to be necessary for the body, because a thing being necessary for another in external things depends on its existence in external things, so if it has no existence in external things, its being necessary for the body is impossible.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a body must always be either in motion or at rest. The author (Razi) first grants ('sallamna') that space is intelligible, then asks why the opponent insists that a body cannot be free from motion or rest. The opponent's proof is: the body either remains in its space or transfers from it. Razi counters with a trilemma: if the body necessarily entailed motion or rest, then the entailment would be either of both simultaneously, or of one specifically, or of one non-specifically. Each option is impossible. Both simultaneously would make a body moving and at rest together. One specifically would make change impossible (e.g., a moving body could never become still). One non-specifically requires a non-specific attribute to exist in external reality, but every existent in the external world (a'yan) is specific; a non-specific attribute cannot exist as an actual property of a body. Therefore, the claimed necessary entailment is false. The passage ends by reinforcing that a non-specific thing cannot be necessary for a body because it would have to exist in external things to be a necessary attribute, and a non-specific existent is impossible. The argument demonstrates that the opponent's assumption leads to logical contradictions, so the body is not necessarily bound to be moving or at rest at every moment.

Key Terms
al-haraka
al-sukun
al-istilzam
mu'ayyan
ghayr mu'ayyan
al-a'yan
al-mustaqirr
al-intiqal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body is always in motion or at rest, and this is a necessary logical truth.
Correction: Razi's argument does not deny that bodies can be moving or at rest; it denies that they are necessarily always in one of those states at all times. The claim of necessity leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility in Razi's trilemma against the necessity of motion or rest?
A) The body entails both motion and rest together.
B) The body entails motion specifically.
C) The body entails one of them non-specifically.
D) The body entails neither motion nor rest.

Key Takeaway
Razi's trilemma refutes the claim that a body necessarily entails motion or rest because each possible form of entailment leads to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you assume you must be either in one state or its opposite (for example, faithful or doubtful, active or passive, at peace or anxious). How does Razi's trilemma challenge the necessity of that binary? What might be a third option that you have been ignoring because it doesn't fit the dichotomy? Write down one specific step you can take this week to explore that overlooked possibility.
?
The argument shows that a non-specific attribute cannot exist in reality. Are there areas in your character where you remain 'non-specific'—refusing to commit to a definite stance or virtue—in order to avoid responsibility? Name one specific quality (like patience, generosity, or truthfulness) where you need to become concrete and intentional. What will you do today to make that quality specific in your actions?

Answers to Three Objections on Body Composition
The author answers three objections about composition, space, and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author systematically refutes three objections about body composition, matter, space, and possibility, and how he uses analogies to clarify his arguments.

Core Idea
The author defends his proof of creation by refuting objections with logical and analogical arguments.

In this passage, the author answers three critical objections to his earlier proof of the temporal origination of bodies. Each objection targets a different aspect: the composition of bodies from hyle and form, the nature of space, and the relationship of possibility to existence and non-existence. By following his responses, you will see how kalam reasoning handles counterarguments with precision and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections and answers
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get an overview of the three questions and answers.
2
Analyze the first answer
Notice the two aspects: the rejection of hyle-form composition and the irrelevance even if accepted.
3
Analyze the second answer
Understand that space is defined as dimension, and note the promise of future detail.
4
Analyze the third answer
Grasp the analogy with number and why it supports the argument about possibility.
5
Reflect on the overall method
Consider how the author uses earlier proofs, definitions, and analogies to refute objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : من وجهين : الأول : إنا بينا في مسألة الهيولى والصورة : أن ذات الجسم لا يمكن أن تكون مركبة من الهيولى والصورة، وأن القول : بإثبات هذه الهيولى : قول باطل . والثاني : إن بتقدير أن يثبت ذلك ، إلا أن الهيولى الخالية عن الجسمية لا تكون جسماً . وإنا إنما حاولنا بهذا الدليل : إثبات حدوث الأجسام ، فلم يكن هذا السؤال وارداً علينا . والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن المكان عندنا عبارة عن البعد ، والكلام فيه سيأتي بالاستقصاء إن شاء الله تعالى . والجواب عن السؤال الثالث : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الممكن ماهية لا تنفك عن مجموع الوجود والعدم، وإن كان قد يعقل انفكاكها عن كل واحد منها بدلاً عن الآخر . فكل ما يقال هناك : فهو قولنا ههنا . وكذلك طبيعة العدد لا تنفك عن الفردية والزوجية ، مع أن طبيعته لا تقتضي أحد هذين الوصفين بعينه . فكذا القول : في هذا الموضع . والله أعلم.
And the answer to the first question: from two aspects: the first: we demonstrated in the issue of hyle and form that the essence of a body cannot be composed of hyle and form, and that the statement affirming this hyle is a false statement. And the second: even if that were established, yet the hyle devoid of corporeality is not a body. And we only intended by this proof to establish the temporal origination of bodies, so this question does not apply to us. And the answer to the second question: space, according to us, is expression for dimension, and the discussion about it will come exhaustively, God willing. And the answer to the third question: we know by necessity that the possible is an essence that does not separate from the totality of existence and non-existence, even if its separation from each one individually in place of the other can be conceived. So whatever is said there, it is our statement here. And likewise, the nature of number does not separate from oddness and evenness, while its nature does not necessitate one of these two attributes specifically. So too is the statement in this place. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a series of answers to three objections raised in a prior discussion about the composition of bodies and related issues. The author refutes each objection systematically. For the first objection, he provides two responses: first, he refers back to his earlier demonstration in the discussion of hyle and form that a body cannot be composed of hyle and form, making the claim invalid. Second, even if hyle were accepted, hyle without corporeality is not a body, so the objection does not affect his proof for the temporal origination of bodies. The second objection concerns the definition of space; the author explains that space is understood as dimension and promises a detailed account later. The third objection deals with the necessity of a possible thing's relationship to existence and non-existence. The author asserts that it is known by necessity that a possible essence cannot be free from the combination of existence and non-existence, though it can be conceived as separate from each individually. He draws an analogy with the nature of number, which cannot be free from oddness and evenness, yet its nature does not necessitate a particular attribute. Thus, the same reasoning applies.

Key Terms
هيولى (hyle)
صورة (form)
مكان (space)
بعد (dimension)
ممكن (possible)
ماهية (essence)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
فردية (oddness)
زوجية (evenness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author's analogy with number implies that possible existence is necessary, but he only illustrates inseparability from a disjunction, not necessity of a particular state.
Correction: The analogy shows that a possible essence always has one of the two states (existence or non-existence), but it does not necessitate which one. Just as the nature of number does not dictate oddness or evenness specifically, the possible does not dictate existence over non-existence or vice versa.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer to the first objection, what is the status of the claim that a body is composed of hyle and form?
A) It is true but irrelevant.
B) It is false because the author already proved that a body cannot be composed of hyle and form.
C) It is accepted for the sake of argument.
D) It is only about celestial bodies.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes each objection by either referring to earlier proofs, clarifying definitions, or using analogies to show that the objections misunderstand the nature of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your belief in a Creator against skeptics, do you find yourself relying on assumptions about the universe's nature (like that it must be made of eternal stuff) without first examining whether those assumptions are logical? How would the author's method of answering objections by going back to first principles challenge you to strengthen your own reasoning?
?
The author uses an analogy with number to show that possibility is not the same as necessity. In your daily life, do you sometimes treat your own possibilities (e.g., what you could achieve, what could happen to you) as if they were necessities, forgetting that God's will is free and your plans are contingent? How does this analogy reframe your reliance on your own expectations?
?
The author refutes the second objection by clarifying the definition of 'space' and deferring further discussion. Are there areas of your faith where you have not taken the time to clarify key concepts (like the nature of God's attributes, or the meaning of qadr), leading you to doubt or confusion? What first step can you take today to seek such clarification?

The First Proof: Past Motions Are Finite
Introduction of the first proof and two of its ten aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first proof that past motions are finite and how the first two aspects of the first premise demonstrate increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Past motions vary in quantity, so they cannot be infinite.

In this passage, the author begins the second chapter by presenting the first of ten proofs that a body cannot be in motion from pre-eternity. The proof is a simple syllogism: past motions are subject to increase and decrease, and anything that is subject to increase and decrease is finite. To establish the first premise, he offers ten aspects; the first two are complete here, using observable astronomical and temporal comparisons to show variation in number.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the overall structure: a statement of the proof, then the beginning of the explanation of the first premise.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the third aspect is incomplete.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Locate the two premises and the conclusion. The proof is: (1) Past motions are subject to increase and decrease. (2) Everything subject to increase and decrease is finite. Therefore, past motions are finite.
3
Examine the first two aspects
The first aspect uses Saturn and the Sun: one Saturn revolution equals 30 Sun revolutions, showing quantitative difference. The second aspect uses years, months, and days to show that numbers of days exceed months, and months exceed years.
Both aspects demonstrate that past motions are not all equal in number, hence subject to increase and decrease.
4
Consider the limitation
Notice that the third aspect is cut off. Reflect on how the author might continue: by dividing a day into smaller parts to further illustrate variation.
This passage is incomplete, but the main idea is clear.

الفصل الثاني : في القامة الدلالة على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متمركا في الأزل . مجموع ما حصلناه من الدلائل المذكورة في هذا الباب عشرة . الحجة الأولى : إن الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. فالحركات الماضية متناهية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . فتقريره من عشرة أوجه . الأول : إنه كلما يدور زحل دورة واحدة ، فقد دارت الشمس ثلاثين دورة ، فوجب القطع بأن عدد دوران الشمس أكثر من عدد دوران زحل بثلاثين مرة . الثاني : لا شك أن السنة الواحدة تكون إثني عشر شهراً ، والشهر الواحد يكون ثلاثين يوماً . فعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ، وهو أكثر من عدد السنين . فيثبت : أن الأحوال الماضية قد دخل فيها الزيادة والنقصان . الثالث : إنا إذا أخذنا اليوم مع ليلته شيئاً واحداً ، فلا شك أنه ينقسم
The second chapter: On establishing the proof that a body cannot be in motion in pre-eternity. The total of what we have collected of the proofs mentioned in this section is ten. The first proof: Past motions are subject to increase and decrease. And everything that is like that is finite. So past motions are finite. As for the explanation of the first premise, which is our statement: past motions are subject to increase and decrease, its exposition is from ten aspects. The first: that whenever Saturn completes one revolution, the Sun has completed thirty revolutions, so it must be decisively concluded that the number of revolutions of the Sun is more than the number of revolutions of Saturn by thirty times. The second: there is no doubt that one year is twelve months, and one month is thirty days. So the number of days is greater than the number of months, and it is greater than the number of years. Thus it is established that past states have entered into increase and decrease. The third: if we take the day with its night as one thing, there is no doubt that it divides...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter aiming to prove that a body cannot have been in motion from pre-eternity. The author announces that there are ten proofs in this section, and he begins with the first proof: past motions are subject to increase and decrease, and whatever is subject to increase and decrease is finite; therefore past motions are finite. He then elaborates on the first premise—that past motions are subject to increase and decrease—by presenting ten aspects. Here he gives the first three. The first aspect uses the example of Saturn and the Sun: because Saturn takes longer to orbit, the number of its revolutions is fewer than the Sun's, showing variation in quantity. The second aspect considers the divisions of time: a year has months, a month has days, so the count of days exceeds months, and months exceed years, again demonstrating increase and decrease among past states. The third aspect begins by taking a day and night as a single unit, stating that it is divisible, but the text is cut off. The author is building a cumulative case that past intervals of motion differ in magnitude, proving they are not all equal and thus are subject to increase and decrease, leading to finitude.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on specific astronomical assumptions that may not be accurate today.
Correction: The proof uses observable patterns (like Saturn's orbit and calendar divisions) merely as examples of quantitative variation; the logical structure does not depend on their exact accuracy but on the general principle that past motions can be compared and show inequality.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof aim to establish about past motions?
A) They are infinite.
B) They are subject to increase and decrease and therefore finite.
C) They are all equal in duration.
D) They are only possible, not actual.

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that past motions vary in quantity, and because any varying magnitude is finite, past motions cannot be infinite—they must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life assuming an endless past, but this proof shows that past motions—including those of your own life—must have had a beginning. How does it change the way you view the span of your existence, knowing that before your first breath there was no motion of yours? Will you now live with the awareness that each moment is counted and finite?
?
The proof uses the simple observation that some motions are more numerous than others. Do you ever treat the days of your life as if they are an infinite resource, wasting time? How will you respond to the fact that every day is a distinct, countable unit that brings you closer to the end? What specific action will you take today to honor the finitude of your time?
?
You see the Sun and Saturn as steady, endless cycles, but this argument shows that even their revolutions are countable and finite. If the grandest celestial motions are not eternal, what does that say about your own small routines? Are you building your life on the assumption that your habits can continue indefinitely? How will this change your planning for your afterlife?

Aspect 1: Saturn and Sun Orbits
First of ten aspects: comparing orbital cycles to show increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the comparison of Saturn's and the Sun's orbits demonstrates that past movements admit increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If two motions have a fixed numerical ratio, they must be finite.

The first aspect uses a concrete astronomical example to illustrate a logical principle. By comparing the orbits of Saturn and the Sun, we see a clear inequality that implies past movements are not infinite but admit increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that the argument is presented as a straightforward comparison.
The ratio 1:30 is used as an example; the key is the existence of a fixed ratio.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument states: whenever Saturn completes one orbit, the Sun completes thirty. Therefore, the number of Sun orbits is greater than Saturn's. This shows a clear difference in quantity.
3
Connect to the main proof
This difference (increase and decrease) is exactly what the first premise of the proof claims. If past movements were infinite, such a fixed ratio would be impossible.
The aspect serves as evidence for the premise that past movements admit increase and decrease.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If both Saturn and Sun had completed an infinite number of orbits, could one be exactly thirty times the other? This shows finitude.

الحجة الأولى : إن الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. فالحركات الماضية متناهية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . فتقريره من عشرة أوجه الأول : إنه كلما يدور زحل دورة واحدة ، فقد دارت الشمس ثلاثين دورة ، فوجب القطع بأن عدد دوران الشمس أكثر من عدد دوران زحل بثلاثين مرة الثاني : لا شك أن السنة الواحدة تكون إثني عشر شهراً ، والشهر الواحد يكون ثلاثين يوماً . فعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ، وهو أكثر من عدد السنين . فيثبت : أن الأحوال الماضية قد دخل فيها الزيادة والنقصان الثالث : إنا إذا أخذنا اليوم مع ليلته شيئاً واحداً ، فلا شك أنه ينقسم إلى ليل ونهار ، فيكون عدد المجموع : نصف عدد مجموع الشهر والليالي وذلك هو المطلوب الرابع : إنه كلما حدث يوم ، فإنه تزاد الجملة الماضية بيوم واحد ، فكان المجموع الحاصل قبل هذا اليوم أنقص من المجموع الحاصل بعد حصول هذا اليوم. وهذه الزيادات والنقصانات حاصلة أبداً . الخامس : إنا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية من هذا اليوم الحاضر إلى الأزل ، جملة واحدة. وأخذناها من أول وقت الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية ، بالمقدار الذي حصل من زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم . فإذا أطبقنا هاتين الجملتين من الجانب الذي يلينا ، فإما أن يمتد من الجانب الأول أبداً من غير حصول التفاوت ، أو لابد وأن يظهر التفاوت. والأول يقتضي أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي أن تكون إحدى الجملتين ناقصة عن الأخرى . السادس : إنا لو قدرنا أنه حدث في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية شيء ، وبقي بحيث لا يغنى البتة . فعلى هذا التقدير لو كانت الدورات الماضية غير متناهية ، لكان مجموع تلك الأشياء المجتمعة في زماننا عدداً غير متناهي ومثاله : أن عند الفلاسفة حدث في كل دورة من الأدوار الماضية أعداد من النفوس الناطقة . ثم قالوا : النفوس الناطقة لا تقبل العدم، فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع النفوس الناطقة الموجودة في هذا اليوم : عدد غير متناه . ثم لا شك أن هذا المجموع قابل للزيادة والنقصان . لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العدد الذي كان حاصلا في زمان الطوفان أقل من العدد الذي حصل الآن
The first proof: Past movements are capable of increase and decrease. And everything that is like that is finite. Thus past movements are finite. As for the explanation of the first premise—our statement 'Past movements are capable of increase and decrease'—its elaboration is from ten aspects. First: Whenever Saturn completes one orbit, the Sun completes thirty orbits, so it is necessarily concluded that the number of the Sun's orbits is greater than the number of Saturn's orbits by thirty times. Second: There is no doubt that one year consists of twelve months, and one month consists of thirty days. Thus the number of days is greater than the number of months, and it is greater than the number of years. So it is established that past states have entered into increase and decrease. Third: If we take the day with its night as one thing, there is no doubt that it divides into night and day, so the number of the total is half the number of the total of the month and nights, and that is what is sought. Fourth: Whenever a day occurs, the past total is increased by one day, so the total obtained before this day is less than the total obtained after the occurrence of this day. And these increases and decreases occur perpetually. Fifth: If we take the past events from this present day to eternity as one total, and take them from the first time of the flood to eternity as another total, there is no doubt that the first total exceeds the second total by the amount that occurred from the time of the flood to this day. Then if we align these two totals from the side facing us, either the first side extends forever without any difference occurring, or the difference must appear. The first would require that the excess be equal to the deficit, which is impossible. The second requires that one of the two totals be deficient with respect to the other. Sixth: If we suppose that in every one of the past cycles something occurred and remained such that it never perishes at all, then on this supposition, if the past cycles were infinite, the sum of those things gathered in our time would be an infinite number. An example of this: According to the philosophers, in every one of the past cycles numbers of rational souls occurred. Then they said: Rational souls do not admit non-existence. On this supposition, it necessarily follows that the sum of the rational souls existing in this day is an infinite number. Then there is no doubt that this sum is capable of increase and decrease, because it is known necessarily that the number that existed at the time of the flood is less than the number that exists now.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first of ten proofs that a body cannot be moving from eternity. The proof argues that past movements are finite because they are capable of increase and decrease. The author first states the proof: past movements admit increase and decrease; anything that admits increase and decrease is finite; therefore past movements are finite. Then he elaborates the first premise—that past movements admit increase and decrease—through ten aspects, of which six are given here. The first aspect compares the orbits of Saturn and the Sun: Saturn's slower orbit means the Sun completes more orbits, showing a definite numerical relationship, so past orbits are countable and thus finite. The second aspect uses the subdivisions of time (years, months, days) to show that past time units are related by increase. The third aspect shows that a day-and-night period divides into two parts, so the total number of such periods is half the total number of parts, implying a finite partition. The fourth aspect notes that each new day adds to the past total, so the total before was less, proving increase and decrease. The fifth aspect compares two intervals: from now to eternity and from the flood to eternity; if both were infinite, aligning them would either imply no difference or a difference; the first leads to an impossible equality of excess and deficit, so the second must hold, meaning one total is less than the other, proving finitude. The sixth aspect uses a hypothetical accumulation of imperishable entities per cycle; if cycles were infinite, the accumulated number would be infinite; but since the number at the flood is less than now, the total is capable of increase and decrease, contradicting the possibility of an infinite actual number. Each aspect reinforces that past movements are not merely conceptually finite but demonstrably subject to increase and decrease, which entails finitude.

Key Terms
زحل (Saturn)
الشمس (Sun)
دورة (orbit/cycle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on the exact orbital period ratio being 1:30.
Correction: The ratio is an example; the logical point is that any fixed ratio between two series implies finitude. The specific numbers are illustrative.

Checkpoint
What does the comparison of Saturn and Sun orbits show about past movements?
A) They are infinite because the Sun orbits more.
B) They admit increase and decrease because the numbers differ.
C) They are unrelated to finitude.
D) Saturn's orbits are more numerous.

Key Takeaway
A fixed numerical ratio between two motions proves that past movements are not infinite but admit increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This simple ratio of 1:30 compels you to admit that the past is finite. Do you live your life as if time is endless, or do you truly believe that every moment is limited? How would your priorities shift today if you fully internalized that the universe—and your own time—has a definite beginning?
?
The argument uses a fixed ratio to prove finiteness. Think of one fixed pattern in your life (e.g., a habit, a relationship, a commitment). Does that pattern rely on the assumption that things continue indefinitely? What would it mean if that pattern were broken by a necessary end?
?
You have just seen that a necessary conclusion can be drawn from a simple observation. What necessary conclusions about your own soul have you been avoiding because they demand a decisive change? Will you act on one of them today?

Aspect 2: Years, Months, and Days
Second aspect: using calendar subdivisions to show increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the subdivisions of time (years, months, days) demonstrate that past time periods admit increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Time subdivisions reveal a hierarchy of quantities that cannot be infinite.

The second aspect uses the familiar structure of years, months, and days to show that past time intervals are related by increase. This undeniable fact supports the premise that past movements are finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical progression: one year = 12 months, one month = 30 days, so days > months > years.
2
Analyze the inequality
The argument shows that the number of days is greater than the number of months, which is greater than the number of years. This hierarchy proves increase and decrease.
This is based on the conventional calendar, which is a human construct but still demonstrates the principle.
3
Relate to the proof's premise
The existence of such inequalities in past time intervals confirms the first premise: past movements (here, units of time) admit increase and decrease.
4
Consider the implication of infinity
If the number of years were infinite, could the number of days be exactly 360 times that? No, because infinite quantities cannot have such proportional relationships.

?

Aspect 3: Day-Night Division
Third aspect: day-night division showing a 1:2 ratio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how taking a day-and-night period as a unit and then dividing it into two parts demonstrates increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If a unit can be split into two unequal parts, the number of such parts is double the number of units.

The third aspect uses a simple mathematical observation: each day-and-night period divides into day and night. This gives a 1:2 ratio between periods and their parts, proving that past quantities can be compared by increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument: A day-with-its-night is one thing; it divides into night and day. So the total number of days+nights is twice the number of full day-night cycles.
2
Understand the ratio
If we let N be the number of day-night cycles, then the number of parts (days + nights) is 2N. This shows that the parts are double the cycles, hence increase.
3
Connect to the premise
This relationship holds for any finite number of cycles, but if the cycles were infinite, the number of parts would also be infinite, and the ratio would break down.
4
Reflect: why does this matter?
The existence of such a fixed ratio (1:2) between two sets of past events proves that they are finite.

الحجة الأولى : إن الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. فالحركات الماضية متناهية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . فتقريره من عشرة أوجه الأول : إنه كلما يدور زحل دورة واحدة ، فقد دارت الشمس ثلاثين دورة ، فوجب القطع بأن عدد دوران الشمس أكثر من عدد دوران زحل بثلاثين مرة الثاني : لا شك أن السنة الواحدة تكون إثني عشر شهراً ، والشهر الواحد يكون ثلاثين يوماً . فعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ، وهو أكثر من عدد السنين . فيثبت : أن الأحوال الماضية قد دخل فيها الزيادة والنقصان الثالث : إنا إذا أخذنا اليوم مع ليلته شيئاً واحداً ، فلا شك أنه ينقسم إلى ليل ونهار ، فيكون عدد المجموع : نصف عدد مجموع الشهر والليالي وذلك هو المطلوب الرابع : إنه كلما حدث يوم ، فإنه تزاد الجملة الماضية بيوم واحد ، فكان المجموع الحاصل قبل هذا اليوم أنقص من المجموع الحاصل بعد حصول هذا اليوم. وهذه الزيادات والنقصانات حاصلة أبداً . الخامس : إنا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية من هذا اليوم الحاضر إلى الأزل ، جملة واحدة. وأخذناها من أول وقت الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية ، بالمقدار الذي حصل من زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم . فإذا أطبقنا هاتين الجملتين من الجانب الذي يلينا ، فإما أن يمتد من الجانب الأول أبداً من غير حصول التفاوت ، أو لابد وأن يظهر التفاوت. والأول يقتضي أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي أن تكون إحدى الجملتين ناقصة عن الأخرى . السادس : إنا لو قدرنا أنه حدث في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية شيء ، وبقي بحيث لا يغنى البتة . فعلى هذا التقدير لو كانت الدورات الماضية غير متناهية ، لكان مجموع تلك الأشياء المجتمعة في زماننا عدداً غير متناهي ومثاله : أن عند الفلاسفة حدث في كل دورة من الأدوار الماضية أعداد من النفوس الناطقة . ثم قالوا : النفوس الناطقة لا تقبل العدم، فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع النفوس الناطقة الموجودة في هذا اليوم : عدد غير متناه . ثم لا شك أن هذا المجموع قابل للزيادة والنقصان . لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العدد الذي كان حاصلا في زمان الطوفان أقل من العدد الذي حصل الآن
The first proof: Past movements are capable of increase and decrease. And everything that is like that is finite. Thus past movements are finite. As for the explanation of the first premise—our statement 'Past movements are capable of increase and decrease'—its elaboration is from ten aspects. First: Whenever Saturn completes one orbit, the Sun completes thirty orbits, so it is necessarily concluded that the number of the Sun's orbits is greater than the number of Saturn's orbits by thirty times. Second: There is no doubt that one year consists of twelve months, and one month consists of thirty days. Thus the number of days is greater than the number of months, and it is greater than the number of years. So it is established that past states have entered into increase and decrease. Third: If we take the day with its night as one thing, there is no doubt that it divides into night and day, so the number of the total is half the number of the total of the month and nights, and that is what is sought. Fourth: Whenever a day occurs, the past total is increased by one day, so the total obtained before this day is less than the total obtained after the occurrence of this day. And these increases and decreases occur perpetually. Fifth: If we take the past events from this present day to eternity as one total, and take them from the first time of the flood to eternity as another total, there is no doubt that the first total exceeds the second total by the amount that occurred from the time of the flood to this day. Then if we align these two totals from the side facing us, either the first side extends forever without any difference occurring, or the difference must appear. The first would require that the excess be equal to the deficit, which is impossible. The second requires that one of the two totals be deficient with respect to the other. Sixth: If we suppose that in every one of the past cycles something occurred and remained such that it never perishes at all, then on this supposition, if the past cycles were infinite, the sum of those things gathered in our time would be an infinite number. An example of this: According to the philosophers, in every one of the past cycles numbers of rational souls occurred. Then they said: Rational souls do not admit non-existence. On this supposition, it necessarily follows that the sum of the rational souls existing in this day is an infinite number. Then there is no doubt that this sum is capable of increase and decrease, because it is known necessarily that the number that existed at the time of the flood is less than the number that exists now.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first of ten proofs that a body cannot be moving from eternity. The proof argues that past movements are finite because they are capable of increase and decrease. The author first states the proof: past movements admit increase and decrease; anything that admits increase and decrease is finite; therefore past movements are finite. Then he elaborates the first premise—that past movements admit increase and decrease—through ten aspects, of which six are given here. The first aspect compares the orbits of Saturn and the Sun: Saturn's slower orbit means the Sun completes more orbits, showing a definite numerical relationship, so past orbits are countable and thus finite. The second aspect uses the subdivisions of time (years, months, days) to show that past time units are related by increase. The third aspect shows that a day-and-night period divides into two parts, so the total number of such periods is half the total number of parts, implying a finite partition. The fourth aspect notes that each new day adds to the past total, so the total before was less, proving increase and decrease. The fifth aspect compares two intervals: from now to eternity and from the flood to eternity; if both were infinite, aligning them would either imply no difference or a difference; the first leads to an impossible equality of excess and deficit, so the second must hold, meaning one total is less than the other, proving finitude. The sixth aspect uses a hypothetical accumulation of imperishable entities per cycle; if cycles were infinite, the accumulated number would be infinite; but since the number at the flood is less than now, the total is capable of increase and decrease, contradicting the possibility of an infinite actual number. Each aspect reinforces that past movements are not merely conceptually finite but demonstrably subject to increase and decrease, which entails finitude.

Key Terms
يوم (day)
ليلة (night)
مجموع (total)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What ratio does the day-night division establish?
A) The number of days equals the number of nights.
B) The number of day-night cycles is half the total of days and nights.
C) The number of nights is greater than days.
D) There is no relationship.

Key Takeaway
Dividing time units into parts reveals a fixed ratio that is only possible if the past is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every day-night cycle you experience brings you closer to your account, how are you using the daylight hours and the night hours differently to prepare for that meeting? What is one night habit you need to change starting tonight?
?
The ratio shows that time is countable and limited. Your own days are numbered. When you woke up this morning, did you act as if this day is a finite gift? What will you do right now to ensure this day is spent in obedience and not heedlessness?

Aspect 4: Daily Increment
Fourth aspect: daily addition to past totals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each new day adds to the past total, proving that past events increase over time.

Core Idea
Every new day makes the past total larger than it was before.

The fourth aspect is straightforward: each passing day increases the total sum of past events. This obvious fact shows that the past is not fixed but grows, meaning it admits increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument: whenever a day occurs, the past total increases by one day. So the total before is less than after.
2
Recognize the logic
This is a direct observation: each new event adds to the count of past events. Therefore, the count changes over time.
3
Apply to infinity
If the past total were infinite, adding one more day would not change it (infinity + 1 = infinity), but we empirically experience increase, so the past must be finite.
4
Summarize the proof
This aspect shows concretely that past movements (days) are capable of increase, fulfilling the first premise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Aspect 5: Comparing Two Intervals to Eternity
Fifth aspect: aligning two intervals to show finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing two intervals (from now to eternity and from the flood to eternity) demonstrates finitude through the impossibility of equal excess and deficit.

Core Idea
If two infinite intervals are aligned, either they are identical or one is shorter; both options imply finitude.

The fifth aspect uses a thought experiment: consider the total past events from today back to eternity, and from the time of the flood back to eternity. Aligning them from the present side reveals a logical contradiction unless the past is finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. The passage describes two totals: A (from today to eternity) and B (from the flood to eternity).
2
Understand the alignment
Align the two totals from the present (our side). Either they extend equally without difference (meaning A and B are the same length) or there is a difference (one is shorter).
3
Analyze the first option
If they extend equally, then the excess (the period from flood to today) must be equal to the deficit (the same period, but on the other side), which is impossible because the excess is positive and the deficit is zero.
The text says 'the first would require that the excess be equal to the deficit, which is impossible.'
4
Conclude from the second option
If there is a difference, then one total is less than the other, meaning they are not both infinite (since infinite quantities cannot differ by a finite amount). Hence, the past must be finite.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes a literal flood event; but it can be any arbitrary event.
Correction: The flood is used as a convenient marker; any finite interval in the past would work.

Checkpoint
What is impossible according to the first option when aligning the two totals?
A) The excess being equal to the deficit.
B) One total being shorter than the other.
C) The totals overlapping completely.
D) The existence of a flood.

Key Takeaway
Comparing two intervals to eternity leads to a contradiction unless the past is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that an infinite past leads to contradiction. Think of one area in your life where you act as if things will continue indefinitely—your health, relationships, or time to repent. How does the finitude of all things challenge you to change something this week?
?
You have just read that aligning two intervals to eternity forces a finite past. Does your daily schedule reflect a belief that your time is limited? What would you do differently if you truly felt that every moment is a finite gift from a Creator?

Aspect 6: Accumulation of Imperishable Entities
Sixth aspect: accumulation of imperishable entities shows finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the accumulation of rational souls (or any imperishable entities) over cycles demonstrates that past cycles must be finite.

Core Idea
If infinite cycles produced imperishable entities, we would have an infinite number today, but we can add to that number, contradicting infinity.

The sixth aspect uses the example of rational souls from the philosophers. If each cycle produces souls that never perish, and cycles were infinite, then the number of souls existing today would be infinite. Yet we can observe that the number at the time of the flood was less than now, proving increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the hypothetical: suppose in every past cycle something imperishable came into existence. If cycles were infinite, the total now would be infinite.
2
Understand the example
The philosophers believed rational souls are imperishable. So if cycles are infinite, the number of souls now would be infinite. But we can compare numbers across time: fewer at flood than now.
3
See the contradiction
An infinite number cannot be increased, but we know the number at flood is less than now, so the number has increased. Therefore, the number is not infinite, meaning cycles are finite.
4
Generalize the principle
Any set of imperishable entities produced over time would behave similarly, proving that the time series must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. فالحركات الماضية متناهية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . فتقريره من عشرة أوجه الأول : إنه كلما يدور زحل دورة واحدة ، فقد دارت الشمس ثلاثين دورة ، فوجب القطع بأن عدد دوران الشمس أكثر من عدد دوران زحل بثلاثين مرة الثاني : لا شك أن السنة الواحدة تكون إثني عشر شهراً ، والشهر الواحد يكون ثلاثين يوماً . فعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ، وهو أكثر من عدد السنين . فيثبت : أن الأحوال الماضية قد دخل فيها الزيادة والنقصان الثالث : إنا إذا أخذنا اليوم مع ليلته شيئاً واحداً ، فلا شك أنه ينقسم إلى ليل ونهار ، فيكون عدد المجموع : نصف عدد مجموع الشهر والليالي وذلك هو المطلوب الرابع : إنه كلما حدث يوم ، فإنه تزاد الجملة الماضية بيوم واحد ، فكان المجموع الحاصل قبل هذا اليوم أنقص من المجموع الحاصل بعد حصول هذا اليوم. وهذه الزيادات والنقصانات حاصلة أبداً . الخامس : إنا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية من هذا اليوم الحاضر إلى الأزل ، جملة واحدة. وأخذناها من أول وقت الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية ، بالمقدار الذي حصل من زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم . فإذا أطبقنا هاتين الجملتين من الجانب الذي يلينا ، فإما أن يمتد من الجانب الأول أبداً من غير حصول التفاوت ، أو لابد وأن يظهر التفاوت. والأول يقتضي أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي أن تكون إحدى الجملتين ناقصة عن الأخرى . السادس : إنا لو قدرنا أنه حدث في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية شيء ، وبقي بحيث لا يغنى البتة . فعلى هذا التقدير لو كانت الدورات الماضية غير متناهية ، لكان مجموع تلك الأشياء المجتمعة في زماننا عدداً غير متناهي ومثاله : أن عند الفلاسفة حدث في كل دورة من الأدوار الماضية أعداد من النفوس الناطقة . ثم قالوا : النفوس الناطقة لا تقبل العدم، فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع النفوس الناطقة الموجودة في هذا اليوم : عدد غير متناه . ثم لا شك أن هذا المجموع قابل للزيادة والنقصان . لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العدد الذي كان حاصلا في زمان الطوفان أقل من العدد الذي حصل الآن
The first proof: Past movements are capable of increase and decrease. And everything that is like that is finite. Thus past movements are finite. As for the explanation of the first premise—our statement 'Past movements are capable of increase and decrease'—its elaboration is from ten aspects. First: Whenever Saturn completes one orbit, the Sun completes thirty orbits, so it is necessarily concluded that the number of the Sun's orbits is greater than the number of Saturn's orbits by thirty times. Second: There is no doubt that one year consists of twelve months, and one month consists of thirty days. Thus the number of days is greater than the number of months, and it is greater than the number of years. So it is established that past states have entered into increase and decrease. Third: If we take the day with its night as one thing, there is no doubt that it divides into night and day, so the number of the total is half the number of the total of the month and nights, and that is what is sought. Fourth: Whenever a day occurs, the past total is increased by one day, so the total obtained before this day is less than the total obtained after the occurrence of this day. And these increases and decreases occur perpetually. Fifth: If we take the past events from this present day to eternity as one total, and take them from the first time of the flood to eternity as another total, there is no doubt that the first total exceeds the second total by the amount that occurred from the time of the flood to this day. Then if we align these two totals from the side facing us, either the first side extends forever without any difference occurring, or the difference must appear. The first would require that the excess be equal to the deficit, which is impossible. The second requires that one of the two totals be deficient with respect to the other. Sixth: If we suppose that in every one of the past cycles something occurred and remained such that it never perishes at all, then on this supposition, if the past cycles were infinite, the sum of those things gathered in our time would be an infinite number. An example of this: According to the philosophers, in every one of the past cycles numbers of rational souls occurred. Then they said: Rational souls do not admit non-existence. On this supposition, it necessarily follows that the sum of the rational souls existing in this day is an infinite number. Then there is no doubt that this sum is capable of increase and decrease, because it is known necessarily that the number that existed at the time of the flood is less than the number that exists now.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first of ten proofs that a body cannot be moving from eternity. The proof argues that past movements are finite because they are capable of increase and decrease. The author first states the proof: past movements admit increase and decrease; anything that admits increase and decrease is finite; therefore past movements are finite. Then he elaborates the first premise—that past movements admit increase and decrease—through ten aspects, of which six are given here. The first aspect compares the orbits of Saturn and the Sun: Saturn's slower orbit means the Sun completes more orbits, showing a definite numerical relationship, so past orbits are countable and thus finite. The second aspect uses the subdivisions of time (years, months, days) to show that past time units are related by increase. The third aspect shows that a day-and-night period divides into two parts, so the total number of such periods is half the total number of parts, implying a finite partition. The fourth aspect notes that each new day adds to the past total, so the total before was less, proving increase and decrease. The fifth aspect compares two intervals: from now to eternity and from the flood to eternity; if both were infinite, aligning them would either imply no difference or a difference; the first leads to an impossible equality of excess and deficit, so the second must hold, meaning one total is less than the other, proving finitude. The sixth aspect uses a hypothetical accumulation of imperishable entities per cycle; if cycles were infinite, the accumulated number would be infinite; but since the number at the flood is less than now, the total is capable of increase and decrease, contradicting the possibility of an infinite actual number. Each aspect reinforces that past movements are not merely conceptually finite but demonstrably subject to increase and decrease, which entails finitude.

Key Terms
دورات (cycles)
النفوس الناطقة (rational souls)
عدم (non-existence/perishing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if you accept the philosophers' claim about rational souls.
Correction: The argument is conditional: if cycles were infinite, then the total would be infinite, but we observe increase, so cycles are finite. The example is illustrative.

Argument 7: Accumulation of Souls
Sanusi's seventh argument using soul accumulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thought experiment of souls accumulating from each past cycle shows that past cycles must be subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If past cycles were infinite, the number of souls would be infinitely greater, proving cycles can change.

The seventh argument uses a simple supposition: if each infinite past cycle produced just one soul that remained, we would now have an infinite number of souls. But since each cycle actually produced many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater. This leads to the conclusion that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease, as they are less than the number of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Allow the author's reasoning to become clear.
Pay attention to the conditional 'If we suppose'.
2
Identify the two cases
Notice the author compares a hypothetical (one soul per cycle) with the actual (many souls per cycle).
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. Ask yourself: why does this follow from the comparison?
The reasoning implies that the number of cycles is less than the number of souls, so cycles can be increased or decreased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة • والثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي. العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The seventh: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles there occurred one soul and it remained, then now there would have occurred numbers without end of souls. Since we know that in every one of the past cycles many souls occurred, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls now existing is infinitely many times greater. This indicates that the number of past cycles is subject to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls. The eighth: The number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd which is above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even which is above it by one. In all cases, past things are subject to increase and decrease. The ninth: Past cycles have a number, then half of that number is less than the whole; so half of it is finite. Its double is a double of a finite, and the double of a finite is finite. The tenth: There is no doubt that for each of the spheres there are particular cycles separately. So if the cycles of the greatest sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of fixed stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of Saturn are infinite, and similarly for the rest, then we say: there is no doubt that the number of all these cycles together is greater than the number of cycles of the greatest sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the sphere of fixed stars alone. This indicates that the number of past states accepts increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (seventh through tenth) supporting the premise that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. The seventh argument uses the accumulation of souls: if each infinite past cycle had one soul remaining, there would be infinitely many souls now; but since each cycle contained many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater, yet the number of past cycles is less than the number of souls, showing increase and decrease. The eighth argument uses parity: if the number of past cycles is even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even; thus it can increase or decrease. The ninth argument: past cycles have a number; half of that number is finite, so its double (the whole) is finite, implying the number is finite and thus subject to increase/decrease. The tenth argument compares cycles of different spheres: the total of all past cycles exceeds the cycles of any single sphere, showing that the number of past states can be increased or decreased.

النفوس الناطقة
الزيادة والنقصان

Correction: The argument shows that if past cycles were infinite, the number of souls would be infinitely greater, which is absurd; therefore cycles must be finite and subject to increase.

Argument 8: Even and Odd
Sanusi's eighth argument using parity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the parity (even/odd) of past cycles demonstrates that they are subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If past cycles are even or odd, they can always be increased by one, showing they are not fixed.

The eighth argument is simple: the number of past cycles is either even or odd. If even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even. In either case, the number can be increased by one, and by symmetry decreased, indicating that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two cases: even and odd.
The argument uses basic properties of integers.
2
Understand the reasoning
If the number is even, it is one less than the following odd. If odd, it is one less than the following even.
3
See the conclusion
Since it can be increased by one, it is subject to increase; by reversal, it can be decreased.
This shows the number is not absolute or infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Argument 9: Half and Double
Sanusi's ninth argument using half and double.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how taking half of the number of past cycles shows it must be finite and thus subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Half of the number of past cycles is finite, so the whole number is finite and can change.

The ninth argument considers the number of past cycles as a quantity. Half of that number is less than the whole and is finite. If the number itself were infinite, half would also be infinite, not finite. Therefore, the number of past cycles must be finite and consequently subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key steps: half is less than whole, half is finite, so whole is finite.
2
Understand the logic
If the number of cycles is N, then half N is less than N. The author asserts half N is finite. Then the double of half N (which is N) is finite.
This relies on the premise that the double of a finite number is finite.
3
Grasp the implication
Since N is finite, it cannot be infinite; therefore it can be increased or decreased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة • والثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي. العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The seventh: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles there occurred one soul and it remained, then now there would have occurred numbers without end of souls. Since we know that in every one of the past cycles many souls occurred, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls now existing is infinitely many times greater. This indicates that the number of past cycles is subject to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls. The eighth: The number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd which is above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even which is above it by one. In all cases, past things are subject to increase and decrease. The ninth: Past cycles have a number, then half of that number is less than the whole; so half of it is finite. Its double is a double of a finite, and the double of a finite is finite. The tenth: There is no doubt that for each of the spheres there are particular cycles separately. So if the cycles of the greatest sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of fixed stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of Saturn are infinite, and similarly for the rest, then we say: there is no doubt that the number of all these cycles together is greater than the number of cycles of the greatest sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the sphere of fixed stars alone. This indicates that the number of past states accepts increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (seventh through tenth) supporting the premise that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. The seventh argument uses the accumulation of souls: if each infinite past cycle had one soul remaining, there would be infinitely many souls now; but since each cycle contained many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater, yet the number of past cycles is less than the number of souls, showing increase and decrease. The eighth argument uses parity: if the number of past cycles is even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even; thus it can increase or decrease. The ninth argument: past cycles have a number; half of that number is finite, so its double (the whole) is finite, implying the number is finite and thus subject to increase/decrease. The tenth argument compares cycles of different spheres: the total of all past cycles exceeds the cycles of any single sphere, showing that the number of past states can be increased or decreased.

Key Terms
ضعف المتناهي
الزيادة والنقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the ninth argument conclude about the number of past cycles?
A) It is infinite.
B) It is half of a finite number.
C) It is finite.
D) It is equal to its double.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument shows that since half of the number of past cycles is finite, the whole number must be finite and thus subject to increase and decrease.

What specific change can you make today to align with the truth that time is not infinite?
?

?
Sanusi's reasoning relies on the principle that a part is less than the whole. In your own life, do you often act as if your time is unlimited? How can you use this logical truth to prioritize what truly matters for the hereafter?

Argument 10: Cycles of Different Spheres
Sanusi's tenth argument using spheres' cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing cycles of different spheres proves that the total number of past cycles can be increased.

Core Idea
The total cycles of all spheres exceed cycles of any single sphere, showing the number of past states can increase.

The tenth argument considers cycles attributed to different spheres: the greatest sphere, sphere of fixed stars, Saturn, etc. If each sphere's cycles were infinite, the sum would be greater than any single infinite set, which is impossible. Therefore, the total number of past states is subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the list of spheres: greatest, fixed stars, Saturn, etc.
2
Understand the comparison
If each sphere has infinite cycles, then the total is greater than the cycles of any one sphere.
This is a contradiction if all are infinite, so the cycles cannot be infinite.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore past cycles are finite and can be increased or decreased.
