The Fifth Argument: Attribute of Blackness and Infinite Regress
The fifth argument: the attribute of blackness and infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth argument uses the concept of 'being-described-ness' (al-mawṣūfiyya) to show that any real distinction between an attribute and its subject leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Every distinct attribute creates a new relation, which itself is an attribute — ad infinitum.

This lesson presents a sophisticated argument from classical kalām that tests the coherence of distinguishing attributes from essences. By examining the case of blackness in a body, the argument reveals that even the state of 'being described by blackness' would itself be an additional attribute, setting off an endless chain. This argument is often used to defend the Ash'arī position that attributes are not separate existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'ذات' (essence), 'موصوفية' (being-described-ness), 'صفة مغايرة' (different attribute), and 'التسلسل' (infinite regress). Understand their meanings in context.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument: premise (conceiving essences separately), first inference (being-described-ness is additional), second inference (that relation itself is an attribute), conclusion (infinite regress).
The argument assumes the distinctness of attribute from essence.
4
Understand the regress
Realize that each new relation requires another relation, leading to an endless chain. This is used as a reductio ad absurdum.
The regress is infinite (tasalsul), a key concept in kalām.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this argument supports the view that attributes are not separate entities but are identical with the essence in some way, as held by the Ash'arī school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذات السواد. بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل ذات كل واحد منها مع ذهول . كون أحدهما موصوفاً بالآخر، فموصوفية الجسم بالسواد: صفة مغايرة لذات الجسم ، ولذات ذلك السواد . وعلى هذا التقدير فموصوفية الجسم بتلك الموصوفية أيضاً زائدة عليه . ولزم التسلسل
And for the essence of blackness. The proof: that it is valid to conceive the essence of each one of them while ignoring that one is described by the other. So the being-described-ness of the body by blackness is an attribute different from the essence of the body and from the essence of that blackness. On this estimation, the being-described-ness of the body by that being-described-ness is also additional to it. And an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents an argument (likely the fifth in a series) demonstrating that if an attribute (like blackness) is considered distinct from its subject (the body), then the relation of that attribute to the subject—the 'being-described-ness'—is itself an additional attribute. This leads to an infinite regress because each new relation would itself require another relation. The argument uses the example of a body being black: the state of 'being described by blackness' is shown to be conceptually distinct from both the body and the blackness, and therefore it must be a third thing. But then the relation of this third thing to the body is another attribute, and so on without end.

Key Terms
ذات
موصوفية
صفة مغايرة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute is not distinct from the essence, but the argument pre-assumes their distinctness to show the regress.
Correction: The argument does not prove that attributes are distinct; rather, it assumes that distinction to demonstrate that it leads to an infinite regress, thus it is used as a reductio ad absurdum against the position that attributes are distinct.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument conclude about the attribute of being described by blackness?
A) It is identical to the essence of the body.
B) It is additional to the body and leads to an infinite regress.
C) It is a merely mental consideration.
D) It proves the eternity of the body.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses the example of blackness to show that any real distinction between an essence and its attribute leads to an endless chain of relations, a classic reductio ad absurdum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's attributes, do you find yourself unconsciously treating them as separate entities in your mind? How does this argument reveal the hidden danger of that habit, and what step can you take today to reaffirm that His attributes are not distinct from His essence?
?
This lesson shows that even a simple concept like 'blackness' can lead to an infinite regress if we insist on real distinction. Are there areas in your own thinking—about yourself, others, or even about God—where you multiply unnecessary distinctions, leading to confusion? Identify one such area and commit to simplifying your perspective back to the core reality.
?
The being-described-ness is itself an attribute, then another, ad infinitum. Reflect on a time you got caught in an endless chain of self-analysis or criticism. How does this argument remind you to stop the regress and return to the simple recognition of what you are, without overcomplicating it?

The Sixth Argument: Evenness and Infinite Regress
An argument that the relation of logical concomitance (لزوم) between four and evenness is additional, causing regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the relation of logical concomitance (luzūm) leads to an infinite regress, and why this challenges the reality of such relations.

Core Idea
If a relation is real, it becomes a new subject requiring another relation, causing infinite regress.

This lesson presents a powerful argument against the reality of relations. Using the example of evenness being a concomitant of four, the argument shows that if the relation itself is considered a distinct entity, then it too must have a relation to its relata, leading to an infinite chain. This argument is a classic tool in kalām to refute the ascription of real existence to relations separate from substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the claim to the regress.
The Arabic has a textual corruption in the second sentence; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument states that the relation (luzūm) between four and evenness is distinct (mukhālif) from both the subject (four) and the concomitant (evenness). Locate where this is stated.
The phrase 'والنسبة بين الأمرين مغايرة لهما' is crucial.
3
Trace the regress
Since the relation itself is a concomitant of the relata, it requires another relation. That second relation is again additional, leading to an endless chain. Understand why this is a problem.
The text says 'فيكون لزوم ذلك اللزوم زائد عليه' – the concomitance of that concomitance is additional.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. Ask yourself: what does this regress prove about the nature of relations?
This argument is used to show that relations cannot be real entities independent of the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إذا قلنا : الزوجية لازم للأربعة . فالمفهوم من للمفهوم من كونه أربعة . ومن كونه زوجاً . بدليل : أن مفهوم اللزوم قد يحصل في سائر المواضع. فإن الفردية لازمة للثلاثة ، وأيضاً : فاللزوم نسبة مخصوصة بين الأربعة وبين الزوجية . والنسبة بين الأمرين مغايرة لهما. فهذا اللزوم أمر مغاير لذات اللازم، ولذات الملزوم ثم إن ذلك المغاير لازم لهما . وإلا بطل اللزوم . فيكون لزوم ذلك اللزوم : زائد عليه . ولزم التسلسل
The sixth: If we say: Evenness is a concomitant (lāzim) of four. Then the concept of [being] four [is distinct from] the concept of being even. The proof: that the concept of concomitance (luzūm) may occur in other cases. For oddness is a concomitant of three. Also: concomitance is a specific relation between four and evenness. And the relation between two things is different from them. So this concomitance is something different from the essence of the concomitant (al-lāzim) and from the essence of the subject (al-malzūm). Then that different thing is a concomitant of them both; otherwise the concomitance would be invalid. So the concomitance of that concomitance is additional to it. And an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, which focuses on the relation of logical concomitance (لزوم), such as evenness being a necessary concomitant of four. The author argues that the relation of concomitance itself is distinct from both the four and the evenness. The proof is that the concept of concomitance can apply in other cases (e.g., oddness and three), indicating it is a universal relation. Since any relation (here, the specific relation between four and evenness) is different from its relata, the concomitance is an additional entity. Now, this entity (the relation) itself must be a concomitant of the relata; otherwise, the relation would not hold. This introduces a second-order concomitance, which again is additional, leading to an infinite regress. The argument thus shows that affirming real relations leads to an infinite chain if one posits them as distinct realities.

Key Terms
الزوجية
اللزوم
المغايرة
التسلسل
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the relation of concomitance is merely a logical notion with no extra-mental reality.
Correction: The argument presupposes, for the sake of refutation, that the relation is real; it then shows that this leads to an infinite regress, thus proving that the relation cannot be real.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what is the relationship between the concomitance (لزوم) and the relata?
A) It is identical to the subject.
B) It is different from both the subject and the concomitant.
C) It is a mental construct with no reality.
D) It is the same as the concomitant.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that if relations were real entities, they would lead to an infinite regress, thus relations are not independently existing things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume that the relationships you have with others and with Allah are just there, solid and real. But what if every relation depends on another relation, and that on another, until it reaches the One who is beyond all relations? Have you ever tried to ground your identity in things that are relative, only to find yourself in an infinite regress of insecurity? Will you today turn to Allah alone, who is not 'related' to anything in a way that requires a further relation?
?
If every real relation leads to a regress, then what does that say about your own attempts to define yourself by your connections—your family, your achievements, your status? Are these not mere concepts that, if taken as real independent things, would never end? Can you let go of the need to see yourself as 'related' to this world and instead see yourself as standing directly before Allah, without intermediary?
?
The argument shows that if concomitance is real, it breeds an endless chain. In your own life, where do you place your trust: in the secondary causes and relationships that are themselves dependent, or in the Creator who is the ultimate ground? What concrete step will you take today to shift your reliance from the relative to the Absolute?

The Seventh Argument: Difference and Infinite Regress
An argument that the relation of difference is additional and entails regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of difference (mukhālafa) is argued to be an additional attribute leading to infinite regress, and why this challenges the opponent's view of real relations.

Core Idea
Difference cannot be a real attribute without causing an endless chain of differences.

This lesson presents the seventh argument from the text, which focuses on the relation of difference between two things. The author argues that if difference is a real attribute, it must itself be different from other essences, leading to an infinite regress. Follow along to see how this logical maneuver works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow.
The passage starts with 'السابع' indicating it is the seventh argument.
2
Identify the key steps
Identify the premises: (1) The concept of blackness being different from motion is distinct from blackness and motion. (2) The concept of difference occurs in other pairs. (3) Therefore, difference must itself be different from other essences, leading to regress.
Trace the 'then' (ثم) and 'so' (فيلزم) to follow the logic.
3
Reflect on the regress
Consider why the author thinks infinite regress is problematic. In kalam, infinite regress is invalid because it entails an endless chain of dependencies.
This argument parallels previous ones about relational attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : المفهوم من كون السواد مخالفاً للحركة ، غير المفهوم من كونه سواداً وحركة ، لأن المفهوم من المخالفة حاصل في غير هاتين الصورتين مثل : السواد والحموضة . ثم إن المفهوم من هذه المخالفة لابد وأن تكون مخالفة لسائر الماهيات . فيلزم منه التسلسل
The seventh: The concept of blackness being different (mukhālif) from motion is different from the concept of it being blackness and motion, because the concept of difference (mukhālafa) occurs in other than these two forms, such as blackness and sourness. Then the concept of this difference must itself be different from the other essences. So an infinite regress follows from it.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which uses the example of difference (مخالفة) between blackness and motion. The author asserts that the concept of blackness being different from motion is not the same as the concepts of blackness and motion themselves. The proof is that the concept of difference applies in other cases (e.g., between blackness and sourness), showing it is a universal. Now, if this difference is a real attribute, then it must itself be different from other essences. That is, the difference itself requires a relation of difference to the things it qualifies, which would be another difference, and so on ad infinitum. This argument parallels the previous ones: any relational attribute, if considered real, generates a regress because the relation itself must be related to its relata.

Key Terms
مخالفة
المفهوم
التسلسل
الماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Difference is merely a conceptual relation and does not require any extra ontological status.
Correction: The argument shows that if difference is considered a real attribute, it leads to regress. Thus, the opponent must either accept the regress or admit difference is not real.

Checkpoint
Why does the seventh argument claim that the concept of difference leads to infinite regress?
A) Because difference is identical to the things it relates.
B) Because the concept of difference is universal and must itself be different from other essences.
C) Because blackness and motion are the only things that can differ.
D) Because difference is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument shows that the concept of difference, if taken as a real attribute, necessarily leads to an infinite regress, undermining the claim that relations are real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to see them as provisional concepts rather than ultimate realities?

The Eighth Argument: Unity Distinct from Essence Leads to Regress
Argument that unity (الوحدة) is distinct from essence, leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why treating unity as a real attribute additional to the essence leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If unity is a distinct attribute, it itself must be one—causing regress.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument from classical Islamic philosophy that challenges the view of unity as a real, distinct attribute. The author shows that unity cannot be simply identified with the essence, but if it is separate, it triggers an infinite regress. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The passage is a complete argument in just a few lines.
2
Identify the claim and proof
The claim is that unity is distinct from essence. The proof uses examples: man can be one or many; one can be man or not. This shows difference.
Focus on the logical relationship: essence vs. unity.
3
Follow the regress inference
If unity is a distinct attribute, when it exists, it must itself be one (otherwise it wouldn't be unity). That requires another unity, and so on—regress follows.
This is the heart of the argument against real attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The regress shows that unity cannot be a real attribute separate from the essence. Consider how this argument fits into broader discussions of divine attributes.
This argument is used by those who deny additional attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إن المعلوم من كون الماهية إنساناً وفرساً ، وسواداً وبياضاً . مغاير للمفهوم من كونها واحدة. بدليل : أن الإنسان قد يكون واحداً ، وقد يكون كثيراً ، كما أن الواحد قد يكون إنساناً ، وقد لا يكون . وإذا كانت الوحدة مغايرة لتلك الماهية المخصوصة ، فعند دخول ماهية الوحدة في الوجود ، لابد وأن تكون واحدة فيلزم التسلسل
The eighth: What is known of the essence being man and horse, and black and white, is different from the concept of it being one. The proof: that man may be one and may be many, just as the one may be man and may not be. And if unity is different from that specific essence, then when the essence of unity enters into existence, it must be one, so an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which focuses on the attribute of unity (الوحدة). The author points out that the concept of an essence (e.g., 'man' or 'horse') is different from the concept of it being 'one'. The proof is that the same essence can be one or many (e.g., one man vs. many men), and the same unity can apply to different essences (e.g., one man or one horse). Therefore, unity is distinct from the essence. Now, if unity is a distinct attribute, then when this attribute exists, it itself must be 'one' (otherwise it wouldn't be a unity). This introduces another unity to make the first unity one, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, treating unity as a real attribute additional to the essence leads to an infinite regress.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الماهية
المغايرة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is simply the essence itself considered without multiplicity.
Correction: The argument shows that if unity were identical to essence, then the essence could not be one or many; but it can be both. Also, if unity is a distinct attribute, it leads to regress, so it cannot be a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth argument, why does treating unity as a distinct attribute lead to infinite regress?
A) Because unity cannot be predicated of itself.
B) Because when unity enters existence, it must itself be one, requiring another unity.
C) Because man and horse cannot share the same unity.
D) Because unity is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The eighth argument demonstrates that unity cannot be a real attribute separate from essence, on pain of infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence as a single person, do you treat 'oneness' as a separate quality added to you? How does this argument force you to reconsider that assumption?
?
If Allah's oneness is not an attribute added to His essence, but simply a negation of multiplicity, how does that change the way you understand the Shahada?
?
Think of a time you attributed 'unity' to something in your worship, like focusing on Allah alone. Did you ever think of that unity as a separate reality? How might this argument purify your tawheed?

The Ninth Argument: Relations and Infinite Regress
Argument that specific relations require further relations, causing regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ninth argument demonstrates that if specific relations are real accidents, then each relation requires another relation, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If relations are real, each relation requires another to link it to its subject.

This lesson presents the ninth argument from the text, which examines specific relations like fatherhood and ownership. The argument shows that if these relations are considered real accidents, then the very fact of their existence in a subject creates another relation, leading to an endless chain. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: listing relations, stating they are accidents, then the crucial move about 'residing in'.
Pay attention to the terms 'إضافات' and 'أعراض'.
2
Identify the specific relations mentioned
The passage lists above-ness, below-ness, fatherhood, sonship, ownership, and being-owned. These are examples of relations that are accidents.
3
Understand the accident claim
These relations are said to be accidents (أعراض) residing in their subjects. For example, above-ness resides in the higher object.
4
Grasp the 'residing in' as a relation
The argument states that the fact that these accidents reside in their subjects is itself a relation between the accident and the subject. This is a key step.
This new relation is of the same type as the original relations.
5
See the infinite regress conclusion
Since that new relation also resides in its subject, it too would require another relation, and so on. This leads to an infinite regress, which is unacceptable.
The argument aims to show the impossibility of real relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : الإضافات المخصوصة ، نحو الفوقية والتحتية والأبوة والبنوة ، والمالكية ، والمملوكية : أعراض حالة في محالها ، ثم أن كونها حالة في تلك المحال : إضافة من تلك الإضافات ، بين تلك المحال . فيلزم أن يكون للإضافة : إضافة أخرى . ولزم التسلسل
The ninth: Specific relations (al-iḍāfāt), such as above-ness and below-ness, fatherhood and sonship, ownership and being-owned: they are accidents residing in their subjects. Then the fact that they reside in those subjects is itself a relation from those relations between those subjects. So it follows that a relation would have another relation. And an infinite regress followed.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument, which examines specific relations (إضافات) like 'above', 'below', 'fatherhood', etc. These are considered accidents that exist in their subjects (e.g., above-ness in the higher object). The author points out that the very fact of these accidents 'residing in' their subjects is itself a relation between the accident and the subject. Since this 'residing-in' is a relation, it too would need a relation to its relata, and so on. Thus, if relations are real entities, each relation requires another relation to connect it to its subject, leading to an infinite regress.

Key Terms
الإضافات
الأعراض
المحال
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relations are merely conceptual and have no real existence; the argument is irrelevant.
Correction: The argument targets those who treat relations as real accidents. By showing that this leads to regress, it challenges their position. It does not assume relations are unreal from the start.

Checkpoint
What does the ninth argument claim about the relation of 'residing in' (كونها حالة) with respect to a relation?
A) It is identical to the relation itself.
B) It is a relation that requires another relation.
C) It is a mere mental consideration.
D) It is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument shows that if relations are real accidents, they lead to an infinite regress because the relation of being-in-subject is itself another relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that if relations were real accidents, they would lead to absurdity. Think of your most cherished relationship—say, being a parent or a child. Do you see it as a mere concept or as something truly existing? How does this logical critique challenge you to examine the reality of your attachments?
?
You likely own possessions that you consider 'yours.' The relation of ownership is given as an example. How attached are you to that relation? Can you see it as ultimately not a real accident, and how might that change your attachment to your belongings?
?
Infinite regress demands a stopping point. In your spiritual journey, do you rely on a chain of intermediaries or do you seek the Necessary Being directly? What step can you take today to ground your existence in the only self-sufficient reality?

Critique of Circular Arguments for Finitude
The author critiques two arguments for finitude, revealing circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the even-odd and half-less-than-whole arguments for the finitude of numbers are circular and thus invalid.

Core Idea
Evenness and halves only apply to finite numbers—using them to prove finitude is circular.

This passage presents a sharp logical critique of two common arguments used to prove the finitude of numbers. The author shows that both the even-odd argument and the half-less-than-whole argument secretly assume the very thing they are trying to prove. By exposing this circularity, the author demonstrates why these arguments fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then check the translation. Notice how the author identifies the hidden assumption of finitude in each argument.
Pay attention to the term 'دور' (circularity).
2
Identify the circular structure in each argument
For the even-odd argument: evenness/oddness presupposes finitude, so using it to prove finitude is circular. For the half argument: having a half presupposes finitude, so using it to prove finitude is circular.
The author explicitly says 'ذلك يوجب الدور' (that necessitates circularity).
3
Reflect on why circular arguments are invalid
Think about why circular reasoning fails to prove anything. A circular argument assumes its conclusion in its premises, so it provides no independent evidence.
This is a fundamental logical fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نختم الكلام في هذه السؤلات بكلام آخر : وهو أنا نقول : دليل الشفع والوتر في غاية الخساسة . وذلك لأن العدد الذي يوصف بكونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، هو العدد المتناهي. فأما بتقدير كون العدد غير متناه ، فإنه يمتنع وصفه بكونه شفعاً أو وتراً . وعلى هذا لا يمكن وصف العدد بكونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، إلا إذا كان متناهياً . وأنتم بينتم كونه متناهياً، على كونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، وذلك يوجب الدور . وهذا أيضاً الاعتراض على قوله : ( إن كل عدد ، فإن نصفه أقل من كله وذلك لأن العدد الذي له نصف، هو العدد المتناهي، فما لم يثبت كون العدد متناهيا ، امتنع أن يفرض له نصف . وأنتم تثبتون كـونه متناهياً ، بالبناء على إثبات النصف له وذلك يوجب الدور ، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير السؤال . والله أعلم
Then we conclude the discussion on these objections with another statement: We say: The proof of the even and the odd is extremely weak. That is because the number which is described as being even or odd is the finite number. As for if the number were infinite, it would be impossible to describe it as even or odd. On this basis, a number can only be described as even or odd if it is finite. And you [opponents] have proven its finitude by [relying on] its being even or odd, and that necessitates circularity. And this also is an objection to his statement: "Every number, its half is less than the whole" because the number which has a half is the finite number. As long as its finitude has not been established, it is impossible to assume it has a half. And you [opponents] establish its finitude by building on the establishment of a half for it, and that necessitates circularity. This completes the statement of the objection. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a critique of two arguments used to prove the finitude of past events (or of numbers). The author argues that the even-odd argument is circular because the concept of evenness and oddness applies only to finite numbers; therefore, using it to prove finitude assumes what it is trying to prove. Similarly, the argument that every number has a half that is less than the whole also presupposes finitude: a number can only have a half if it is finite, so invoking halves to prove finitude is circular. The author concludes that both arguments are invalid and that the objection is successfully established.

Key Terms
شفع
وتر
دور
متناه
غير متناه
نصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The even-odd argument is a valid proof that numbers are finite.
Correction: The argument is circular because evenness/oddness already assumes finitude, so it cannot prove finitude.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the even-odd argument weak?
A) It relies on geometric axioms that are not universally accepted.
B) Evenness and oddness only apply to finite numbers, making the argument circular.
C) It only applies to celestial motion, not to numbers.
D) The argument is actually strong, and the author is misrepresenting it.

Key Takeaway
The even-odd and half-less-than-whole arguments for finitude are circular because they assume the very property they are trying to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself using a circular argument to defend a belief you hold dear? How did you justify it then, and how might you examine it now in light of this logical flaw?
?
Consider one assumption you hold about your own life—for example, about your purpose or worth. Does that assumption rely on itself? What would it mean to test it independently?
?
When you argue for something important, do you check that your premises don't already contain your conclusion? Write down one current conviction and trace its logical support: are any steps circular?

Three Arguments Against Continuous Celestial Motion
Author gives three responses to the continuity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians rationally argue that celestial motion is composed of parts, not a single continuous whole.

Core Idea
Motion's past, future, and changing states prove it has divisible parts.

The objector claims celestial motion is one seamless continuum with no parts. But the author counters with three crisp arguments drawn from the nature of time, change, and observable astronomical phases. Each argument shows that motion necessarily involves multiplicity, thereby refuting the claim of indivisible continuity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer overview
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the structure: the objection, then three responses listed as 'first', 'second', 'third'.
Notice the author uses 'نقول: لنا في الجواب عنه وجوه' to introduce the three aspects.
2
Examine the first argument (past and future parts)
Focus on the first argument: parts of motion are either past (non-existent now) or future (non-existent now). Since their properties differ, motion must have parts. Reflect on why 'both are non-existent in the present' is key.
3
Examine the second argument (change of state)
The second argument defines motion's essence as change from one state to another. Since the starting state and ending state are different, multiplicity is unavoidable.
The word 'ماهية' (quiddity/essence) is central here.
4
Examine the third argument (observable phases)
The third argument uses everyday observation: in each cycle, the planet rises, reaches meridian, and sets. These distinct phases are clear evidence that motion has parts.
This argument appeals to empirical common sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : « الحركة الفلكية شيء واحد متصل ، وليس لها أعداد وأبعاض ) فنقول : لنا في الجواب عنه وجوه : الأول : إن أجزاء الحركة . بعضها ماضية ، وبعضها مستقبلة ، وهما معدومان في الحال ، والجزء الماضي هو الذي وجد وفني ، بعد وجوده . والجزء المستقبل هو الذي لم يوجد ، وسيصير موجوداً ، وإذا اختلفت خواص الأجزاء المفترضة في تلك الحركة إختلافاً على سبيل الوجوب ، وجب الاعتراف بحصول الأجزاء والأبعاض الثاني : إن الحركة ماهيتها التغير من حال إلى حال ، ولا شك أن الحالية المتنقل عنها ، مغايرة للحالة المتنقل إليها . وذلك يوجب التغير والتعدد الثالث : إن في كل دورة ، حصول للكوكب طلوع من المشرق ، ووصول إلى نصف النهار ، وغروب في المغرب . ولا شك أن هذه الأحوال متغايرة متعددة . وحينئذ يحصل الغرض والله أعلم
The answer: As for the first objection – which is his statement: 'Celestial motion is one continuous thing, and it has no numbers or parts' – we say: We have in answer several aspects: First: Some parts of motion are past and some are future, and both are non-existent in the present. The past part is that which has occurred and perished after its existence. The future part is that which has not yet occurred and will become existent. When the properties of the assumed parts in that motion differ necessarily, then the existence of parts and portions must be admitted. Second: The quiddity of motion is change from one state to another, and there is no doubt that the state being departed from is different from the state being arrived at. That necessitates change and multiplicity. Third: In every cycle, there is the occurrence of the planet's rising from the east, reaching the meridian, and setting in the west. There is no doubt that these states are diverse and multiple. Thus the purpose is achieved. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first objection. The objector claimed that celestial motion is a single continuous thing without parts or numbers. The author gives three responses: (1) Motion has past and future parts; both are non-existent in the present, and their properties differ, proving that motion has parts. (2) The essence (mahiyya) of motion is change from one state to another; the initial and final states are different, implying multiplicity. (3) In each cycle, the planet rises, reaches the meridian, and sets; these distinct phases demonstrate that motion is composed of multiple parts. The author concludes that the objection is refuted.

Correction: Motion has temporal parts (past/future), involves change of state, and exhibits observable phases, all proving it is composed of parts.

?

The Sum of Events Exists: First Response
First response to the second objection: sum exists sequentially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the existence of the sum of past events by distinguishing between its occurrence in general and its occurrence collectively.

Core Idea
The sum of events exists sequentially, not all at once.

The objector argues that the sum of past events has no existence, so it cannot increase or decrease. The author begins his answer by showing that the objection confuses two meanings. In this lesson, we will study the first response, which uses a logical distinction between general and specific statements to show that the sum does exist in the sense of sequential occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: the sum of events has no existence, therefore it cannot accept increase or decrease. Recognize that this is a challenge to the possibility of an infinite temporal series.
The objection targets the concept of a collective sum existing all at once.
2
Grasp the distinction
The author distinguishes between two meanings of 'the sum of events has occurred': general occurrence (just that it happened) and specific occurrence (collectively). The specific (collective) is false, but the general is true.
This distinction is key to the response.
3
Examine the analogy
The author gives an analogy: if someone says 'I saw the whole town,' you can ask if they saw them together or individually. The general statement is true regardless of the specific mode.
This shows the general does not depend on the specific mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( مجموع الحوادث لا وجود له البتة ، ومالا وجود له ، يمتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان . . ) فنقول : لنا في الجواب عنه وجوه : الأول : إن قولنا : مجموع الحوادث قد وجد . أعم من قولنا : مجموع الحوادث قد وجد بصفة الاجتماع والدليل عليه : أن القائل إذا قال : رأيت جملة أهل البلد . فإنه يصح أن يقال له : أرأيت جملتهم بصفة الاجتماع أو لا بصفة الاجتماع ؟ وذلك يدل على أن المفهوم من رؤيتهم بجملتهم ، أعم من المفهوم من رؤية جملتهم صفة الاجتماع. فكذلك قولنا : جملة الحوادث الماضية قد وجدت ، أعم من قولنا : إن جملة الحوادث الماضية قد وجدت بصفة الاجتماع. وأنتم إنما أبطلتم هذا المفهوم الخاص. وظاهر : أنه لا يلزم من كذب الخاص ، كذب العام . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من كذب قولنا : جملة الحوادث الماضية وجدت بصفة الاجتماع : كذب قولنا : جملة الحوادث قد وجدت . بل نقول : الدليل على أن جملة الحوادث قد وجدت : أنه يصدق قولنا : إن جملة الحوادث قد وجدت بصفة الافتراق والتعاقب . وقولنا : جملة
And as for the second objection – which is his statement: 'The sum of events has no existence at all, and what has no existence, it is impossible to judge it as accepting increase and decrease' – we say: We have in answer several aspects: First: Our statement 'the sum of events has occurred' is more general than our statement 'the sum of events has occurred in the manner of conjunction.' The evidence is that if someone says 'I saw the whole of the people of the town,' it is valid to be asked: 'Did you see them in the manner of conjunction or not?' That indicates that the concept of seeing them as a whole is more general than the concept of seeing them in the manner of conjunction. Similarly, our statement 'the sum of past events has occurred' is more general than our statement 'the sum of past events has occurred in the manner of conjunction.' You [opponents] have only invalidated this specific concept. It is clear that the falsity of the specific does not necessitate the falsity of the general. So it is established that the falsity of our statement 'the sum of past events occurred in the manner of conjunction' does not necessitate the falsity of our statement 'the sum of events has occurred.' Rather, we say: The evidence that the sum of events has occurred is that our statement 'the sum of events has occurred in the manner of separation and succession' is true. And our statement: 'the whole' [unfinished].
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the answer to the second objection. The objector claimed that the sum of all past events has no collective existence, so it cannot be described as increasing or decreasing. The author's first response distinguishes two meanings of the phrase 'the sum of events has occurred': (a) the sum has occurred simply, and (b) it has occurred collectively (بصفة الاجتماع). The latter is false because events do not exist simultaneously. However, the former is true because events occur sequentially (بصفة الافتراق والتعاقب). The author uses an analogy: seeing the whole town can mean seeing individuals separately or together; the question only invalidates the collective sense. Thus, the objection does not disprove the existence of the sum in the sequential sense. The argument is incomplete in the OCR, but the first response is fully presented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sum of past events must exist all at once to be real.
Correction: The sum exists sequentially (in succession), not collectively. Its existence does not require simultaneous presence.

Checkpoint

B) Because the phrase 'the sum has occurred' is more general than 'occurred collectively'.
C) Because increase and decrease apply only to non-existent things.
D) Because the objector's statement is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The sum of past events exists in the sense of sequential occurrence, and the objection only disproves collective existence, not the general fact of occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back at your life—the sum of your actions, thoughts, and feelings—do you see them as a chaotic heap or as a meaningful sequence? If the sum exists only sequentially, what does that imply about the importance of each present moment? Is there a moment you've been trying to re-live or undo that you need to accept as part of the sequence?
?
The objection originally claimed the sum of events has no existence at all. Have you ever felt that your past mistakes have no real existence anymore, that they are 'gone' and thus irrelevant? Yet this lesson says they do exist—sequentially. How does the reality that every past event truly exists (though not simultaneously) affect your sense of accountability before Allah? What past event do you need to acknowledge as still 'present' in the sequence of your life?
?
If the sum of your deeds exists sequentially, then your entire life is like a chain. Is there a weak link you've been neglecting—a sin you keep repeating, a duty you keep delaying? The sum will continue to increase. What will you change today so that the sum of your life, when completed, is one that you would be pleased to present to your Lord? What step can you take right now to improve that chain?

Second Objection: Refuting the Impossibility of Events in Pre-eternity
The author refutes the objection that proofs imply impossibility of events in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the proofs imply the impossibility of events in pre-eternity is logically flawed, and how the two refutation arguments work.

Core Idea
Changing essential impossibility is impossible—and any first moment of possibility leads to infinite regress.

In this passage, the objector argues that if the previous proofs were sound, they would make events impossible in pre-eternity. The author refutes this by showing that such a claim would require impossible changes in essences and an infinite regress of 'first' moments. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, denial, two reasons.
The passage is compact; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons why the objector's claim leads to impossibility. Find them in the text: the first about essential attributes, the second about the first moment of validity.
Reason 1 uses 'ذاتي' (essential); Reason 2 uses 'أول' (first).
3
Understand why the refutation works
Understand why these two reasons are valid objections: essential attributes cannot change, and every supposed first moment would already have priority.
This refutes the objection without denying the original proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن دلائلكم إن صحت ، لزم منها القول بامتناع حدوث الحادث في الأزل . فنقول : هذا باطل . لأنه لا شك أن حدوث الحوادث ممكن في لا يزال . فلو فرضنا : أنها كانت ممتنعة الحصول في الأزل ، ثم صارت ممكنة الحصول في لا يزال ، وجب أن يكون لتلك الإمكانات أول وبداية . إلا أن ذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : يلزم أن يقال : إن هذه الماهيات انقلبت من الامتناع الذاتي ، إلى الإمكان الذاتي . وهو محال . والثاني : وهو أن كل وقت يفرض كونه أولاً لصحة حدوث الحوادث ، فقد كانت الصحة حاصلة قبله . لأن بتقدير أن تحدث الحوادث قبل ذلك الوقت بيومين فقط ، لا يصير قديماً أزلياً. فيثبت : أن دلائلكم توجب إثبات أول لصحة حدوث الحوادث. وثبت : أن إثبات هذه الأولية باطل ، فكانت هذه الدلائل باطلة
The second question: If your proofs are sound, they would necessitate the assertion that the occurrence of an event in pre-eternity is impossible. We say: This is false. For there is no doubt that the occurrence of events is possible in the eternal future (la yazal). If we suppose that they were impossible in pre-eternity, then became possible in the eternal future, it would be necessary for those possibilities to have a first and a beginning. However, that is impossible for two reasons: First, it would necessitate saying that these essences changed from essential impossibility to essential possibility, and that is impossible. Second, that every time supposed to be the first for the validity of the occurrence of events, validity was present before it. For if events were to occur only two days before that time, they would not become pre-eternal and eternal. Thus it is established that your proofs necessitate establishing a first for the validity of the occurrence of events. And it is established that establishing this firstness is invalid, so these proofs are invalid.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is the second objection and its refutation. The objector argues that if the three proofs are correct, they would imply that events cannot occur in pre-eternity at all. The author refutes this by pointing out that events are certainly possible in the future (la yazal). If they were impossible in the past (al-azal) and then became possible, there would have to be a first moment when they became possible. But this leads to two absurdities: (1) it would mean essences change from essential impossibility to essential possibility, which is impossible because essential attributes do not change; (2) for any time supposed to be the first when validity obtains, validity would have already been present before that time, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, the objection fails and the proofs are not invalidated.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
امتناع (imtina')
ذاتي (dhati)
لا يزال (la yazal)
أول (awwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that the proofs imply events cannot occur in pre-eternity.
Correction: The author shows this would require impossible changes in essential attributes and an infinite regress, so the objection does not invalidate the proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the second objection leads to an impossibility?
A) Because it implies that possibilities have no beginning.
B) Because it requires that essential attributes change.
C) Because it contradicts the definition of pre-eternity.
D) Because it assumes time is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The objection that proofs imply impossibility in pre-eternity fails because it would require changing essential attributes and an infinite regress of first moments, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's eternity, do you unconsciously imagine it as an endlessly extended timeline? This lesson shows that any 'first moment' leads to absurdity. How will you train your mind to think of God's timelessness without temporal language?
?
The argument here turns on the impossibility of essential attributes changing. Have you ever assumed that your own character flaws are 'essential' and unchangeable? If essential impossibility cannot become possibility, how does that challenge your view of repentance and divine transformation?

Third Objection: Applying the Reasoning to Post-Eternity
The author applies the opponent's reasoning about pre-eternity to post-eternity to show its absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author applies the same reasoning used against pre-eternity to post-eternity, revealing a symmetric absurdity.

Core Idea
Symmetry forces consistency: if the past must have a first, the future must have a last.

In this objection, the author turns the tables on the opponent by showing that their own argument against infinite past events would equally demand an end to future events. By applying each of the three proofs to the direction of the future, he reveals a parallel absurdity that the opponent cannot avoid. This lesson walks through each of those three applications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the opponent's three proofs
Before engaging with the objection, recall the three proofs the opponent used to argue that events must have a first in pre-eternity. These involved motion, a dichotomy about whether anything occurred in pre-eternity, and the necessity of an endless nonexistence after each event.
2
Apply the first aspect to post-eternity
The author applies the motion proof to post-eternity: just as motion requires being preceded by another part, it also requires being succeeded. Since post-eternity (abad) precludes succession, motion cannot continue forever.
Note the parallel: 'masbuqiyya' (preceded) becomes 'malhuqiyya' (succeeded).
3
Apply the second aspect to post-eternity
The author applies the dichotomy: either nothing occurs in post-eternity or something does. If something does, does something occur after it? Both options lead to a last event, which is absurd because post-eternity has no end.
4
Apply the third aspect to post-eternity
The author applies the third proof: since each event is followed by a nonexistence without end, the total must have a last. This mirrors the original argument about pre-eternity.
The passage is incomplete here, but the intended symmetry is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : أن نقول : هذه الدلائل الثلاثة ، التي ذكرتموها في امتناع حدوث الحوادث في جانب الأزل ، ووجوب انتهائها إلى أول ، يوجب عليكم امتناع حدوث الحوادث في الأبد، ووجوب انتهائها إلى آخر . ولنقرر ذلك في كل واحد منها : فأما الوجه الأول : من هذه الوجوه فهو أنكم قلتم : حقيقة الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، وحقيقة الأزل تنافي المسبوقية بالغير ، والجمع بينهما محال فوجب انتهاء الحوادث إلى أول فنقول: كما أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحركة ، يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بجزء آخر ، فكذلك يجب أن يكون ملحوقاً بجزء آخر ، إذ لو بقي ذلك الجزء لانقلب سكوناً . وكلامنا فيما إذا كانت الحركة باقية . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كما أن حقيقة الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير ، فكذلك تقتضي الملحوقية بالغير، وحقيقة الأبد تنافي الملحوقية بالغير ، والجمع بين الحركة وبين الأبد : محال . فوجب انتهاء الحركات في جانب الأبد ، إلى آخر . وكما أن هذا الكلام فاسد ، فكذا ما ذكرتموه وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قولكم : هل حصل في الأزل شيء من الحوادث أم لا ؟ فقلنا في جانب الأبد : فإن لم يحصل فيه شيء من الحوادث ، فللحوادث آخر على سبيل الوجوب . وإن حصل في الأبد شيء من الحوادث ، فهل حصل عقيبه شيء آخر ، أم لا ؟ فإن كان الأول فقد حصل بعد الأبد شيء آخر ، وذلك محال . لأن الأبد ينافي الملحوقية بالغير ، وما حصل بعده شيء اخر فهو ملحوق بالغير ، والجمع بينهما محال . وإن كان الثاني : وهو أنه لم يحصل بعد ذلك الحادث شيء آخر ، فحينئذ يكون ذلك الشيء آخراً لكل الحوادث ، فيكون لكل الحوادث آخر على سبيل الوجوب . وذلك محال . وأما الوجه الثالث من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة : فهو أن نقول : لما وجب في كل واحد من الحوادث، أن يحصل بعده : عدم لا آخر له ، كان مجموع
The third question: We say: These three proofs that you mentioned concerning the impossibility of the occurrence of events in the direction of pre-eternity and the necessity of their ending with a first require you to hold the impossibility of the occurrence of events in post-eternity and the necessity of their ending with a last. And we will establish that for each one of them: As for the first aspect: of these aspects, it is that you said: The reality of motion requires being preceded by another, and the reality of pre-eternity is incompatible with being preceded by another, and combining them is impossible, so the ending of events at a first is necessary. We say: Just as every part of the parts of motion must be preceded by another part, so too it must be succeeded by another part, since if that part remained, it would turn into rest. And our discussion is when motion is continuous. Once you understand this, we say: Just as the reality of motion requires being preceded by another, so too it requires being succeeded by another, and the reality of post-eternity is incompatible with being succeeded by another, and the combination between motion and post-eternity is impossible. So the termination of motions in the direction of post-eternity to a last is necessary. And just as this statement is corrupt, so too is what you mentioned. As for the second aspect: And it is your statement: Did anything of events occur in pre-eternity or not? So we say concerning post-eternity: If nothing of events occurred in it, then events have a last necessarily. And if something of events occurred in post-eternity, then did something else occur after it or not? If the first, then something else occurred after post-eternity, and that is impossible. Because post-eternity is incompatible with being succeeded by another, and what occurs after it is something else is succeeded by another, and combining them is impossible. And if the second: that nothing else occurred after that event, then that thing is a last for all events, so all events have a last necessarily. And that is impossible. As for the third aspect of these three aspects: It is that we say: Since it is necessary for every one of the events that after it occurs a nonexistence that has no end, then the total...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which applies the same reasoning used for pre-eternity (azal) to post-eternity (abad). The objector argues that if the proofs necessitate a first for events in the past, then by symmetry they would necessitate a last for events in the future, which is also impossible. The author then examines each of the three original proofs in turn, showing how they would apply to the future direction. The first aspect concerns motion: just as motion requires being preceded, it also requires being succeeded; but post-eternity is incompatible with being succeeded, so motion must end. The second aspect uses the same dichotomy as the original proof: either nothing occurs in post-eternity or something does; both lead to a last event. The third aspect, though incomplete, seems to argue that since each event is followed by a nonexistence without end, the sum would require a total. The author concludes that if this reasoning is corrupt for the future, it is equally corrupt for the past.

Key Terms
أبد (abad)
مسبوقية (masbuqiyya)
ملحوقية (malhuqiyya)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims that the future is actually finite and will end.
Correction: The objection is not asserting finitude of the future; it is showing that the opponent's argument, if valid, would force an absurd consequence.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the third objection's first aspect regarding motion?
A) Motion requires being preceded but not succeeded.
B) Motion cannot exist in post-eternity because it would require being succeeded.
C) Motion is possible in post-eternity because it is not subject to the same constraints.
D) The argument for the past does not apply to the future.

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that the opponent's reasoning, if consistent, would force an equally absurd conclusion about the future as it does about the past.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you hold a double standard—applying a certain logic to your past but refusing to apply it to your future? Will you commit to consistency today?
?
The objector forces you to see that if you accept a premise for pre-eternity, you must accept it for post-eternity. What belief about your past mistakes are you refusing to extend to your future failures? How can embracing that consistency free you from self-deception?
?
The argument reveals that avoiding absurdity requires either rejecting the initial premise or accepting both consequences. What uncomfortable truth about your spiritual journey have you been avoiding because accepting it would require a change in how you view your future? Will you accept it now?

Refuting the Three Proofs: Their Logic Implies an End
The author refutes the three proofs by showing they imply an end for events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the three proofs by showing that the same reasoning would force an end to events, and why this reveals a fundamental misconception about eternity.

Core Idea
The same logic that forces a beginning also forces an end—but events have no end.

In this passage, the author takes the opponent's own three proofs and turns them against them. He shows that if these proofs were valid, they would not only prove a beginning but also an end for temporal events. Since events do not have an end—as both sides agree—the proofs must be flawed. The key is recognizing that pre-eternity (al-azal) and perpetual eternity (al-abad) are not points in time but unbounded durations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before breaking it down.
Notice the author uses a dilemma structure: either X or Y.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities regarding non-existences in perpetual eternity. Identify what they are and label them as 'first horn' and 'second horn'.
The dilemma concerns whether anything from existents occurs in al-abad.
3
Trace the consequences of each horn
Follow the logical outcome of each possibility. The first leads to impossible coexistence of prior and posterior; the second forces an end to events.
Both horns lead to absurdity from the opponent's perspective.
4
Understand the conclusion and the root error
The author concludes that the proofs are fallacies because they would also prove an end, which is false. He identifies the cause: treating al-azal and al-abad as specific times.
This is a crucial insight: eternity cannot be treated as a point on a timeline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه العدمات حاصل في الأبد، فإما أن يحصل في الأبد شيء من الموجودات أو لم يحصل . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يحصل السابق والمسبوق معاً ، وهو محال وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لم يحصل في الأبد شيء من الحوادث ، فوجب أن يحصل للحوادث آخر ونهاية . فيثبت : أن هذه الدلائل الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها إن أوجبت أن يحصل للحوادث أول ، فكذلك أوجبت أن يحصل للحوادث آخر . ولما بطل ذلك ، علمنا أن هذه الدلائل مغالطات، نشأت من اعتقاد : أن الأزل وقت معين في الماضي ، وأن الأبد وقت معين في المستقبل . ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، كانت هذه الدلائل التي ذكرتموها فاسدة .
These non-existences occur in perpetual eternity (الأبد). Either something from the existents occurs in perpetual eternity, or it does not occur. If it is the first, then the prior and the posterior would have to occur together, which is impossible. And if it is the second, then at that time nothing of the originated things (الحوادث) occurs in perpetual eternity, so it becomes necessary that originated things have an end and a termination. Thus it is established: that these three proofs which you mentioned, if they necessitate that originated things have a beginning, likewise they necessitate that originated things have an end. And since that is false, we know that these proofs are fallacies, arising from the belief that pre-eternity (الأزل) is a specific time in the past, and that perpetual eternity (الأبد) is a specific time in the future. And since that is false, these proofs which you mentioned are corrupt.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author argues against an opponent who has presented three proofs that claim to establish a beginning for events. The refutation proceeds by applying the same logic to the future (الأبد) as was applied to the past (الأزل). The reasoning: these non-existences occur in perpetual eternity. Then a dilemma is posed: either something from existents occurs in that perpetual eternity, or nothing occurs. If something occurs, the prior and posterior would coexist, which is impossible. If nothing occurs, events must have an end. Thus the proofs that forced a beginning would also force an end, but since events do not have an end, the proofs are invalid. They are fallacies stemming from the mistaken belief that pre-eternity and perpetual eternity are specific points in time, rather than unbounded durations.

Correction: They are unbounded durations without endpoints; neither is a specific time.

Answer to the First Objection: The Third Proof Explained
The author answers the first objection by explaining the third proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third proof shows that every event is preceded by non-existence without requiring the assumption that al-azal is a specific time.

Core Idea
The third proof works by hypothetically treating non-existences as eternal things to demonstrate logical necessity.

The first objection claimed that the earlier proofs only work if you imagine al-azal as a specific point in time. The author responds by clarifying the third proof, which does not rely on that assumption. He uses a hypothetical scenario where the non-existences preceding events are considered eternal things, leading to a contradiction if an event coexists with its own non-existence. This establishes the proof without needing to conceive of al-azal as a moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the objection to the detailed explanation.
The author begins by dismissing the objection and then provides the third proof.
2
Understand the hypothetical
The key move is the supposition: if the non-existences preceding events were considered existent things, they would be eternal. Grasp why this is the starting point.
This is a mental exercise to illustrate the proof.
3
Follow the deduction
From the hypothetical, deduce that these eternal non-existences must be simultaneous in their locus. Then see why no originated thing can coexist with them—because that would require existence and non-existence to coincide.
The impossibility of contradiction reinforces the proof.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The proof concludes that each event is necessarily preceded by non-existence, regardless of imagining al-azal as a time. Reflect on how this answers the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : هذا الخيال إنما وقع لتوهم كون الأزل وقتاً معيناً ، قلنا : إنه لا حاجة في تقرير هذه الدلائل إلى ما ذكرتموه . وبيانه في الدليل الثالث أن نقول : إن كل واحد من الحوادث ، مسبوق بعدم لا أول له . فلو قدرنا كل واحد من تلك العدمات شيئاً موجوداً ، لكان كل واحد من تلك الأشياء قديماً. وتلك القدماء لا بد وأن تكن متقارنة الوجود في حيزها، وإلا لكان حصول بعضها بعد الغير. وذلك يقدح في كونها قدماء . وإذا كانت تلك الأشياء متقارنة في الوجود ، امتنع أن يحصل معها شيء من الحوادث . لأن أحد الأمور الحاصلة عند تلك المقارنة ، وهو عدم هذا الحادث. فلو حصل وجود هذا الحادث معه، لكان وجود الشيء مقارناً لعدمه. وإنه محال . فيثبت : أنا متى فرضنا أن لكل واحد منها عدماً سابقاً عليه ، لا أول له وجب الحكم بكون كلها مسبوقاً بالعدم .
And the answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: 'This imagination only occurred from the supposition that al-azal is a specific time,' we say: There is no need in establishing these proofs for what you mentioned. And its explanation is in the third proof, that we say: Every single one of the originated things is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning. If we were to consider every one of those non-existences as something existent, then every one of those things would be eternal (قديم). And those eternal things must be simultaneous in existence in their locus (في حيزها), otherwise the occurrence of some after others would undermine their being eternal. And if those things are simultaneous in existence, it is impossible that anything of the originated things occurs with them. Because one of the things occurring at that simultaneity is the non-existence of this originated thing. So if the existence of this originated thing occurred with it, the existence of a thing would coincide with its non-existence, which is impossible. Thus it is established that whenever we suppose for each of them a non-existence preceding it without beginning, it becomes necessary to judge that each of them is preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first objection. The opponent had claimed that the proofs of the author only work if one imagines al-azal as a specific point in time. The author responds that this is not needed and explains using the third proof. The third proof assumes each event is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning. If we reify those non-existences as actual things, each would be eternal. Eternal things must be simultaneous in their locus, otherwise they would have priority over each other, contradicting their eternity. If they are simultaneous, no originated thing can coexist with them because among the things present in that simultaneity is the very non-existence of that originated thing. If the originated thing existed alongside that non-existence, existence and non-existence would coincide, which is impossible. Therefore, each event must be preceded by non-existence, preserving the proof without assuming al-azal is a specific time.

Correction: The proof is a hypothetical (لو قدرنا) to illustrate the logical implication; it does not assert that non-existences are actually existent.

Beginningless Possibility Does Not Imply Pre-Eternity
The author answers the second objection about the possibility of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the beginningless possibility of events implies their actual occurrence in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Beginningless possibility does not entail actual pre-eternal occurrence.

In answer to the second objection, the author concedes the beginninglessness of possibility but denies this forces pre-eternity. He then provides a proof showing that an existent conditioned by temporal precedence cannot have its possibility confined to any first moment, yet that does not make it eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and its translation. Notice the author's concession and the structure of his proof.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the logic presented.
2
Understand the key distinction
Identify the difference between the possibility of an event (which has no beginning) and the actual event itself (which cannot be eternal because it is preceded by non-existence).
The author uses the condition 'being preceded by non-existence with temporal precedence' to block the inference to pre-eternity.
3
Grasp the proof's logic
Follow the reasoning: taking a possible existent conditioned by temporal precedence, its possibility cannot have a first moment. Yet, this does not make it eternal, because the eternal is defined as not having a beginning—but here the condition of temporal precedence still applies.
The eternal (أزلي) is that which has no beginning; the possible existent under consideration does have a beginning in actuality, even if its possibility is beginningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو المعارضة بالصحة . فنقول : نحن نسلم أنه لا بداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث. ومع هذا فإنه لا يلزم صحة حدوثها في الأزل . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا أخذنا موجوداً ممكن الوجود ، بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، سبقاً زمانياً . فهذا الشيء بهذا الشرط ، لا أول لإمكان وجوده . لأنه لا وقت يجعل أولاً لصحة وجوده ، إلا وتلك الصحة كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت . ثم مع هذا لا يلزم صحة كونه أزلياً ، لأن الأزلي هو الذي لا يكون
And as for the second question: it is the contention by possibility. We say: We concede that there is no beginning for the possibility of the origination of events. Yet despite this, it does not necessitate the possibility of their occurrence in pre-eternity (الأزل). And the proof for it: that if we take an existent that is possible of existence, conditioned by being preceded by non-existence, with temporal precedence, then this thing with this condition has no first for the possibility of its existence. Because there is no time that can be made the first for the possibility of its existence, except that that possibility was already present before that time. Then despite this, it does not necessitate the possibility of it being eternal, because the eternal is that which does not exist... (the text breaks off).
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which argues that since events are possible, they must have always been possible, so there is no beginning to their possibility, thus they could be eternal. The author responds by conceding that the possibility of events has no beginning, but that does not entail that events actually occur in pre-eternity. The proof: consider an existent that is possible and conditioned by temporal priority. Such a thing, by this condition, has no first moment for its possibility: for any candidate first time, the possibility must have existed before it. However, this does not mean the thing could be eternal (أزلي), because the eternal is defined as that which does not have beginning (or possibly 'that which does not exist' — the text is cut). The key point is that the condition of being preceded by non-existence prevents it from being eternal, even if its possibility is beginningless.

Correction: The author shows that even if possibility is beginningless, the event itself cannot be eternal because it is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence. The eternal is that which has no beginning, but the event's actual existence is preceded by non-existence, so it cannot be eternal.
