The First Objection: Is Eternity a Time?
Refutation of the first objection: eternity is not a time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that events are preceded by non-existence does not require treating eternity as a specific time, and how the author's third proof establishes that each event has a preceding non-existence with no beginning.

Core Idea
The proof that events are preceded by non-existence does not depend on imagining eternity as a time.

The first objection argues that the proof mistakenly treats eternity as a specific time. The author responds by showing that the proof's logic does not require that assumption. Instead, it relies on the idea that each event is preceded by a non-existence with no beginning, leading to the impossibility of an event coexisting with its own non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'والجواب : أما السؤال الأول' up to 'مسبوقاً بالعدم'. Then read the translation. Note the structure: objection, then response via the third proof.
The author immediately dismisses the need for the opponent's assumption.
2
Understand the hypothetical reasoning
The author asks you to suppose each non-existence preceding an event were an existing thing—then those would be eternal and contemporaneous, preventing any event from occurring with them because existence would coincide with non-existence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that whenever we suppose an event has a preceding non-existence with no first, we must judge that the event is preceded by non-existence. This does not require treating eternity as a time.
The proof is self-contained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : هذا الخيال إنما وقع لتوهم كون الأزل وقتاً معيناً ، قلنا : إنه لا حاجة في تقرير هذه الدلائل إلى ما ذكرتموه . وبيانه في الدليل الثالث أن نقول : إن كل واحد من الحوادث ، مسبوق بعدم لا أول له . فلو قدرنا كل واحد من تلك العدمات شيئاً موجوداً ، لكان كل واحد من تلك الأشياء قديماً. وتلك القدماء لا بد وأن تكون متقارنة الوجود في حيزها، وإلا لكان حصول بعضها بعد الغير. وذلك يقدح في كونها قدماء . وإذا كانت تلك الأشياء متقارنة في الوجود ، امتنع أن يحصل معها شيء من الحوادث . لأن أحد الأمور الحاصلة عند تلك المقارنة ، وهو عدم هذا الحادث. فلو حصل وجود هذا الحادث معه، لكان وجود الشيء مقارناً لعدمه. وإنه محال . فيثبت : أنا متى فرضنا أن لكل واحد منها عدماً سابقاً عليه ، لا أول له وجب الحكم بكون كلها مسبوقاً بالعدم . وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو المعارضة بالصحة . فنقول : نحن نسلم أنه لا بداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث. ومع هذا فإنه لا يلزم صحة حدوثها في الأزل . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا أخذنا موجوداً ممكن الوجود ، بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، سبقاً زمانياً . فهذا الشيء بهذا الشرط ، لا أول لإمكان وجوده . لأنه لا وقت يجعل أولاً لصحة وجوده ، إلا وتلك الصحة كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت . ثم مع هذا لا يلزم صحة كونه أزلياً ، لأن الأزلي هو الذي لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . وهذا الشيء قد أخذناه بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ، فكان كونه أزلياً : محال . فكذا ههنا . وأما السؤال الثالث : فجوابه : أن الشرط في كون الحادث حادثاً : أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم. وليس الشرط في كونه حادثاً : كونه ملحوقاً بالعدم . فيثبت : أن حصول العدم قبل وجود الحادث : واجب . أما حصول العدم بعده : فليس بواجب . فظهر الفرق . وأما السؤال الرابع : فقد سبق الكلام فيه والله أعلم
The answer: As for the first question—which is his statement: 'This imagination only occurred due to imagining that eternity (al-azal) is a specific time'—we say: There is no need for what you mentioned in establishing these proofs. Its clarification is in the third proof that we say: Every single event is preceded by a non-existence (ʿadam) that has no first. So if we suppose each of those non-existences to be an existing thing, each of those things would be eternal (qadīm). And those eternal things must necessarily be contemporaneous in existence in their locus; otherwise, some would occur after others, which would invalidate their being eternal. And when those things are contemporaneous in existence, it is impossible that any event should occur with them, because one of the things occurring at that contemporaneity is the non-existence of this event. So if the existence of this event occurred with it, then the existence of a thing would be concomitant with its non-existence, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that whenever we suppose that each of them has a non-existence preceding it with no first, it is necessary to judge that all of them are preceded by non-existence. As for the second question—which is the objection by possibility (al-ṣiḥḥa)—we say: We concede that there is no beginning for the possibility of the occurrence of events. Nevertheless, it does not necessitate the possibility of their occurrence in eternity (al-azal). The proof of that is: If we take an existent that is possible of existence, conditioned by being preceded by non-existence in a temporal precedence, then this thing with this condition has no beginning for its possibility of existence, because there is no time that is made a first for the possibility of its existence except that that possibility was present before that time. Then with this, the possibility of its being eternal does not follow, because the eternal is that which is not preceded by non-existence. And this thing we have taken with the condition of its being preceded by non-existence, so its being eternal is impossible. So it is here similarly. As for the third question, its answer is: The condition for an event being an event is that it be preceded by non-existence. It is not a condition for its being an event that it be followed by non-existence. Thus it is established that the occurrence of non-existence before the existence of the event is necessary, but the occurrence of non-existence after it is not necessary. So the difference is clear. As for the fourth question: The discussion about it has already preceded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage contains the author's responses to four objections (suʾālāt) raised against the argument that events are preceded by non-existence. The author answers each in order. First objection: The opponent claimed that the argument's reasoning mistakenly treats eternity (al-azal) as a specific time. The author replies that this is unnecessary; the third proof shows that each event is preceded by a non-existence with no beginning. If those non-existences were supposed to be eternal things, they would be contemporaneous, preventing any event from occurring with them, because the existence of an event would then coincide with its own non-existence. Thus, events must be preceded by non-existence. Second objection: The opponent argues that since the possibility of events has no beginning, events could occur eternally. The author concedes that possibility has no beginning, but this does not imply actual occurrence in eternity, because an event taken as preceded by non-existence cannot be eternal by definition. Third objection: The opponent claims that an event must be both preceded and followed by non-existence. The author distinguishes: the condition for being an event is only precedence by non-existence, not being followed by it. Thus non-existence before the event is necessary, but after it is not. Fourth objection: The author says it has been answered earlier. These responses clarify the precise conditions for contingency and defend the necessity of a first event.

Key Terms
الأزل
العدم
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof that events are preceded by non-existence requires treating eternity as a specific time.
Correction: The author shows that the proof works without any such assumption, using the concept of a non-existence with no beginning.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, what does the opponent claim the proof mistakenly imagines?
A) That events are eternal
B) That eternity is a specific time
C) That non-existence is eternal
D) That events have no beginning

Key Takeaway
The proof that events are preceded by non-existence does not depend on treating eternity as a time; it stands on its own logical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. You are an event (ḥādith) — you did not always exist. Your non-existence stretches back infinitely before you were born. Does that thought make you feel insignificant or precious? How should it shape your relationship with your Creator?
?
2. The proof shows that it is impossible for existence and non-existence to coexist. Yet you often act as if your worldly pursuits will never end. What would be different in your daily habits if you truly lived in the awareness that your existence is sandwiched between an eternal non-existence and a finite life?
?
3. The opponent in this passage imagined that the proof required seeing eternity as a time, so he dismissed it. Are there areas in your own faith where you reject a truth because you cannot imagine it the way you want? Will you submit to what the proofs demand, even if your imagination struggles?

The Second Objection: Does Possibility Imply Actuality in Eternity?
Refutation of the second objection: possibility does not necessitate eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of events having no beginning does not entail that they could occur in eternity, due to the condition of being preceded by non-existence.

Core Idea
Endless possibility does not mean actual occurrence in eternity, because an event conditioned by precedence is by definition not eternal.

The second objection argues that since the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of events has no beginning, events could have occurred in eternity itself. The author responds by conceding the endlessness of possibility but explaining that an event taken as preceded by non-existence cannot be eternal, because the eternal is defined as not preceded by non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the second objection
Read the Arabic from 'وأما السؤال الثاني' to 'فكذا ههنا' and its translation. Identify the objection and the author's admission.
The author concedes the premise but rejects the conclusion.
2
Follow the proof
The author asks you to consider an existent that is possible but conditioned by being temporally preceded by non-existence. Such a thing has no first moment of possibility, yet it cannot be eternal because eternal means not preceded by non-existence.
The condition prevents eternity.
3
Apply the lesson
The same logic applies to events: they are conditioned by precedence, so their endless possibility does not make them eternal.
The distinction between possibility and actuality is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : هذا الخيال إنما وقع لتوهم كون الأزل وقتاً معيناً ، قلنا : إنه لا حاجة في تقرير هذه الدلائل إلى ما ذكرتموه . وبيانه في الدليل الثالث أن نقول : إن كل واحد من الحوادث ، مسبوق بعدم لا أول له . فلو قدرنا كل واحد من تلك العدمات شيئاً موجوداً ، لكان كل واحد من تلك الأشياء قديماً. وتلك القدماء لا بد وأن تكون متقارنة الوجود في حيزها، وإلا لكان حصول بعضها بعد الغير. وذلك يقدح في كونها قدماء . وإذا كانت تلك الأشياء متقارنة في الوجود ، امتنع أن يحصل معها شيء من الحوادث . لأن أحد الأمور الحاصلة عند تلك المقارنة ، وهو عدم هذا الحادث. فلو حصل وجود هذا الحادث معه، لكان وجود الشيء مقارناً لعدمه. وإنه محال . فيثبت : أنا متى فرضنا أن لكل واحد منها عدماً سابقاً عليه ، لا أول له وجب الحكم بكون كلها مسبوقاً بالعدم . وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو المعارضة بالصحة . فنقول : نحن نسلم أنه لا بداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث. ومع هذا فإنه لا يلزم صحة حدوثها في الأزل . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا أخذنا موجوداً ممكن الوجود ، بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، سبقاً زمانياً . فهذا الشيء بهذا الشرط ، لا أول لإمكان وجوده . لأنه لا وقت يجعل أولاً لصحة وجوده ، إلا وتلك الصحة كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت . ثم مع هذا لا يلزم صحة كونه أزلياً ، لأن الأزلي هو الذي لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . وهذا الشيء قد أخذناه بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ، فكان كونه أزلياً : محال . فكذا ههنا . وأما السؤال الثالث : فجوابه : أن الشرط في كون الحادث حادثاً : أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم. وليس الشرط في كونه حادثاً : كونه ملحوقاً بالعدم . فيثبت : أن حصول العدم قبل وجود الحادث : واجب . أما حصول العدم بعده : فليس بواجب . فظهر الفرق . وأما السؤال الرابع : فقد سبق الكلام فيه والله أعلم
The answer: As for the first question—which is his statement: 'This imagination only occurred due to imagining that eternity (al-azal) is a specific time'—we say: There is no need for what you mentioned in establishing these proofs. Its clarification is in the third proof that we say: Every single event is preceded by a non-existence (ʿadam) that has no first. So if we suppose each of those non-existences to be an existing thing, each of those things would be eternal (qadīm). And those eternal things must necessarily be contemporaneous in existence in their locus; otherwise, some would occur after others, which would invalidate their being eternal. And when those things are contemporaneous in existence, it is impossible that any event should occur with them, because one of the things occurring at that contemporaneity is the non-existence of this event. So if the existence of this event occurred with it, then the existence of a thing would be concomitant with its non-existence, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that whenever we suppose that each of them has a non-existence preceding it with no first, it is necessary to judge that all of them are preceded by non-existence. As for the second question—which is the objection by possibility (al-ṣiḥḥa)—we say: We concede that there is no beginning for the possibility of the occurrence of events. Nevertheless, it does not necessitate the possibility of their occurrence in eternity (al-azal). The proof of that is: If we take an existent that is possible of existence, conditioned by being preceded by non-existence in a temporal precedence, then this thing with this condition has no beginning for its possibility of existence, because there is no time that is made a first for the possibility of its existence except that that possibility was present before that time. Then with this, the possibility of its being eternal does not follow, because the eternal is that which is not preceded by non-existence. And this thing we have taken with the condition of its being preceded by non-existence, so its being eternal is impossible. So it is here similarly. As for the third question, its answer is: The condition for an event being an event is that it be preceded by non-existence. It is not a condition for its being an event that it be followed by non-existence. Thus it is established that the occurrence of non-existence before the existence of the event is necessary, but the occurrence of non-existence after it is not necessary. So the difference is clear. As for the fourth question: The discussion about it has already preceded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage contains the author's responses to four objections (suʾālāt) raised against the argument that events are preceded by non-existence. The author answers each in order. First objection: The opponent claimed that the argument's reasoning mistakenly treats eternity (al-azal) as a specific time. The author replies that this is unnecessary; the third proof shows that each event is preceded by a non-existence with no beginning. If those non-existences were supposed to be eternal things, they would be contemporaneous, preventing any event from occurring with them, because the existence of an event would then coincide with its own non-existence. Thus, events must be preceded by non-existence. Second objection: The opponent argues that since the possibility of events has no beginning, events could occur eternally. The author concedes that possibility has no beginning, but this does not imply actual occurrence in eternity, because an event taken as preceded by non-existence cannot be eternal by definition. Third objection: The opponent claims that an event must be both preceded and followed by non-existence. The author distinguishes: the condition for being an event is only precedence by non-existence, not being followed by it. Thus non-existence before the event is necessary, but after it is not. Fourth objection: The author says it has been answered earlier. These responses clarify the precise conditions for contingency and defend the necessity of a first event.

Key Terms
الصحة
الأزل
العدم
السبق الزماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the possibility of events has no beginning, then it is possible for events to occur in eternity.
Correction: The author shows that the condition of being preceded by non-existence prevents events from being eternal, regardless of the endlessness of possibility.

Checkpoint
In the second objection, what does the author concede?
A) That events are eternal
B) That the possibility of events has no beginning
C) That events can occur in eternity
D) That events are not preceded by non-existence

Key Takeaway
Endless possibility does not make an event eternal because the event is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often dwell on paths not taken, but this lesson shows that possibility does not make things actual. Will you let go of one regret today, trusting that only what Allah wills actually occurs?
?
If you truly believe you are contingent—preceded by non-existence—how does that reshape your reliance on Allah as the only Necessary Being? Will you start each day with a conscious acknowledgment of your dependence?
?
The condition of being preceded by non-existence defines everything created. Do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What one habit will you change to remind yourself that you were created from nothing and are sustained moment by moment?

The Third Objection: Is an Event Both Preceded and Followed by Non-Existence?
Refutation of the third objection: condition for an event is precedence, not subsequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise condition for an event: it must be preceded by non-existence, but it is not necessary that it be followed by non-existence.

Core Idea
An event is defined by being preceded by non-existence, not by being followed by it.

The third objection claims that an event must be both preceded and followed by non-existence. The author distinguishes: the condition for being an event (ḥādith) is only that it is preceded by non-existence; being followed by non-existence is not a condition. Thus non-existence before the event is necessary, but after it is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the third answer
Read the Arabic from 'وأما السؤال الثالث' to 'فظهر الفرق' and its translation. Identify the objection and the author's clear distinction.
This is a short but precise response.
2
Analyze the condition
The author states that the condition (sharṭ) for an event is being preceded by non-existence, not being followed by it. Understand the difference.
This refutes the opponent's assumption.
3
Apply the conclusion
Therefore, non-existence before the event is required; non-existence after is optional. This shows the event is contingent on a beginning, not an end.
The conclusion supports the main argument.

The Beginning of Time is Not Intuitive
Author denies that the existence of a beginning of time is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of a beginning of time is not considered self-evident in kalam.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational people proves a claim is not intuitive.

In this passage, the author tackles an objection that claims the existence of a beginning of time is self-evident and therefore your proof is invalid. The author responds by showing that far from being intuitive, the beginning of time has been a matter of disagreement among rational thinkers. This disagreement itself becomes the evidence that the claim is not intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's objection
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent argues that the proof of a beginning of time contradicts intuitive knowledge.
2
Identify the author's response
Notice that the author denies the opponent's premise: the beginning of time is not self-evident.
3
Analyze the evidence
The author points to disagreement among rational people (العقلاء) as proof that the claim is not intuitive. If it were intuitive, everyone would agree.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Ask yourself: why does disagreement undermine intuitive knowledge? Intuitive knowledge is universal; disagreement proves it is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع : وهو قولكم : ((۲) و إن دليلكم يوجب انتهاء المدة إلى المبدأ ، لا يحصل قبله قبل البنة ، وذلك مدفوع في بديهة العقل ، فكان دليلكم دفعاً للعلم البديهي ، فيكون باطلاً ، قلنا : لا نسلم : أن إثبات مبدأ المدة والزمان مدفوع في بديهية العقل والدليل عليه : أن العقلاء اختلفوا فيه ، ولو كان بديهياً لما وقع الاختلاف فيه
As for the fourth question: it is your statement: 'And indeed your proof requires the ending of duration at a beginning, nothing occurs before it at all, and that is repudiated by the intuition of the intellect, so your proof is a repudiation of intuitive knowledge, thus it is false.' We say: We do not accept that establishing a beginning of duration and time is repudiated by the intuition of the intellect. The evidence for that is that rational people have disagreed about it, and if it were intuitive, disagreement would not occur.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage responds to the fourth question. The opponent claims that the proof of a beginning of time contradicts intuitive knowledge, so the proof is invalid. The author refutes this by denying that the existence of a beginning of time is intuitive. The author provides evidence: rational people (العقلاء) have disagreed on this issue. If it were self-evident (بديهي), there would be no disagreement. Therefore, the opponent's claim that it is intuitive is false, and the proof stands.

Key Terms
مبدأ المدة
الزمان
بديهة العقل
اختلاف العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The beginning of time is self-evident to everyone.
Correction: The author shows it is not self-evident because rational people disagree about it.

D) By appealing to sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that the beginning of time is not intuitive because intelligent people have disagreed about it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it cost you to honestly consider that possibility?
?
This lesson uses disagreement itself as evidence that a claim is not intuitive. Is there a belief you hold so firmly that you have never questioned its obviousness? What would change in your life if you subjected that belief to the same test—asking whether rational people genuinely disagree about it?

Refuting the Intuition Objection: Disagreement as Evidence
The author refutes an objection by denying that time's beginning is intuitive, citing disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that time had a beginning is not an intuitive truth, based on the fact that rational thinkers have disagreed about it.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational thinkers shows a claim is not self-evident.

Philosophers often appeal to intuition or self-evident truths. But how do we know if something is truly intuitive? In this passage, the author faces an objection that his proof for a beginning of time contradicts intuition. His response is both simple and powerful: if it were intuitive, there would be no debate. Instead, great minds have disagreed, proving the matter is not self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that the author's proof leads to a conclusion that seems counterintuitive (no time before the beginning), so the proof must be false because it contradicts intuition.
The objector treats intuition as an infallible source of truth.
2
Identify the author's move
The author does not directly argue about intuition's reliability. Instead, he questions the premise that this particular claim (that time has a beginning) is intuitive.
3
Analyze the evidence
The author provides a test for intuitiveness: if something were intuitive, rational people would not disagree about it. He notes that rational thinkers have indeed disagreed about the beginning of time.
Disagreement is a sign that a claim is not self-evident.
4
Apply the test
Since disagreement exists, the claim is not intuitive, and the objection fails. The proof is not invalidated by intuition.
This argument does not prove time had a beginning; it only defends the proof from the intuition objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع : وهو قولكم : وإن دليلكم يوجب انتهاء المدة إلى المبدأ ، لا يحصل قبله قبل البنة [؟]، وذلك مدفوع في بديهة العقل ، فكان دليلكم دفعاً للعلم البديهي ، فيكون باطلاً ، قلنا : لا نسلم : أن إثبات مبدأ المدة والزمان مدفوع في بديهية العقل والدليل عليه : أن العقلاء اختلفوا فيه ، ولو كان بديهياً لما وقع الاختلاف فيه
As for the fourth question, which is your statement: 'Your proof necessitates the termination of duration at the beginning, which does not occur before it before [؟], and that is repudiated by the intuition of the intellect, so your proof is a repudiation of intuitive knowledge, therefore it is false.' We say: We do not concede that establishing the beginning of duration and time is repudiated by the intuition of the intellect. The evidence for that is that rational thinkers have differed concerning it. Had it been intuitive, no disagreement would have occurred concerning it.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a specific objection (the fourth objection) raised against a previous proof for a beginning of time. The objector claimed that the author's proof contradicts intuitive reason because it implies that no 'before' exists before the beginning, which intuition rejects. The author responds by denying that the existence of a beginning of time is an intuitive truth. He supports this with the observation that rational thinkers have disagreed on whether time had a beginning; if it were intuitively evident, there would be no room for disagreement. The argument is that disagreement is a sign of non-axiomatic status, so the objector cannot claim that the proof contradicts a self-evident truth.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
مبدأ المدة
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If many people accept a claim, it must be intuitive.
Correction: Intuitive truths are self-evident and cannot be reasonably doubted. Disagreement among rational thinkers shows the claim is not intuitive, even if widely held.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main reason for denying that the beginning of time is an intuitive truth?
A) Intuition cannot be known with certainty.
B) Rational thinkers have disagreed about it.
C) The objector's proof is weak.
D) Intuition only applies to mathematics.

Key Takeaway
A claim is not intuitive if rational thinkers have genuinely disagreed about it.

The Sixth Proof: Past Cycles Must Have a Beginning
Sanusi presents the sixth proof for the world's beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of an infinite series terminating leads to the conclusion that past cycles must have a beginning.

Core Idea
An infinite series cannot reach the present day.

This passage presents the core of the sixth proof: if past cycles had no beginning, they would be infinite, but an infinite series cannot terminate at the present day. Since sense perception confirms that past cycles have reached this day, they must have had a beginning. This short argument is the foundation of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Note the conditional form 'if...then'.
The proof is concise; each clause carries weight.
2
Identify the premises
Spot the two main premises: (1) If no first cycle, then cycles are infinite. (2) That which is infinite does not terminate.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Notice how the conclusion follows: since sense shows termination, the cycles cannot be infinite, so they must have a first.
This is a direct modus tollens argument.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why this argument is compelling for proving the world had a beginning.
It relies on the nature of actual infinities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو لم يكن للأدوار الماضية أول ، لكانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية، وما لا نهاية له ، لا ينتهي . فلو كانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية ، لامتنع ابتداؤها ولما دل الحس على انتهاء الأدوار الماضية إلى هذا اليوم علمنا : أن الأدوار الماضية لها أول . اعترض الخصم عليه . فقال : إن هذه الحوادث لا نهاية لها من جانب الأزل ، وهي متناهية من الجانب الذي يلينا ويتصل بنا . وكونه غير متناه من أحد الجانبين ، لا يناقض كونه متناهياً من الجانب الآخر . فإن قال المستدل : المراد من هذا الدليل : أن الدورات الماضية لو كانت غير متناهية في العدد ، لامتنع انتهاؤها إلى طرف معين، ومقطع معين ، وحينئذ يظهر وجه الاستدلال قال المعترض: الأعداد التي لا نهاية لها ، إذا ابتدأت من وقت معين ، امتنع انتهاؤها إلى وقت آخر ، يحكم فيه بأن كل ما لا نهاية له ، قد خرج إلى الفعل، فيما بين هذين الطرفين. لأن على هذا التقدير تكون مدة حدوثها محصورة بين حاصرين ، وذلك يمنع من القول بكونها غير متناهية في العدد . إلا أن هذا المحال ، إنما يلزم ، لو قلنا : إن تلك الحوادث ابتدأت بالحدوث من وقت معين. وهذا هو عين المطلوب. فتصير صحة الدليل . موقوفة على صحة المطلوب . وذلك باطل . وأما إن رفعنا هذا الشرط ، وقلنا : إن الحوادث التي لا أول لها ، يمتنع انتهاؤها إلى طرف معين من الجانب الذي يلينا . فهذا [؟] محل النزاع ، فلا يمكن جعله دليلاً على صحة محل النزاع .
The sixth proof: If the past cycles had no first, then the past cycles would be infinite, and that which has no end does not terminate. So if the past cycles were infinite, their beginning would be impossible. Since sense perception indicates the termination of past cycles at this day, we know that the past cycles have a first. The opponent objected to it, saying: These events have no end from the side of pre-eternity, and they are finite from the side that is next to us and connected to us. That it is infinite from one side does not contradict its being finite from the other side. Then the proponent said: The meaning of this proof is that if the past cycles were infinite in number, their termination at a specific limit and specific point would be impossible, and then the aspect of inference appears. The objector said: Numbers that have no end, if they begin at a specific time, their termination at another time is impossible, and it is judged that everything that has no end has come into actuality between these two limits. Because on this supposition, the duration of their occurrence is confined between two confines, and that prevents the claim that they are infinite in number. However, this impossibility only follows if we say that those events began to occur at a specific time. And this is exactly the claim in question. So the validity of the proof becomes dependent on the validity of the claim in question, and that is false. But if we remove this condition and say that events without a first cannot terminate at a specific limit on the side that is next to us, then this [؟] is the point of disagreement, so it cannot be taken as evidence for the validity of the point of disagreement.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the beginning of the world, followed by an objection and a detailed response. The proof argues that if past cycles had no beginning, they would be infinite. But an infinite series cannot terminate; yet sense perception shows that past cycles have terminated at the present day. Therefore, past cycles must have a beginning. The opponent objects that events are infinite only from the side of pre-eternity but finite from the side that meets us, and this is not contradictory. The proponent clarifies that the proof is about number: an infinite number of past cycles cannot reach a specific point like today. The opponent then argues that if the series began at a specific time, then it would be impossible for it to reach another time without being actualized between two limits, which contradicts its infinity. However, this objection only works if one assumes the series has a starting point—which is exactly what needs to be proved. So the objection is circular. If instead one removes that condition, the opponent claims that the impossibility of termination from our side is just the disputed point, not a proof. The author’s final response is cut off but the structure shows the argument is about whether one can use the impossibility of an infinite series reaching a limit without presupposing a beginning.

غير متناه
انتهاء
الحس

D) The present day is the end of time.

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof shows that past cycles must have a beginning because an infinite series cannot reach the present day.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof relies on sense perception telling us that the past terminates at today. Am I using my senses to recognize the signs of God's power in the present moment, or am I numbly letting days pass without reflecting on why today is possible?

Objection and Response: Infinite Past Cycles and the Present
Sanusi presents the objection and response to the sixth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectic surrounding the sixth proof, including the objection that infinity on one side does not preclude finitude on the other, and the circularity counter.

Core Idea
The objection fails because it assumes what needs to be proved.

After presenting the proof, Sanusi records an objection: events could be infinite from the side of pre-eternity but finite from our side. The proponent clarifies that the proof concerns the number of cycles, not the temporal direction. The objector then responds that if the series began at a specific time, it would be finite between two limits, which is exactly what is disputed. This leads to a discussion of circularity in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and responses
Read the full Arabic passage. Notice the back-and-forth between objector and proponent.
There are three moves: objection, clarification, counter.
2
Understand the objection
The opponent says events can be infinite on one side and finite on the other. Identify why this seems plausible.
He distinguishes direction from number.
3
Trace the proponent's clarification
The proponent reframes the proof in terms of number: an infinite number cannot terminate at a specific point.
This shifts the focus to the quantity of events.
4
Analyze the objector's counter
The objector says: if the series began at a specific time, then it is confined between two limits, making it finite. But that assumes a beginning—the very point at issue.
This reveals a circularity in the objector's reasoning.
5
Recognize the impasse
The proponent's final remark indicates that if we remove the condition of a beginning, the impossibility of termination from our side is just the disputed point, not a proof.
The dialectic ends without resolution in this passage.

The Seventh Proof: From Today to a Finite Past
Proof that infinite past leads to impossibility of today.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of today proves that the past cycles must be finite.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, today could never arrive.

The author presents a clear logical proof that the past must be finite. Using the simple fact that today exists, he shows that an infinite past would make today's occurrence impossible. This is a classic argument in Islamic theology for the temporal finitude of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Follow the logical steps the author outlines.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the premise that today depends on the past passing, and the assumption that the past is infinite.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the impossibility
Grasp why passing an infinite series is considered impossible in this argument.
The author equates 'passing' with 'completing' an infinite series.
4
Reach the conclusion
See how the contradiction leads to the conclusion that the past must have a beginning.
Today's existence is the evidence for finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لا شك أن حصول هذا اليوم الذي نحن فيه ، موقوف على انقضاء ما قبله ، فلو كانت الأدوار الحاصلة قبل هذا اليوم ، أعداد غير متناهية ، لكان حصول هذا اليوم موقوفاً على انقضاء ما لا نهاية له ، لكن انقضاء ما لا نهاية له : محال . والموقوف على المحال : محال . فكان يلزم أن يمتنع حصول هذا اليوم. ولما حصل هذا اليوم، علمنا : أن الأدوار الماضية لها أول . اعترض الخصم فقال :قولكم إن كل ما توقف حدوثه، على انقضاء ما لانهاية له، كان ممتنع الحصول كلام يحتمل أمرين: أحدهما: أن يكون المشروط والشرط معدومين ، ثم كان ذلك المشروط مشروطاً بأن تتبدل الحوادث بعد عدمها، وتنقضي منها أعداد لا نهاية لها . ثم يحدث بعد انقضائها ذلك المشروط . والثاني : أن لا يعتبر كون المشروط والشرط معدومين . بل قلنا : إنه قد انقضى قبل هذا اليوم حوادث لا أول لها ، ثم حدث عقيبها هذا اليوم. فإن أردتم بقولكم : لو كانت الحوادث الماضية غير متناهية ، لتوقف حدوث هذا اليوم على انقضاء أمور غير متناهية ، فإن كان المراد هو الوجه الأول فهو باطل من وجهين :
The seventh proof: There is no doubt that the occurrence of this day in which we are is dependent upon the passing of what is before it. So if the cycles occurring before this day were infinite numbers, then the occurrence of this day would be dependent upon the passing of that which has no end. But the passing of that which has no end is impossible. And that which is dependent upon the impossible is impossible. Thus it would necessarily be impossible for this day to occur. And since this day has occurred, we know that the past cycles have a first. The opponent objected, saying: Your statement that everything whose occurrence is dependent upon the passing of that which has no end is impossible of occurrence is a statement that admits of two interpretations: One of them is that the conditioned and the condition are both nonexistent, then that conditioned is conditioned by the events changing after their non-existence and numbers without end passing from them, then that conditioned occurs after their passing. The second is that we do not consider the conditioned and the condition as nonexistent. Rather we say that events without a first have passed before this day, then this day occurred after them. And if you mean by your statement 'If the past events were infinite, the occurrence of this day would be dependent upon the passing of infinite things'—if the intended meaning is the first interpretation, then it is false on two grounds:
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof and the beginning of an objection. The proof states that today's occurrence depends on the passing of all preceding days. If those preceding days were infinite in number, then today would depend on passing an infinite series, which is impossible. Therefore, the past must be finite. The opponent objects that the claim 'whatever depends on passing an infinite series is impossible' can be interpreted in two ways. In the first interpretation, both the condition (the passing of infinite events) and the conditioned (today) are considered nonexistent, and then the conditioned is supposed to occur after the passing of an infinite number of events that begin from non-existence. In the second interpretation, the condition and conditioned are not considered nonexistent; rather, infinite events have already passed, and today occurred after them. The author then begins to refute the first interpretation, stating it is false on two grounds, but the passage ends before listing those grounds. The argument is that the first interpretation is circular because it assumes what needs to be proved (that events have a beginning), while the second interpretation simply restates the opponent's position without proving its possibility.

Key Terms
موقوف
انقضاء
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite past could be traversed if it is not 'traversed' from a starting point.
Correction: The argument shows that the very notion of 'today' after an actual infinite past is contradictory, because it would require completing an infinite series by successive addition.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the seventh proof?
A) Today cannot occur if the past is infinite.
B) The past cycles have no beginning.
C) Today depends on an infinite series.
D) The opponent's objection is false.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of today proves that the past must be finite, because an infinite past would make today's occurrence impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If time is finite and created, then your life is a limited opportunity. How will you use the 'today' you have been given to prepare for what comes after?
?
The argument shows that an infinite past is impossible because today could never arrive. Consider your own journey: every moment you experience depends on the moment before. Does your life feel like a series of dependencies leading back to a starting point? Who or what do you acknowledge as the ultimate starting point of your existence?
?
If the past were infinite, you would not exist to read this. But you do exist. What one change will you make today to live in light of the reality that time has a beginning and an end?

Objection to the Proof: Two Interpretations
Objection with two interpretations of the impossibility premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's objection to the seventh proof and the two interpretations of the key conditional statement.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that the statement 'whatever depends on passing an infinite is impossible' can be interpreted in two ways.

The opponent raises a subtle objection: the key premise in the proof—that anything dependent on passing an infinite series is impossible—can be understood in two different ways. One interpretation makes the argument circular, the other simply restates the opponent's worldview. The author begins to refute the first interpretation, but the passage is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its context
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Understand that the opponent is responding to the previous proof.
The objection aims to undermine the key premise.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find and distinguish between the first interpretation (both condition and conditioned nonexistent) and the second (condition and conditioned not nonexistent).
The first interpretation is about a hypothetical scenario, the second about actual past events.
3
Analyze the first interpretation
Understand the logical structure: from nonexistence, events begin, pass infinitely, then conditioned occurs. Note the circularity.
This interpretation seems to assume a starting point, which is what the opponent wants to prove.
4
Analyze the second interpretation
This interpretation asserts that infinite events already passed, and today occurred after them. It simply assumes the possibility of an infinite past.
The author will later argue that this is not a genuine objection but a restatement of the opposing view.
5
Note the incomplete refutation
The author begins to refute the first interpretation but the passage ends abruptly. Be aware that the full refutation is not present.
The refutation is cut off at 'من وجهين:' (false on two grounds).

الحجة السابعة : لا شك أن حصول هذا اليوم الذي نحن فيه ، موقوف على انقضاء ما قبله ، فلو كانت الأدوار الحاصلة قبل هذا اليوم ، أعداد غير متناهية ، لكان حصول هذا اليوم موقوفاً على انقضاء ما لا نهاية له ، لكن انقضاء ما لا نهاية له : محال . والموقوف على المحال : محال . فكان يلزم أن يمتنع حصول هذا اليوم. ولما حصل هذا اليوم، علمنا : أن الأدوار الماضية لها أول . اعترض الخصم فقال :قولكم إن كل ما توقف حدوثه، على انقضاء ما لانهاية له، كان ممتنع الحصول كلام يحتمل أمرين: أحدهما: أن يكون المشروط والشرط معدومين ، ثم كان ذلك المشروط مشروطاً بأن تتبدل الحوادث بعد عدمها، وتنقضي منها أعداد لا نهاية لها . ثم يحدث بعد انقضائها ذلك المشروط . والثاني : أن لا يعتبر كون المشروط والشرط معدومين . بل قلنا : إنه قد انقضى قبل هذا اليوم حوادث لا أول لها ، ثم حدث عقيبها هذا اليوم. فإن أردتم بقولكم : لو كانت الحوادث الماضية غير متناهية ، لتوقف حدوث هذا اليوم على انقضاء أمور غير متناهية ، فإن كان المراد هو الوجه الأول فهو باطل من وجهين :
The seventh proof: There is no doubt that the occurrence of this day in which we are is dependent upon the passing of what is before it. So if the cycles occurring before this day were infinite numbers, then the occurrence of this day would be dependent upon the passing of that which has no end. But the passing of that which has no end is impossible. And that which is dependent upon the impossible is impossible. Thus it would necessarily be impossible for this day to occur. And since this day has occurred, we know that the past cycles have a first. The opponent objected, saying: Your statement that everything whose occurrence is dependent upon the passing of that which has no end is impossible of occurrence is a statement that admits of two interpretations: One of them is that the conditioned and the condition are both nonexistent, then that conditioned is conditioned by the events changing after their non-existence and numbers without end passing from them, then that conditioned occurs after their passing. The second is that we do not consider the conditioned and the condition as nonexistent. Rather we say that events without a first have passed before this day, then this day occurred after them. And if you mean by your statement 'If the past events were infinite, the occurrence of this day would be dependent upon the passing of infinite things'—if the intended meaning is the first interpretation, then it is false on two grounds:
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof and the beginning of an objection. The proof states that today's occurrence depends on the passing of all preceding days. If those preceding days were infinite in number, then today would depend on passing an infinite series, which is impossible. Therefore, the past must be finite. The opponent objects that the claim 'whatever depends on passing an infinite series is impossible' can be interpreted in two ways. In the first interpretation, both the condition (the passing of infinite events) and the conditioned (today) are considered nonexistent, and then the conditioned is supposed to occur after the passing of an infinite number of events that begin from non-existence. In the second interpretation, the condition and conditioned are not considered nonexistent; rather, infinite events have already passed, and today occurred after them. The author then begins to refute the first interpretation, stating it is false on two grounds, but the passage ends before listing those grounds. The argument is that the first interpretation is circular because it assumes what needs to be proved (that events have a beginning), while the second interpretation simply restates the opponent's position without proving its possibility.

شرط
محال

Checkpoint
How many interpretations does the opponent propose?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The opponent presents two interpretations of the key premise, and the author begins to refute the first one, but the refutation is incomplete in this passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a truth that challenges your deeply held assumptions, do you tend to reinterpret it to avoid its implications, as the opponent does here? What would it cost you to accept the truth fully?
?
Your life depends on countless causes beyond your control—from the air you breathe to the sun's energy. Yet you often feel self-sufficient. How does acknowledging this dependence reshape your daily gratitude and reliance on the Creator?
?
This lesson forces you to consider that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In your own life, do you sometimes act as though your habits or faults have always been there, with no beginning? What would it mean to trace them back to a first cause and take responsibility for them?

The Tenth Argument and the Objection
Sanusi presents the tenth argument and a dialectical exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' tenth argument for temporal origination and the philosophers' initial objection focusing on individual vs. collective events.

Core Idea
Temporal origination applies individually, not collectively.

This passage presents the tenth argument of the theologians: the reality of temporal origination cannot coexist with eternity. The philosophers object by distinguishing between each event and the totality. This exchange sets the stage for a deeper discussion about the nature of time and priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three-part structure: argument, objection, response.
2
Identify the three parts
Find the tenth argument, the opponent's objection, and the theologians' rejoinder. Label each.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the philosophers distinguish between individual events and their totality. Does this resolve the contradiction?
4
Understand the dismissal
The theologians dismiss the objection as a return to the ninth argument. Why might they say that?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قالوا : حقيقة الحدوث تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير ، فكانت حقيقة الحدوث منافية لحقيقة الأزل. فالقول بحدوث الحوادث في الأزل محال . واعترض الخصم فقال : المحكوم عليه بالحدوث عندي : هو كل واحد منها ، لا مجموعها . وكل واحد منها محكوم عليه بأنه ليس بأزلي . فإن قلتم : لما كان كل واحد منها : محكوم عليه بأنه ليس بأزلي امتنع أن يكون مجموعها أزلياً : كان هذا عوداً إلى الحجة التاسعة ، وقد سبق الكلام عليها . فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب وللفلاسفة ههنا مقام آخر : قالوا : لا شك أن الدلائل التي ذكرتموها في وجوب أن تكون للمدة أول دلائل غامضة دقيقة مشتبهة لا يمكن الوقوف عليها إلا بفكر دقيق ونظر غامض وأما مطلوبنا في هذا الباب : فهو أمر كل من وقف عليه على الوجه الملخص ، حكم صريح عقله بالصحة. وذلك لأن المفهوم من القبلية والبعدية [ والتقدمية ] والتقدم والتأخر أمر لا يحصل إلا عند تقرر المدة والزمان . فإنه لا معنى للمدة والزمان إلا الموجود الذي بسببه تحصل هذه المفهومات . فإذ حكمنا بمقتضى ما ذكرتموه من الدلائل ، وجب أن نعتقد : أن للمدة والزمان أولاً وبداية . وحينئذ لا يحصل قبل ذلك الأول ، لا قبل ولا بعد ، ولا تأخر ولا تقدم البتة . إلا أن صريح العقل [ حاكم ] بدفع ذلك . لأن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن عدم ذلك الأول سابق عليه ومتقدم عليه . وبديهة العقل حاكمة أيضاً : بأنه يمكن فرض حوادث قبل ذلك الأول [ بحيث تنتهي إلى ذلك الأول بعشرة دورات . ويمكن أيضاً : فرض حوادث أخزى ، تنتهي إلى ذلك الأول بعشرين دورة . ويحكم صريح العقل بأن مبدأ هذا المفروض الثاني ، يجب أن يكون سابقاً على مبدأ المفروض الأول ، فيثبت : أن دلائلكم توجب نفي القبلية والبعدية والتقدم والتأخر ، قبل حدوث ذلك المبدأ . وصريح العقل شاهد بأن هذه الأمور كانت حاصلة . وتمام الكلام في هذا المقام : مذكور في باب الزمان ، في الفصل الذي نبين فيه : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان : علم بديهي لا يمكن إزالته عن العقل البتة . ويرجع حاصل الكلام : إلى أن دلائلكم توجب فساد ما علمت صحته بالبديهة ، فوجب القطع بفساد هذه الدلائل قال المتكلمون : لو كان العلم بوجوب حصول القبلية والبعدية والتقدم والتأخر ، علماً بديهياً لما وقع الخلاف فيه بين العقلاء . ولما وقع الخلاف فيه : علمنا أن ذلك ليس من البديهيات قالت الفلاسفة : المعتبر في تمييز البديهيات عن النظريات : الفطر السليمة الباقية على السلامة الأصلية. وأهل الجدل من المتكلمين قد فسدت فطرة نفوسهم الأصلية ، بسبب المواظبة على المجادلات والمنازعات . وأما من لم يمارس الجدل [ والنظر ] فإنه تشهد فطرته بأن كل أمر حدث بعد أن يكن ، فعدمه سابق على وجوده ، فيكون [ معنى ] القبلية والسبق حاصلا قبل ذلك الحادث أجاب المتكلمون عنه : بأن هذا مجرد وهم وخيال ، ولا عبرة بهما البتة . فهذا منتهى الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم [ بالصواب ]
The tenth argument: They said: The reality of temporal origination (ḥudūth) necessitates being preceded by another, so the reality of temporal origination is incompatible with the reality of eternity (azal). Hence, the statement that events originate (ḥudūth al-ḥawādith) in the eternal (al-azal) is impossible. And the opponent objected and said: For me, that which is judged to be temporally originated is each individual event, not the totality. Each individual event is judged not to be eternal. If you say: Since each individual event is judged not to be eternal, it is impossible for their totality to be eternal — this is a return to the ninth argument, and it has already been discussed. So this is the summary of the discourse in this chapter. And the philosophers have another position here: They said: There is no doubt that the proofs you have mentioned regarding the necessity of time (al-mudda) having a beginning are subtle, precise, and ambiguous proofs that cannot be grasped except by precise thought and deep reflection. As for what we seek in this chapter: it is a matter such that everyone who examines it in a simplified manner will judge its correctness by his clear reason. That is because the concept of priority (al-qabliyya) and posteriority (al-ba'diyya) and [progressiveness (al-taqaddumiyya)] and precedence (al-taqaddum) and delay (al-ta'akhkhur) does not occur except when duration (al-mudda) and time (al-zamān) are established. For there is no meaning of duration and time except the existent by means of which these concepts occur. So if we judge according to what you have mentioned of proofs, we must believe that duration and time have a first and a beginning. In that case, before that first there is no prior, no posterior, no delay, and no precedence at all. However, clear reason judges decisively [ḥākim] to reject that. Because the intuition (badīha) of reason judges that the nonexistence of that first is prior and antecedent to it. And the intuition of reason also judges that it is possible to suppose events before that first [such that they end at that first in ten cycles]. And it is also possible to suppose other events that end at that first in twenty cycles. And clear reason judges that the beginning of this second supposed [set] must be prior to the beginning of the first supposed [set]. Thus it is established that your proofs necessitate the negation of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay before the occurrence of that beginning. And clear reason testifies that these matters were present. And the completion of the discourse on this point is mentioned in the chapter on time (bāb al-zamān), in the section where we show that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is necessary knowledge (ʿilm badīhī) that cannot be removed from the mind at all. And the upshot of the discourse is that your proofs necessitate the corruption of what has been known to be correct by intuition, so it is necessary to judge definitively that these proofs are corrupt. The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) said: If knowledge of the necessity of the occurrence of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay were necessary knowledge, then there would be no disagreement about it among rational people. And since there is disagreement about it, we know that it is not among the necessary truths. The philosophers said: The criterion for distinguishing necessary truths (al-badīhiyyāt) from theoretical truths (al-naẓariyyāt) is sound innate dispositions (al-fiṭar al-salīma) remaining upon original soundness. The disputatious people among the theologians have had the original disposition of their souls corrupted due to their persistence in arguments and disputes. As for one who has not practiced disputation [and reflection (al-naẓar)], his innate disposition testifies that every thing that occurs after not having been — its non-existence is prior to its existence, so the meaning of priority and precedence occurs before that event. The theologians answered him: That is mere fancy and imagination, and no consideration is given to them at all. So this is the end of the discourse in this chapter. And God knows best [what is correct].
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a multi-layered debate between the theologians (mutakallimūn) and the philosophers (falāsifa) regarding the beginning of time and the implications of temporal origination. It begins with the 'tenth argument' of the theologians: the reality of temporal origination (ḥudūth) necessitates being preceded by something else, which contradicts the nature of eternity (azal); thus, it is impossible for events to originate in the eternal. The opponent (likely a philosopher) objects by clarifying that temporal origination applies to each individual event, not to the totality of events. Each event is not eternal, but the totality might still be eternal? The theologians reject this as a repetition of the ninth argument already addressed. The philosophers then present their own position: they argue that the theologians' proofs for a first moment of time are obscure and can only be grasped through deep thought. Their desired conclusion is that the concepts of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay only arise with the existence of time. If we accept the theologians' proofs, we would have to believe that time has a beginning, and before that beginning there would be no prior or posterior at all. But clear reason ('aql ṣarīḥ) decisively rejects this, because intuition (badīha) tells us that the non-existence of that first moment is prior to it. Moreover, we can suppose events before that first moment, e.g., in sets of ten or twenty cycles, and reason judges that the first of each set is prior to others. Thus the theologians' proofs would negate the very concepts of priority and posteriority that are evidently present. The philosophers conclude that the theologians' proofs corrupt what is known by intuition, so they must be false. The theologians respond by challenging the claim that these concepts are known intuitively: if they were truly necessary truths (badīhiyyāt), there would be no disagreement among rational people; since there is disagreement, they are not necessary. The philosophers counter with their criterion for necessary truths: sound innate dispositions (fiṭar) that remain in their original state. They argue that the theologians, through their dialectical practice, have corrupted their fiṭra. An ordinary person with an uncorrupted fiṭra recognizes that for any event that occurs after not existing, its non-existence is prior to its existence, meaning priority is present. The theologians dismiss this as mere fancy and imagination. The passage ends with a concise summary: 'This is the end of the discourse in this chapter.' The structure follows a back-and-forth: argument, objection, counter-argument, rebuttal, and final dismissal. The key tension is between theoretical proofs (the theologians' arguments) and immediate rational intuition (the philosophers' appeal to badīha and fiṭra).

Key Terms
حدوث
أزل
مسبوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if every event is temporal, the whole series must also be temporal.
Correction: The philosophers argue that temporal origination applies individually, not collectively, allowing the possibility of an eternal series of temporal events.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth argument, why is it impossible for events to originate in eternity?
A) Because eternity has no beginning, so events cannot be added to it.
B) Because the reality of temporal origination requires being preceded by another, contradicting eternity.
C) Because the philosophers deny causality.
D) Because the theologians have proven it in the ninth argument.

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination applies to each individual event, not necessarily to the entire series — a crucial nuance in the debate on eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a difficult Islamic teaching, do you ever exempt yourself by thinking it applies to 'others in general' but not to you personally? How does this lesson’s distinction between individual and collective judgment expose that tendency in your own heart?
?
The opponent used a technical distinction to avoid the clear implication of the argument. Are there areas in your life where you use intellectual loopholes or semantic games to resist a truth that demands your submission?
?
This argument is about the impossibility of events originating in eternity. Reflect: is there any belief or habit you hold onto that you treat as if it can coexist with the eternal decrees of Allah, even though in your heart you know it is temporally originated and must be surrendered? Will you surrender it today?

The Philosophers' Rebuttal and the Criterion of Intuitive Knowledge
Sanusi presents the philosophers' counter-argument and the ensuing dialectic on intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the theologians' proofs for a beginning of time conflict with intuitive reason, and the subsequent debate on the criterion for necessary truths.

Core Idea
If a proof contradicts what is known by intuition, the proof must be false.

The philosophers now present their own position: the concepts of priority and posteriority only make sense within time. The theologians' proofs would deny these concepts before the first moment, but intuition affirms them. This leads to a clash between theoretical proof and immediate rational knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the passage from 'وللفلاسفة ههنا مقام آخر' onward. Understand their claim that priority and posteriority require time.
2
Understand the thought experiment
The philosophers propose imagining events in cycles before a supposed first moment. Notice how reason judges priority among them.
3
Explore the theologians' reply
The theologians argue that if these concepts were necessary, there would be no disagreement. Disagreement shows they are not intuitive.
4
Examine the philosophers' criterion
The philosophers introduce sound innate disposition (fiṭra) as the criterion for necessary truths, claiming theologians have corrupted theirs.
5
See the final dismissal
The theologians reject the philosophers' appeal as mere fancy. Note how the chapter ends.

The tenth argument: They said: The reality of temporal origination (ḥudūth) necessitates being preceded by another, so the reality of temporal origination is incompatible with the reality of eternity (azal). Hence, the statement that events originate (ḥudūth al-ḥawādith) in the eternal (al-azal) is impossible. And the opponent objected and said: For me, that which is judged to be temporally originated is each individual event, not the totality. Each individual event is judged not to be eternal. If you say: Since each individual event is judged not to be eternal, it is impossible for their totality to be eternal — this is a return to the ninth argument, and it has already been discussed. So this is the summary of the discourse in this chapter. And the philosophers have another position here: They said: There is no doubt that the proofs you have mentioned regarding the necessity of time (al-mudda) having a beginning are subtle, precise, and ambiguous proofs that cannot be grasped except by precise thought and deep reflection. As for what we seek in this chapter: it is a matter such that everyone who examines it in a simplified manner will judge its correctness by his clear reason. That is because the concept of priority (al-qabliyya) and posteriority (al-ba'diyya) and [progressiveness (al-taqaddumiyya)] and precedence (al-taqaddum) and delay (al-ta'akhkhur) does not occur except when duration (al-mudda) and time (al-zamān) are established. In that case, before that first there is no prior, no posterior, no delay, and no precedence at all. However, clear reason judges decisively [ḥākim] to reject that. Because the intuition (badīha) of reason judges that the nonexistence of that first is prior and antecedent to it. And the intuition of reason also judges that it is possible to suppose events before that first [such that they end at that first in ten cycles]. And it is also possible to suppose other events that end at that first in twenty cycles. And clear reason judges that the beginning of this second supposed [set] must be prior to the beginning of the first supposed [set]. Thus it is established that your proofs necessitate the negation of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay before the occurrence of that beginning. And clear reason testifies that these matters were present. And the completion of the discourse on this point is mentioned in the chapter on time (bāb al-zamān), in the section where we show that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is necessary knowledge (ʿilm badīhī) that cannot be removed from the mind at all. And the upshot of the discourse is that your proofs necessitate the corruption of what has been known to be correct by intuition, so it is necessary to judge definitively that these proofs are corrupt. The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) said: If knowledge of the necessity of the occurrence of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay were necessary knowledge, then there would be no disagreement about it among rational people. And since there is disagreement about it, we know that it is not among the necessary truths. The philosophers said: The criterion for distinguishing necessary truths (al-badīhiyyāt) from theoretical truths (al-naẓariyyāt) is sound innate dispositions (al-fiṭar al-salīma) remaining upon original soundness. The disputatious people among the theologians have had the original disposition of their souls corrupted due to their persistence in arguments and disputes. As for one who has not practiced disputation [and reflection (al-naẓar)], his innate disposition testifies that every thing that occurs after not having been — its non-existence is prior to its existence, so the meaning of priority and precedence occurs before that event. The theologians answered him: That is mere fancy and imagination, and no consideration is given to them at all. So this is the end of the discourse in this chapter. And God knows best [what is correct].
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a multi-layered debate between the theologians (mutakallimūn) and the philosophers (falāsifa) regarding the beginning of time and the implications of temporal origination. It begins with the 'tenth argument' of the theologians: the reality of temporal origination (ḥudūth) necessitates being preceded by something else, which contradicts the nature of eternity (azal); thus, it is impossible for events to originate in the eternal. The opponent (likely a philosopher) objects by clarifying that temporal origination applies to each individual event, not to the totality of events. Each event is not eternal, but the totality might still be eternal? The theologians reject this as a repetition of the ninth argument already addressed. The philosophers then present their own position: they argue that the theologians' proofs for a first moment of time are obscure and can only be grasped through deep thought. Their desired conclusion is that the concepts of priority, posteriority, precedence, and delay only arise with the existence of time. If we accept the theologians' proofs, we would have to believe that time has a beginning, and before that beginning there would be no prior or posterior at all. But clear reason ('aql ṣarīḥ) decisively rejects this, because intuition (badīha) tells us that the non-existence of that first moment is prior to it. Moreover, we can suppose events before that first moment, e.g., in sets of ten or twenty cycles, and reason judges that the first of each set is prior to others. Thus the theologians' proofs would negate the very concepts of priority and posteriority that are evidently present. The philosophers conclude that the theologians' proofs corrupt what is known by intuition, so they must be false. The theologians respond by challenging the claim that these concepts are known intuitively: if they were truly necessary truths (badīhiyyāt), there would be no disagreement among rational people; since there is disagreement, they are not necessary. The philosophers counter with their criterion for necessary truths: sound innate dispositions (fiṭar) that remain in their original state. They argue that the theologians, through their dialectical practice, have corrupted their fiṭra. An ordinary person with an uncorrupted fiṭra recognizes that for any event that occurs after not existing, its non-existence is prior to its existence, meaning priority is present. The theologians dismiss this as mere fancy and imagination. The passage ends with a concise summary: 'This is the end of the discourse in this chapter.' The structure follows a back-and-forth: argument, objection, counter-argument, rebuttal, and final dismissal. The key tension is between theoretical proofs (the theologians' arguments) and immediate rational intuition (the philosophers' appeal to badīha and fiṭra).

بعدية
تقدم
تأخر
مدة
زمان
بديهة
فطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a proof seems to conflict with common sense, common sense must be wrong.
Correction: The philosophers argue that if a proof contradicts what is known by clear intuition (badīha), the proof must be flawed.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what is the criterion for distinguishing necessary truths from theoretical truths?
A) Consensus among all rational people
B) The authority of revelation
C) Sound innate dispositions (fiṭar salīma) remaining on original soundness
D) The complexity of the argument

Key Takeaway
When theoretical proofs contradict immediate rational intuition, the proof is suspect — but the very definition of 'intuition' becomes a point of contention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When an argument from a respected teacher or scholar contradicts what your heart feels is obviously true, do you trust your inner conviction or the authority? What would it take for you to side with your intuition against a learned proof?
?
The philosophers claim that constant debating corrupts one's fiṭra. As you engage in discussions about religion and theology, are you more concerned with winning the argument or with preserving the purity of your innate disposition to recognize truth?
?
If an ordinary, unschooled person's spontaneous judgment about time and priority supports the philosophers' view, would you have the humility to reconsider your own position? Or do you dismiss it as 'mere fantasy' as the theologians did?

The Debate on Temporal Priority: A Priori or Illusion?
Mutakallimun and philosophers debate a priori status of temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument against the a priori nature of temporal priority and the philosophers' counter-argument based on fitra, as well as the mutakallimun's response dismissing it as illusion.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is not self-evident; disagreement proves it is theoretical, not a priori.

This passage presents a sharp epistemological exchange between the mutakallimun and the philosophers. The issue is whether the concepts of priority and posteriority (qabliyya and ba'diyya) are known a priori by every rational mind, or whether they require reasoning. The mutakallimun begin by pointing out that disagreement among rational people shows these are not a priori. Read on to see how the philosophers respond and how the mutakallimun counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the mutakallimun's opening argument
Notice how they use disagreement as evidence that temporal priority is not a priori. Read the Arabic and translation.
They assume that a priori knowledge is universally agreed upon.
2
Examine the philosophers' objection
The philosophers argue that the criterion for a priori is uncorrupted fitra. They claim dialeticians have corrupted fitra, so disagreement reflects corruption, not lack of self-evidence.
Think about what 'fitra' means in this context.
3
Consider the mutakallimun's final response
The mutakallimun dismiss the appeal to natural intuition as mere wahm and khayāl. Ask yourself: why would they say this? Does this help their case?
This response ends the chapter, implying their view prevails.

قال المتكلمون : لو كان العلم بوجوب حصول القبلية والبعدية والتقدم والتأخر ، علماً بديهياً لما وقع الخلاف فيه بين العقلاء . ولما وقع الخلاف فيه : علمنا أن ذلك ليس من البديهيات . قالت الفلاسفة : المعتبر في تمييز البديهيات عن النظريات : الفطر السليمة الباقية على السلامة الأصلية. وأهل الجدل من المتكلمين قد فسدت فطرة نفوسهم الأصلية ، بسبب المواظبة على المجادلات والمنازعات . وأما من لم يمارس الجدل [ والنظر ] فإنه تشهد فطرته بأن كل أمر حدث بعد أن يكن ، فعدمه سابق على وجوده ، فيكون [ معنى ] القبلية والسبق حاصلا قبل ذلك الحادث . أجاب المتكلمون عنه : بأن هذا مجرد وهم وخيال ، ولا عبرة بهما البتة . فهذا منتهى الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم [ بالصواب ]
The mutakallimun said: If knowledge of the necessity of obtaining priority (qabliyya) and posteriority (ba'diyya) and anteriority (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur) were a priori knowledge (badīhī), disagreement among rational people would not have occurred. And since disagreement has occurred, we know that this is not from the a priori things. The philosophers said: The criterion for distinguishing a priori matters from theoretical matters (naẓariyyāt) is the sound natural dispositions (fiṭar) remaining on original soundness. And the dialecticians (ahl al-jadal) among the mutakallimun have corrupted the original natural disposition of their souls due to persistent engagement in disputations and contentions. As for one who has not practiced disputation [and theoretical reflection], his natural disposition bears witness that every occurrence that came to be after having not been, its non-existence precedes its existence, so [the meaning of] priority and precedence occurs before that event. The mutakallimun answered this: that this is mere illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl), and there is no regard for them at all. This is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best [the truth].
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents a complete objection-and-answer exchange between the mutakallimun and the philosophers regarding the epistemological status of the concepts of temporal priority (qabliyya, ba'diyya, taqaddum, ta'akhkhur). It begins with the mutakallimun arguing that if the necessity of these concepts were a priori (badīhī) and self-evident, there would be no disagreement among rational people about them. Since disagreement exists, they conclude these concepts are not a priori. The philosophers counter by distinguishing between sound and corrupted natural dispositions (fiṭar). They argue that the dialeticians among the mutakallimun have corrupted their innate soundness due to excessive argumentation. An ordinary person who has not engaged in dialectic will intuitively recognize that for any thing that comes into existence after not having existed, its non-existence is prior to its existence—thus the concept of priority is a priori. The mutakallimun respond by dismissing the philosophers' appeal to intuition as mere illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl) that carry no weight. The exchange ends with the mutakallimun asserting that this is the final word on the matter.

Correction: In this passage, the mutakallimun do not offer an alternative foundation; they simply deny the a priori status and dismiss the philosophers' rebuttal as illusion.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun deny that temporal priority is a priori because of disagreement, and they dismiss the philosophers' appeal to uncorrupted fitra as mere illusion.

The Two Premises and the Impossibility of Eternal Rest
Author states two premises and a conclusion about eternal rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two stated premises and how they lead to the conclusion that no body can be eternally at rest.

Core Idea
Eternal rest is impossible because any attribute that can cease cannot be eternal.

This lesson introduces the second and third premises of the argument, which together show that a body cannot be at rest in pre-eternity. The first premise is missing from the text, but the logical structure is clear. Understanding these foundations is essential before diving into the detailed proofs about rest.

The first premise is not given; assume it is stated earlier in the missing portion.
2
Understand the conclusion

3
Connect to the next issues
The author says the conclusion depends on three issues. The first issue is proving rest is an existing attribute, which begins immediately after. Notice the transition.
