The second: a statement that every rest (sukun) is subject to cessation. And the third: that everything subject to cessation is impossible to be eternal. And upon establishing these three demands, it is evident that it is impossible for a body to be at rest in pre-eternity. So this required conclusion depends on three issues. The first issue is proving that rest must be an existing attribute. The theologians (mutakallimun) agreed that the matter is so. And the philosophers (falasifa) agreed that the meaning of rest (sukun) is nothing but the absence of motion (adam al-haraka) from what is apt to move. The theologians argued that rest is an existing attribute with weak proofs. The first proof: they said: if rest were an expression for the absence of motion, then the Creator, exalted, is not described with motion. So it would necessitate that He is at rest. Also: motion is not described with rest, so it would be necessary that motion is at rest. And similarly for other accidents (a'rad). But one could say: we do not say that rest is an expression for absolute absence of motion; rather, rest is an expression for the absence of motion from a thing that is capable of motion. On this account, the mentioned speech falls [and God knows best]. The second proof: they said: making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than making motion an expression for the absence of rest. So it is necessary to say either that each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, or that each of them is an existing attribute. The first is invalid because if each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, and each of them is pure nonexistence, then each of them would be an expression for the absence of nonexistence, so each of them would be an existing attribute, despite our having assumed each of them is pure nonexistence, and that is contradictory. And if this is invalid, it is established that the truth is that each of them is an existing attribute. And one might say: the statement 'making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than the converse' may intend either negation of priority in reality or negation of it in our minds and intellects. The first is denied; why is it not permissible that one of them be the absence of the other and the other an existing attribute? The second is admitted, except that we must suspend judgment and not decide on either side except by separate evidence. When you know this, we say: if we say we know that the essence moved after being at rest, then this kind of change and alteration has occurred. So here, according to rational division, there are four divisions: one: that both states are non-existent, meaning the arriving and the departing. Second: that the arriving is non-existence and the departing is existence. Third: the opposite. Fourth: that each of them is an existing thing, contrary to the other. As for the first possibility, it is absolutely invalid because nonexistence has no reality, no individuation, and no subsistence in any respect. It is impossible for one non-existence to change into another. And some people say this possibility is not invalid. Evidence for it includes: first: that the occurrence of events in pre-eternity was impossible in itself. Then...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument covering three main parts. First, the author states two of three premises (the first presumably given earlier) leading to the conclusion that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The second premise: every rest is subject to cessation. The third: everything subject to cessation cannot be eternal. From these, it follows that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The author then notes that this conclusion depends on three issues, and begins the first: proving that rest is an existing attribute, not merely the absence of motion. He reports the consensus of the theologians that rest is existent, while the philosophers define rest as the absence of motion from something capable of motion. Then two weak theological proofs are presented. The first proof argues that if rest is mere absence, then God would be described as at rest (since He lacks motion), and motion would be at rest, which is absurd. The objection refines the definition: rest is absence only from something capable of motion, so the argument fails. The second proof uses a symmetry argument: neither 'rest is absence of motion' nor 'motion is absence of rest' is more appropriate, so either both are absences (leading to contradictions) or both are existent attributes. An objector distinguishes between priority in reality and priority in our minds; the theologian claims priority in reality is denied, so the symmetry argument fails unless supported by separate evidence. The author then introduces a rational division of four possibilities when a thing changes from rest to motion: both states non-existent, first non-existent and second existent, the reverse, or both existent and contrary. The first possibility is rejected because nonexistence lacks reality and cannot change into another nonexistence. Some argue against this rejection, giving an example: events occurring in pre-eternity are impossible in themselves (but the text cuts off). The author presents this intricate structure to explore the nature of rest, motion, and change.

جائز الزوال (ja'iz al-zawal)
أزلي (azali)
جسم (jism)

Misconception: Some might think that a body could be eternally at rest because it never changes.
Correction: The passage argues that rest is an attribute that can cease, and anything that can cease cannot be eternal, so eternal rest is impossible.

Key Takeaway

First Proof: If Rest Were Absence of Motion, God Would Be at Rest
Author presents first weak proof for rest as an existing attribute and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first weak proof that rest is an existing attribute and how it is refuted by a refined definition of rest.

Core Idea
Rest cannot be mere absence of motion because that would entail absurdities about God and motion.

The author begins the first issue by reporting the consensus of theologians that rest is an existing attribute, while philosophers define it as the absence of motion. He then presents the first of two weak theological proofs. This lesson focuses on that first proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the academic positions
Note the initial statement: theologians agree rest is existent; philosophers define rest as absence of motion from something capable of motion.
2
Analyze the first proof
The proof argues: if rest is absence of motion, then God (who has no motion) would be at rest, and motion itself would be at rest — both absurd.
3
Examine the objection
An objector refines the definition: rest is absence of motion only for things capable of motion. This invalidates the proof.
The author calls the proofs 'weak'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : بيان أن كل سكون ، فإنه جائز الزوال والثالث : أن كل ما كان جائز الزوال ، فإنه يمتنع كونه أزلياً وعند إثبات هذه المطالب الثلاثة يظهر : أنه أنه يمتنع كون الجسم ساكناً في الأزل · فترتب هذا المطلوب على ثلاث مسائل : المسألة الأولى في إثبات أن السكون يجب أن يكون صفة موجودة : اتفق المتكلمون على أن الأمر كذلك . واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن معنى السكون : لا معنى له إلا عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك واحتج المتكلمون على أن السكون صفة موجودة بدلائل ضعيفة : الحجة الأولى : قالوا : لو كان السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة ، فالبارىء تعالى غير موصوف بالحركة. فيلزم كونه ساكناً ، وأيضاً : الحركة غير موصوفة بالسكون ، فوجب كون الحركة ساكنة . وكذا القول في سائر الأعراض ولتماثل أن يقول : إنا لا نقول : السكون : عبارة عن عدم الحركة على الاطلاق، بل السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة ، عن الشيء الذي يكون قابلا للحركة . وعلى هذا التقرير ، فالكلام المذكور ساقط والله أعلم الحجة الثانية : قالوا : ليس جعل السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة ، أولى من جعل الحركة عبارة عن عدم السكون ، فوجب القول ، إما بكون كل واحد منهما عبارة عن عدم الآخر وإما يكون كل واحد منهما صفة وجودية . والأول باطل لأنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما عبارة عن عدم الآخر، وكان كل واحد منهما عدماً محضاً، كان كل واحد منهما عبارة عن عدم العدم، فيكون كل واحد منهما صفة موجودة. مع أنا فرضنا كون كل واحد منهما عدماً محضاً ، وذلك متناقض . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت أن الحق هو كون كل واحد منهما صفة موجودة . ولقائل أن يقول : إن قول القائل : ليس جعل السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة ، أولى من العكس : إما أن يكون المراد منه : نفي الأولوية في نفس الأمر ، أو نفيها في أفكارنا وعقولنا ، والأول ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهم عدماً للآخر، ويكون الآخر صفة موجودة . والثاني مسلّم ، إلا أنه يجب علينا أن نتوقف في الحكم ، وأن لا نجزم بأحد الطرفين ، إلا لدليل منفصل. وإذا عرفت هذا، فنقول إذا قلنا إنا عملنا أن الذات قد تحركت بعد أن كانت ساكنة ، فقد حصل هذا النوع من التبدل والتغير، فههنا يحصل بحسب التقسيم العقلي : أقسام أربعة : أحدهما : أن يكون كلتا الحالتين أمراً عدمياً . أعنى الطارى والزائل . والثاني : أن يكون الطارى، عدماً ، والزائل وجوداً . والثالث : أن يكون الأمر بالعكس منه . والرابع : أن يكون كل واحد منها أمراً موجوداً ، مضاداً للآخر. أما الاحتمال الأول فهو باطل على الاطلاق . لأن العدم لا حقيقة له ، ولا تشخص فيه ، ولا ثبوت لـه بوجه من الوجوه . امتنع تبدل أحد العدمين بالثاني . ومن الناس من قال : هذا الاحتمال غير باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : وهو أن حدوث الحوادث في الأزل ، كان ممتنعاً لعينه . ثم
The second: a statement that every rest (sukun) is subject to cessation. And the third: that everything subject to cessation is impossible to be eternal. And upon establishing these three demands, it is evident that it is impossible for a body to be at rest in pre-eternity. So this required conclusion depends on three issues. The first issue is proving that rest must be an existing attribute. The theologians (mutakallimun) agreed that the matter is so. And the philosophers (falasifa) agreed that the meaning of rest (sukun) is nothing but the absence of motion (adam al-haraka) from what is apt to move. The theologians argued that rest is an existing attribute with weak proofs. The first proof: they said: if rest were an expression for the absence of motion, then the Creator, exalted, is not described with motion. So it would necessitate that He is at rest. Also: motion is not described with rest, so it would be necessary that motion is at rest. And similarly for other accidents (a'rad). But one could say: we do not say that rest is an expression for absolute absence of motion; rather, rest is an expression for the absence of motion from a thing that is capable of motion. On this account, the mentioned speech falls [and God knows best]. The second proof: they said: making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than making motion an expression for the absence of rest. So it is necessary to say either that each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, or that each of them is an existing attribute. The first is invalid because if each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, and each of them is pure nonexistence, then each of them would be an expression for the absence of nonexistence, so each of them would be an existing attribute, despite our having assumed each of them is pure nonexistence, and that is contradictory. And if this is invalid, it is established that the truth is that each of them is an existing attribute. And one might say: the statement 'making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than the converse' may intend either negation of priority in reality or negation of it in our minds and intellects. The first is denied; why is it not permissible that one of them be the absence of the other and the other an existing attribute? The second is admitted, except that we must suspend judgment and not decide on either side except by separate evidence. When you know this, we say: if we say we know that the essence moved after being at rest, then this kind of change and alteration has occurred. So here, according to rational division, there are four divisions: one: that both states are non-existent, meaning the arriving and the departing. Second: that the arriving is non-existence and the departing is existence. Third: the opposite. Fourth: that each of them is an existing thing, contrary to the other. As for the first possibility, it is absolutely invalid because nonexistence has no reality, no individuation, and no subsistence in any respect. It is impossible for one non-existence to change into another. And some people say this possibility is not invalid. Evidence for it includes: first: that the occurrence of events in pre-eternity was impossible in itself. Then...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument covering three main parts. First, the author states two of three premises (the first presumably given earlier) leading to the conclusion that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The second premise: every rest is subject to cessation. The third: everything subject to cessation cannot be eternal. From these, it follows that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The author then notes that this conclusion depends on three issues, and begins the first: proving that rest is an existing attribute, not merely the absence of motion. He reports the consensus of the theologians that rest is existent, while the philosophers define rest as the absence of motion from something capable of motion. Then two weak theological proofs are presented. The first proof argues that if rest is mere absence, then God would be described as at rest (since He lacks motion), and motion would be at rest, which is absurd. The objection refines the definition: rest is absence only from something capable of motion, so the argument fails. The second proof uses a symmetry argument: neither 'rest is absence of motion' nor 'motion is absence of rest' is more appropriate, so either both are absences (leading to contradictions) or both are existent attributes. An objector distinguishes between priority in reality and priority in our minds; the theologian claims priority in reality is denied, so the symmetry argument fails unless supported by separate evidence. The author then introduces a rational division of four possibilities when a thing changes from rest to motion: both states non-existent, first non-existent and second existent, the reverse, or both existent and contrary. The first possibility is rejected because nonexistence lacks reality and cannot change into another nonexistence. Some argue against this rejection, giving an example: events occurring in pre-eternity are impossible in themselves (but the text cuts off). The author presents this intricate structure to explore the nature of rest, motion, and change.

Key Terms
سكون (sukun)
حركة (haraka)
عدم (adam)
صفة وجودية (sifa wujudiya)
بارىء (bari')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the first proof is valid and shows rest is an existing attribute.
Correction: The proof is refuted because the definition of rest is not absolute absence but absence from what is capable of motion.

Checkpoint
What consequence did the first proof draw if rest were merely the absence of motion?
A) Motion would cease to exist.
B) God would be at rest.
C) Rest would be motion.
D) Bodies would not be capable of motion.

Key Takeaway
The first theological proof that rest is an existing attribute fails because it uses an imprecise definition of rest as absolute absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that the theologians initially erred by not defining rest precisely. Are there aspects of your faith where you rely on vague or imprecise definitions? How can you commit to seeking accurate knowledge, even if it challenges your current understanding?
?
If God is not described by rest, what does that imply about your relationship with Him? Do you ever imagine Allah as passive or distant? How does this lesson push you to see Him as ever-active and aware of your every moment?

Second Proof: Symmetry Argument That Rest and Motion Are Both Existing Attributes
Author presents second weak proof and its refutation via distinction in priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second weak proof using symmetry and how the objection distinguishes ontological from epistemological priority.

Core Idea
Symmetry alone cannot prove rest is existent; separate evidence is needed.

The second theological proof attempts to force a choice between two options: either both rest and motion are absences (leading to contradiction) or both are existing attributes. The objector cleverly distinguishes between priority in reality and priority in our minds, showing that the proof only works if we accept the premise about reality, which the objector denies.

Guided Path
1
Read the symmetry argument
The proof claims: neither 'rest is absence of motion' nor 'motion is absence of rest' is more appropriate, so we must either say both are absences (contradictory) or both are existent.
2
Identify the contradiction
If both are absences of each other, each becomes an absence of non‑being, which is existence — contradicting the assumption.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector says the premise 'not more appropriate' can mean either in reality or in our minds. In reality, it is denied; one could be absence and the other existent. In our minds, we must suspend judgment without separate evidence.
Thus the proof is inconclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on a symmetry argument to decide a matter of deep personal belief—like whether an action is right or wrong based on its similarity to another—do you ever stop to ask whether the apparent symmetry is real or just in your mind? What would it take for you to seek independent evidence?
?
The objector in this lesson forces you to suspend judgment when you lack separate evidence. Is there an area of your life where you have been too quick to conclude based on what 'seems equal' or 'just as plausible'? Will you commit today to identifying one such area and seeking the separate evidence you have avoided?
?
If the distinction between epistemic and ontological priority were applied to your own assumptions about God's attributes, what claim about Him might you be holding without separate evidence? Are you ready to suspend judgment until you find that evidence, or will you continue to let a weak proof suffice?

Four Possibilities When Change Occurs: The Rational Division
Author presents four logical possibilities for change and rejects the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational division of four possibilities when an essence changes from rest to motion, and why the first possibility is rejected.

Core Idea
Change from rest to motion cannot involve both states being non‑existent, since non‑existence has no reality.

After discussing the nature of rest, the author introduces a rational division of possibilities for any change from rest to motion. This logical exercise helps clarify what must be true about the states of rest and motion. The first possibility — both states non‑existent — is rejected because non‑existence lacks reality and cannot change into another non‑existence. The passage then records an objection that this possibility might not be invalid, but the text cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four possibilities
Read the four options: 1) both states non‑existent; 2) new non‑existent, old existent; 3) opposite; 4) both existent and contrary.
2
Understand the rejection of the first
Non‑existence has no reality, so change from one non‑existence to another is impossible.
3
Note the counterargument
Some argue the first possibility is not invalid, giving an example about events in pre‑eternity. The text ends here.
The passage is incomplete; the full argument is not given.

الحركة ، أولى من العكس : إما أن يكون المراد منه : نفي الأولوية في نفس الأمر ، أو نفيها في أفكارنا وعقولنا ، والأول ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهم عدماً للآخر، ويكون الآخر صفة موجودة . والثاني مسلّم ، إلا أنه يجب علينا أن نتوقف في الحكم ، وأن لا نجزم بأحد الطرفين ، إلا لدليل منفصل. وإذا عرفت هذا، فنقول إذا قلنا إنا عملنا أن الذات قد تحركت بعد أن كانت ساكنة ، فقد حصل هذا النوع من التبدل والتغير، فههنا يحصل بحسب التقسيم العقلي : أقسام أربعة : أحدهما : أن يكون كلتا الحالتين أمراً عدمياً . أعنى الطارى والزائل . والثاني : أن يكون الطارى، عدماً ، والزائل وجوداً . والثالث : أن يكون الأمر بالعكس منه . والرابع : أن يكون كل واحد منها أمراً موجوداً ، مضاداً للآخر. أما الاحتمال الأول فهو باطل على الاطلاق . لأن العدم لا حقيقة له ، ولا تشخص فيه ، ولا ثبوت لـه بوجه من الوجوه . امتنع تبدل أحد العدمين بالثاني . ومن الناس من قال : هذا الاحتمال غير باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : وهو أن حدوث الحوادث في الأزل ، كان ممتنعاً لعينه . ثم
The second: a statement that every rest (sukun) is subject to cessation. And the third: that everything subject to cessation is impossible to be eternal. And upon establishing these three demands, it is evident that it is impossible for a body to be at rest in pre-eternity. So this required conclusion depends on three issues. The first issue is proving that rest must be an existing attribute. The theologians (mutakallimun) agreed that the matter is so. And the philosophers (falasifa) agreed that the meaning of rest (sukun) is nothing but the absence of motion (adam al-haraka) from what is apt to move. The theologians argued that rest is an existing attribute with weak proofs. The first proof: they said: if rest were an expression for the absence of motion, then the Creator, exalted, is not described with motion. So it would necessitate that He is at rest. Also: motion is not described with rest, so it would be necessary that motion is at rest. And similarly for other accidents (a'rad). But one could say: we do not say that rest is an expression for absolute absence of motion; rather, rest is an expression for the absence of motion from a thing that is capable of motion. On this account, the mentioned speech falls [and God knows best]. The second proof: they said: making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than making motion an expression for the absence of rest. So it is necessary to say either that each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, or that each of them is an existing attribute. The first is invalid because if each of them is an expression for the absence of the other, and each of them is pure nonexistence, then each of them would be an expression for the absence of nonexistence, so each of them would be an existing attribute, despite our having assumed each of them is pure nonexistence, and that is contradictory. And if this is invalid, it is established that the truth is that each of them is an existing attribute. And one might say: the statement 'making rest an expression for the absence of motion is not more appropriate than the converse' may intend either negation of priority in reality or negation of it in our minds and intellects. The first is denied; why is it not permissible that one of them be the absence of the other and the other an existing attribute? The second is admitted, except that we must suspend judgment and not decide on either side except by separate evidence. When you know this, we say: if we say we know that the essence moved after being at rest, then this kind of change and alteration has occurred. So here, according to rational division, there are four divisions: one: that both states are non-existent, meaning the arriving and the departing. Second: that the arriving is non-existence and the departing is existence. Third: the opposite. Fourth: that each of them is an existing thing, contrary to the other. As for the first possibility, it is absolutely invalid because nonexistence has no reality, no individuation, and no subsistence in any respect. It is impossible for one non-existence to change into another. And some people say this possibility is not invalid. Evidence for it includes: first: that the occurrence of events in pre-eternity was impossible in itself. Then...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument covering three main parts. First, the author states two of three premises (the first presumably given earlier) leading to the conclusion that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The second premise: every rest is subject to cessation. The third: everything subject to cessation cannot be eternal. From these, it follows that a body cannot be eternally at rest. The author then notes that this conclusion depends on three issues, and begins the first: proving that rest is an existing attribute, not merely the absence of motion. He reports the consensus of the theologians that rest is existent, while the philosophers define rest as the absence of motion from something capable of motion. Then two weak theological proofs are presented. The first proof argues that if rest is mere absence, then God would be described as at rest (since He lacks motion), and motion would be at rest, which is absurd. The objection refines the definition: rest is absence only from something capable of motion, so the argument fails. The second proof uses a symmetry argument: neither 'rest is absence of motion' nor 'motion is absence of rest' is more appropriate, so either both are absences (leading to contradictions) or both are existent attributes. An objector distinguishes between priority in reality and priority in our minds; the theologian claims priority in reality is denied, so the symmetry argument fails unless supported by separate evidence. The author then introduces a rational division of four possibilities when a thing changes from rest to motion: both states non-existent, first non-existent and second existent, the reverse, or both existent and contrary. The first possibility is rejected because nonexistence lacks reality and cannot change into another nonexistence. Some argue against this rejection, giving an example: events occurring in pre-eternity are impossible in themselves (but the text cuts off). The author presents this intricate structure to explore the nature of rest, motion, and change.

عدم (adam)
حالة (hala)
طارى (tari)
زائل (za'il)
ضد (didd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that change from one non‑existent state to another is possible.
Correction: The passage argues non‑existence has no reality, so change between non‑existences is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first possibility (both states non‑existent)?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
B) Because non‑existence has no reality and cannot change.
C) Because the philosophers proved it wrong.
D) Because rest cannot be non‑existent.

Key Takeaway
Change from rest to motion cannot involve both states being non‑existent because non‑existence lacks reality and cannot change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a significant change you have undergone—perhaps a change in your character or belief. According to this lesson, could that change have occurred if both your previous state and your new state were mere non‑existences? What does that imply about the reality of your transformation? Will you now view personal change as more real and consequential?
?
The author asserts that non‑existence has no reality and no individuation. In your daily life, you may fear future 'non‑existence'—loss of health, wealth, or relationships. How does this lesson challenge the way you treat those fears? Are you giving reality to something that lacks it? Commit to one concrete way you will shift your focus from what you might lose to what you actually have.
?
If change requires a real subject with real states, then your actions and choices are not trivial—they are founded on real existence and have real consequences. How does this awareness affect your intention (niyya) for your next action? What is one thing you will do today with full presence, knowing that your state is real before Allah?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Motion and Rest as Existing Attributes and the Objection about Priority
Author argues each state is an existing attribute and answers an objection about priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion and rest are existing attributes, not mere non-existence, and how an objection about priority is resolved by distinguishing real priority from epistemic priority.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are real attributes; priority must be proven externally.

This passage concludes an argument that both motion and rest are real, existing attributes. The author anticipates an objection: if neither is inherently the negation of the other, how do we decide? He breaks the objection into two possible interpretations—one about reality, one about our knowledge—and shows that only the second is valid, requiring a separate proof. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how kalam handles relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Let the author's argument flow before analyzing.
The Arabic uses concise logical phrasing; pay attention to the conditional 'If...then' structure.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
Notice that the author first restates that each state is an existing attribute. Then an objection is raised: 'Is making 'rest' an expression for non-existence of motion more appropriate than the opposite?' This is the core objection.
The objection is about determining which state is the negation of the other.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
The objector suggests two interpretations of his statement: either priority is denied in reality itself, or in our thoughts. Work through each interpretation with the author's response.
The first interpretation is rejected; the second is accepted but requires caution.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
The author accepts that in our thoughts we lack priority, so we must suspend judgment until a separate proof (دليل منفصل) is provided. This does not undermine his claim that each is an existing attribute.
The key takeaway: we need external evidence to decide which is the negation, but that does not make either non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون كل واحد منهما صفة موجودة. مع أنا فرضنا كون كل واحد منهما عدماً محضاً، وذلك متناقض. وإذا بطل هذا، ثبت أن الحق هو كون كل واحد منهما صفة موجودة. ولقائل أن يقول: إن قول القائل: ليس جعل السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة، أولى من العكس: إما أن يكون المراد منه: نفي الأولوية في نفس الأمر، أو نفيها في أفكارنا وعقولنا، والأول ممنوع. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهم عدماً للآخر، ويكون الآخر صفة موجودة. والثاني مسلم، إلا أنه يجب علينا أن نتوقف في الحكم، وأن لا نجزم بأحد الطرفين، إلا لدليل منفصل.
So each one of them is an existing attribute. Even though we assumed each one of them to be pure non-existence, that is contradictory. And when this is invalid, it is established that the truth is that each one of them is an existing attribute. And someone may say: The statement of the speaker 'Making 'rest' an expression for the non-existence of motion is not more preponderant than the opposite'—either what is meant is the negation of priority in the matter itself, or its negation in our thoughts and intellects. The first is prevented. Why is it not permissible that one of them is a non-existence for the other, and the other is an existing attribute? The second is conceded, except that we must suspend judgment and not be certain of either side without a separate proof.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is part of an argument about the nature of motion and rest. The author has been establishing that each state (motion and rest) is an existing attribute, not pure non-existence. He now restates that if we assumed both were pure non-existence, that would be contradictory. Hence the truth is that each is an existing attribute. Then an objector raises a point: when someone says that 'rest' is not more appropriately defined as the absence of motion than the opposite (i.e., motion as absence of rest), there are two possible interpretations. The first interpretation is that there is no priority in reality itself, meaning that it is not the case that one is inherently the negation of the other. The author rejects this interpretation because it would allow one to be non-existence and the other an attribute, which he has already argued against. The second interpretation is that there is no priority in our thoughts—we need an external proof to decide which is the negation. The author concedes this, but insists that we must suspend judgment until a separate proof is provided. Thus the objection does not refute his position; it only shows that we need further evidence to determine which state is the negation of the other.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection suggests that both motion and rest could be non-existence.
Correction: The objection only questions which state is the negation of the other, not that both are negations. The author maintains both are real attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is required before deciding which state is the negation of the other?
A) Intuition
B) A separate proof (دليل منفصل)
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are each real attributes; if we cannot decide which is the negation by reason alone, we must wait for external proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily choices between action and rest, do you treat both as real states that matter to Allah, or do you assume one is merely the absence of the other and thus neglect its significance? How will you seek external guidance (like the Quran and Sunnah) to prioritize them correctly?
?
When you face two options where neither seems inherently better, do you suspend judgment and actively seek proof from Allah, or do you follow your own inclination without realizing you might be denying a real attribute? Commit to one area today where you will wait for clear evidence before deciding.
?
The lesson shows that without external proof, you cannot be certain which state is the negation of the other. Is there a belief or action in your life where you have assumed priority (e.g., 'this is more important') without divine proof? How can you correct that by seeking a separate proof from revelation?
